Example 35.10. As in Example 35.3, let $\nu$ be a finite measure on the unit interval with Lebesgue measure $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$. For $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ the $\sigma$-field generated by the dyadic intervals of rank $n$, (35.10) gives $X_{n}$. In this case $\mathscr{F}_{n} \uparrow \mathscr{F}_{\infty}=\mathscr{F}$. For each $\omega$ and $n$ choose the dyadic rationals $a_{n}(\omega)=k 2^{-n}$ and $b_{n}(\omega)= (k+1) 2^{-n}$ for which $a_{n}(\omega)<\omega \leq b_{n}(\omega)$. By Theorem 35.7, if $F$ is the distribution function for $\nu$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{F\left(b_{n}(\omega)\right)-F\left(a_{n}(\omega)\right)}{b_{n}(\omega)-a_{n}(\omega)} \rightarrow X(\omega) \tag{35.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 .
According to Theorem 31.2, $F$ has a derivative $F^{\prime}$ except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 , and since the intervals $\left(a_{n}(\omega), b_{n}(\omega)\right]$ contract to $\omega$, the difference ratio (35.27) converges almost everywhere to $F^{\prime}(\omega)$ (see(31.8)). This identifies $X$. Since (35.27) involves intervals $\left(a_{n}(\omega), b_{n}(\omega)\right]$ of a special kind, it does not quite imply Theorem 31.2.

By Theorem 35.7, $X=F^{\prime}$ is the density for $\nu$ in the absolutely continuous case, and $X=F^{\prime}=0$ (except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 ) in the singular case, facts proved in a different way in Section 31. The singular case gives another example where $E\left[X_{n}\right] \rightarrow E[X]$ fails in Theorem 35.5. $\square$

## Likelihood Ratios

Return to Example 35.4: $\nu=Q$ is a probability measure, $\mathscr{F}_{n}=\sigma\left(Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}\right)$ for random variables $Y_{n}$, and the Radon-Nikodym derivative or likelihood ratio $X_{n}$ has the form (35.11) for densities $p_{n}$ and $q_{n}$ on $R^{n}$. By Theorem 35.7 the $X_{n}$ converge to some $X$ which is integrable and measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}=\sigma\left(Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots\right)$.

If the $Y_{n}$ are independent under $P$ and under $Q$, and if the densities are different, then $P$ and $Q$ are mutually singular on $\sigma\left(Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots\right)$, as shown in Example 35.4. In this case $X=0$ and the likelihood ratio converges to 0 on a set of $P$-measure 1. The statistical relevance of this is that the smaller $X_{n}$ is, the more strongly one prefers $P$ over $Q$ as an explanation of the observation ( $Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}$ ), and $X_{n}$ goes to 0 with probability 1 if $P$ is in fact the measure governing the $Y_{n}$.

It might be thought that a disingenuous experimenter could bias his results by stopping at an $X_{n}$ he likes-a large value if his prejudices favor $Q$, a small value if they favor $P$. This is not so, as the following analysis shows. For this argument $P$ must dominate $Q$ on each $\mathscr{F}_{n}=\sigma\left(Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}\right)$, but the likelihood ratio $X_{n}$ need not have any special form.

Let $\tau$ be a positive-integer-valued random variable representing the time the experimenter stops. Assume that $\tau$ is finite, and to exclude prevision, assume that it is a stopping time. The $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ defined by (35.20) represents the information available at time $\tau$, and the problem is to show that $X_{\tau}$
is the likelihood ratio (Radon-Nikodym derivative) for $Q$ with respect to $P$ on $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$. First, $X_{\tau}$ is clearly measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$. Second, if $A \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau}$, then $A \cap[\tau= n] \in \mathscr{F}_{n}$, and therefore

$$
\int_{A} X_{\tau} d P=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \int_{A \cap\lfloor\tau=n\rfloor} X_{n} d P=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} Q(A \cap[\tau=n])=Q(A)
$$

as required.

## Reversed Martingales

A left-infinite sequence $\ldots, X_{-2}, X_{-1}$ is a martingale relative to $\sigma$ fields $\ldots, \mathscr{F}_{-2}, \mathscr{F}_{-1}$ if conditions (ii) and (iii) in the definition of martingale are satisfied for $n \leq-1$ and conditions (i) and (iv) are satisfied for $n<-1$. Such a sequence is a reversed or backward martingale.

Theorem 35.8. For a reversed martingale, $\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} X_{-n}=X$ exists and is integrable, and $E[X]=E\left[X_{-n}\right]$ for all $n$.

Proof. The proof is alinost the same as that for Theorem 35.5. Let $X^{*}$ and $X_{*}$ be the limits superior and inferior of the sequence $X_{-1}, X_{-2}, \ldots$. Again (35.25) holds. Let $U_{n}$ be the number of upcrossings of $[\alpha, \beta]$ by $X_{-n}, \ldots, X_{-1}$. By the upcrossing theorem, $E\left[U_{n}\right] \leq\left(E\left[\left|X_{-1}\right|\right]+|\alpha|\right) /(\beta-\alpha)$. Again $E\left[U_{n}\right]$ is bounded, and so $\sup _{n} U_{n}$ is finite with probability 1 and the sets in (35.25) have probability 0 .

Therefore, $\lim _{n \rightarrow \infty} X_{-n}=X$ exists with probability 1 . By the property (35.4) for martingales, $X_{-n}=E\left[X_{-1} \| \mathscr{F}_{-n}\right]$ for $n=1,2, \ldots$. The lemma above (p. 469) implies that the $X_{-n}$ are uniformly integrable. Therefore, $X$ is integrable and $E[X]$ is the limit of the $E\left[X_{-n}\right]$; these all have the same value by (35.5). $\square$

If $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ are $\sigma$-fields satisfying $\mathscr{F}_{1} \supset \mathscr{F}_{2} \ldots$, then the intersection $\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} \mathscr{F}_{n}= \mathscr{F}_{0}$ is also a $\sigma$-field, and the relation is expressed by $\mathscr{F}_{n} \downarrow \mathscr{F}_{0}$.

Theorem 35.9. If $\mathscr{F}_{n} \downarrow \mathscr{F}_{0}$ and $Z$ is integrable, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right] \rightarrow E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{0}\right] \tag{35.28}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1.
Proof. If $X_{-n}=E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right]$, then $\ldots, X_{-2}, X_{-1}$ is a martingale relative to $\ldots, \mathscr{F}_{2}, \mathscr{F}_{1}$. By the preceding theorem, $E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right]$ converges as $n \rightarrow \infty$ to an integrable $X$ and by the lemma, the $E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right]$ are uniformly integrable. As the limit of the $E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right]$ for $n \geq k, X$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{k} ; k$ being arbitrary, $X$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{0}$.

By uniform integrability, $A \in \mathscr{F}_{0}$ implies that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{A} X d P & =\lim _{n} \int_{A} E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right] d P=\lim _{n} \int_{A} E\left[E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right] \| \mathscr{F}_{0}\right] d P \\
& =\lim _{n} \int_{A} E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{0}\right] d P=\int_{A} E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{0}\right] d P
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus $X$ is a version of $E\left[Z \| \mathscr{F}_{0}\right]$.
Theorems 35.6 and 35.9 are parallel. There is an essential difference between Theorems 35.5 and 35.8, however. In the latter, the martingale has a last random variable, namely $X_{-1}$, and so it is unnecessary in preving convergence to assume the $\left.E\left[\mid X_{n}\right]\right]$ bounded. On the other hand, the proof in Theorem 35.8 that $X$ is integrable would not work for a submartingale.

## Applications: de Finetti's Theorem

Let $\theta, X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be random variables such that $0 \leq \theta \leq 1$ and, conditionally on $\theta$, the $X_{\eta}$ are independent and assume the values 1 and 0 with probabilities $\theta$ and $1-\theta$ : for $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}$ a sequence of 0 's and 1 's,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \| \theta\right]=\theta^{s}(1-\theta)^{n-s} \tag{35.29}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $s=u_{1}+\cdots+u_{n}$.
To see that such sequences exist, let $\theta, Z_{1}, Z_{2}, \ldots$ be independent random variables, where $\theta$ has an arbitrarily prescribed distribution supported by $[0,1]$ and the $Z_{n}$ are uniformly distributed over [0,1]. Put $X_{n}=I_{\left[Z_{n} \leq \theta\right]}$. If, for instance, $f(x)=x(1-x) =P\left[Z_{1} \leq x, Z_{2}>x\right]$, then $P\left[X_{1}=1, X_{2}=0 \| \theta\right]_{\omega}^{n}=f(\theta(\omega))$ by (33.13). The obvious extension establishes (35.29).

Integrate (35.29):

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n}\right]=E\left[\theta^{s}(1-\theta)^{n-s}\right] \tag{35.30}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a mixture of Bernoulli processes. It is clear from (35.30) that the $X_{k}$ are exchangeable in the sense that for each $n$ the distribution of ( $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$ ) is invariant under permutations. According to the following theorem of de Finetti, every exchangeable sequence is a mixture of Bernoulli sequences.

Theorem 35.10. If the random variables $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are exchangeable and take values 0 and 1 , then there is a random variable $\theta$ for which (35.29) and (35.30) hold.

Proof. Let $S_{m}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{m}$. If $t \leq m$, then

$$
P\left[S_{m}=t\right]=\sum_{u_{1}} P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{m}=u_{m}\right]
$$

where the sum extends over the sequences for which $u_{1}+\cdots+u_{m}=t$. By exchangeability, the terms on the right are all equal, and since there are $\binom{m}{t}$ of them,

$$
P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{m}=u_{m} \mid S_{m}=t\right]=\binom{m}{t}^{-1}
$$

Suppose that $s \leq n \leq m$ and $u_{1}+\cdots+u_{n}=s \leq t \leq m$; add out the $u_{n+1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ that sum to $t-s$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[X_{1}\right. & \left.=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \mid S_{m}=t\right]=\binom{m-n}{t-s} /\binom{m}{t} \\
& =\frac{(t)_{s}(m-t)_{n-s}}{(m)_{n}}=f_{n, s, m}\left(\frac{t}{m}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

where

$$
f_{n, s, m}(x)=\prod_{i=0}^{s-1}\left(x-\frac{i}{m}\right)^{n-s-1} \prod_{i=0}^{n-1}\left(1-x-\frac{i}{m}\right) / \prod_{i=0}^{n-1}\left(1-\frac{i}{m}\right) .
$$

The preceding equations still hold if further constraints $S_{m+1}=t_{1}, \ldots, S_{m+j} =t_{j}$ are joined to $S_{m}=t$. Therefore, $P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \| S_{m}, \ldots, S_{m+j}\right]= f_{n, s, m}\left(S_{m / m}\right)$.

Let $\mathscr{L}_{m}=\sigma\left(S_{m}, S_{m+1}, \ldots\right)$ and $\mathscr{L}=\bigcap_{m} \mathscr{L}_{m}$. Now fix $n$ and $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}$, and suppose that $u_{1}+\cdots+u_{n}=s$. Let $j \rightarrow \infty$ and apply Theorem 35.6: $P\left[X_{1}=\right. \left.u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \| \mathscr{L}_{m}\right]=f_{n, s, m}\left(S_{m} / m\right)$. Let $m \rightarrow \infty$ and apply Theorem 35.9:

$$
P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \| \mathscr{L}\right]_{\omega}=\lim _{m} f_{n, s, m}\left(\frac{S_{m}(\omega)}{m}\right)
$$

holds for $\omega$ outside a set of probability 0 .
Fix such an $\omega$ and suppose that $\left\{S_{m}(\omega) / m\right\}$ has two distinct limit points. Since the distance from each $S_{m}(\omega) / m$ to the next is less than $2 / m$, it follows that the set of limit points must fill a nondegenerate interval. But $\lim _{k} x_{m_{k}}=x$ implies $\lim _{k} f_{n, s, m_{k}}\left(x_{m_{k}}\right)=x^{s}(1-x)^{n-s}$, and so it follows further that $x^{s}(1-x)^{n-s}$ must be constant over this interval, which is impossible. Therefore, $S_{m}(\omega) / m$ must converge to some limit $\theta(\omega)$. This shows that $P\left[X_{1}=u_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}=u_{n} \| \mathscr{L}\right]=\theta^{s}(1-\theta)^{n-s}$ with probability 1 . Take a conditional expectation with respect to $\sigma(\theta)$, and (35.29) follows.

## Bayes Estimation

From the Bayes point of view in statistics, the $\theta$ in (35.29) is a parameter governed by some a priori distribution known to the statistician. For given $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$, the Bayes estimate of $\theta$ is $E\left[\theta \| X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right]$. The problem is to show that this estimate is consistent in the sense that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[\theta \| X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right] \rightarrow \theta \tag{35.31}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 . By Theorem $35.6, E\left[\theta \| X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right] \rightarrow E\left[\theta \| \mathscr{F}_{\infty}\right]$, where $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}=\sigma\left(X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots\right)$, and so what must be shown is that $E\left[\theta \| \mathscr{F}_{\infty}\right]=\theta$ with probability 1 .

By an elementary argument that parallels the unconditional case, it follows from (35.29) for $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$ that $E\left[S_{n} \| \theta\right]=n \theta$ and $E\left[\left(S_{n}-n \theta\right)^{2} \| \theta\right] =n \theta(1-\theta)$. Hence $E\left[\left(n^{-1} S_{n}-\theta\right)^{2}\right] \leq n^{-1}$, and by Chebyshev's inequality $n^{-1} S_{n}$ converges in probability to $\theta$. Therefore (Theorem 20.5), $\lim _{k} n_{k}^{-1} S_{n_{k}} =\theta$ with probability 1 for some subsequence. Thus $\theta=\theta^{\prime}$ with probability 1 for a $\theta^{\prime}$ measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$, and $E\left[\theta \| \mathscr{F}_{\infty}\right]=E\left[\theta^{\prime} \| \mathscr{F}_{\infty}\right]=\theta^{\prime}=\theta$ with probability 1 .

## A Central Limit Theorem*

Suppose $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is a martingale relative to $\mathscr{F}_{1}, \mathscr{F}_{2}, \ldots$, and put $Y_{n}=X_{n} -X_{n-1}\left(Y_{1}=X_{1}\right)$, so that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[Y_{n} \| \mathscr{F}_{n-1}\right]=0 . \tag{35.32}
\end{equation*}
$$

View $Y_{n}$ as the gambler's gain on the $n$th trial in a fair game. For example, if $\Delta_{1}, \Delta_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and have mean $0, \mathscr{F}_{n}^{-}=\sigma\left(\Delta_{1}, \ldots, \Delta_{n}\right), W_{n}$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{n-1}$, and $Y_{n}=W_{n} \Delta_{n}$, then (35.32) holds (see (35.17)). A specialization of this case shows that $X_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{n} Y_{k}$ need not be approximately normally distributed for large $n$.

Example 35.11. Suppose that $\Delta_{n}$ takes the values $\pm 1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each and $W_{1}=0$, and suppose that $W_{n}=1$ for $n \geq 2$ if $\Delta_{1}=1$, while $W_{n}=2$ for $n \geq 2$ if $\Delta_{1}=-1$. If $S_{n}=\Delta_{2}+\cdots+\Delta_{n}$, then $X_{n}$ is $S_{n}$ or $2 S_{n}$ according as $\Delta_{1}$ is +1 or -1 . Since $S_{n} / \sqrt{n}$ has approximately the standard normal distribution, the approximate distribution of $X_{n} / \sqrt{n}$ is a mixture, with equal weights, of the centered normal distributions with standard deviations 1 and 2. $\square$

To understand this phenomenon, consider conditional variances. Suppose for simplicity that the $Y_{n}$ are bounded, and define

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sigma_{n}^{2}=E\left[Y_{n}^{2} \| \mathscr{F}_{n-1}\right] \tag{35.33}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^0](take $\mathscr{F}_{0}=\{\varnothing, \Omega\}$ ). Consider the stopping times
$$
\begin{equation*}
\nu_{l}=\min \left[n: \sum_{k=1}^{n} \sigma_{k}^{2} \geq t\right] . \tag{35.34}
\end{equation*}
$$

Under appropriate conditions, $X_{\nu_{t}} / \sqrt{t}$ will be approximately normally distributed for large $t$. Consider the preceding example. Roughly: If $\Delta_{1}=+1$, then $\sum_{k=1}^{n} \sigma_{k}^{2}=n-1$, and so $\nu_{1} \approx t$ and $X_{\nu_{1}} / \sqrt{t} \approx S_{t} / \sqrt{t}$; if $\Delta_{1}=-1$, then $\sum_{k=1}^{n} \sigma_{k}^{2}=4(n-1)$, and so $\nu_{i} \approx t / 4$ and $X_{\nu_{t}} / \sqrt{t} \approx 2 S_{t / 4} / \sqrt{t}=S_{t / 4} / \sqrt{t / 4}$. In either case, $X_{\nu_{1}} / \sqrt{i}$ approximately follows the standard normal law.

If the $n$th play takes $\sigma_{n}^{2}$ units of time, then $\nu_{1}$ is essentially the number of plays that take place during the first $t$ units of time. This change of the time scale stabilizes the rate at which money changes hands.

Theorem 35.11. Suppose the $Y_{n}=X_{n}-X_{n-1}$ are uniformly bounded and satisfy (35.32), and assume that $\Sigma_{n} \sigma_{n}^{2}=\infty$ with probability 1 . Then $X_{v_{1}} / \sqrt{i} \Rightarrow N$.

This will be deduced from a more general result, one that contains the Lindeberg theorem. Suppose that, for each $n, X_{n 1}, X_{n 2}, \ldots$ is a martingale with respect to $\mathscr{F}_{n 1}, \mathscr{F}_{n 2}, \ldots$. Define $Y_{n k}=X_{n k}-X_{n, k-1}$, suppose the $Y_{i n k}$ have second moments, and put $\sigma_{n k}^{2}=E\left[Y_{n k}^{2} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]\left(\mathscr{F}_{n 0}=\{\varnothing, \Omega\}\right)$. The probability space may vary with $n$. If the martingale is originally defined only for $1 \leq k \leq r_{n}$, take $Y_{n k}=0$ and $\mathscr{F}_{n k}=\mathscr{F}_{n r_{n}}$ for $k>r_{n}$. Assume that $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} Y_{n k}$ and $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n k}^{2}$ converge with probability 1 .

Theorem 35.12. Suppose that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n k}^{2} \rightarrow_{p} \sigma^{2} \tag{35.35}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $\sigma$ is a positive constant, and that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} E\left[Y_{n k}^{2} I_{\left[\left|Y_{n k}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]}\right] \rightarrow 0 \tag{36.36}
\end{equation*}
$$

for each $\epsilon$. Then $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} Y_{n k} \Rightarrow \sigma N$.
Proof of Theorem 35.11. The proof will be given for $t$ going to infinity through the integers. ${ }^{\dagger}$ Let $Y_{n k}=I_{\left[\nu_{n} \geq k\right]} Y_{k} / \sqrt{n}$ and $\mathscr{F}_{n k}=\mathscr{F}_{k}$. From $\left[\nu_{n} \geq k\right] =\left[\sum_{j=1}^{k-1} \sigma_{j}^{2}<n\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{k-2}$ follow $E\left[Y_{n k} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]=0$ and $\sigma_{n k}^{2}=E\left[Y_{n k}^{2} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right] =I_{\left[\nu_{n} \geq k\right]} \sigma_{k}^{2} / n$. If $K$ bounds the $\left|Y_{k}\right|$, then $1 \leq \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n k}^{2}=n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{\nu_{n}} \sigma_{k}^{2} \leq 1+ K^{2} / n$, so that (35.35) holds for $\sigma=1$. For $n$ large enough that $K / \sqrt{n}<\epsilon$, the sum in (35.36) vanishes. Theorem 35.12 therefore applies, and $\sum_{k=1}^{\nu_{n}} Y_{k} / \sqrt{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} Y_{n k} \Rightarrow N$.

[^1]Proof of Theorem 35.12. Assume at first that there is a constant $c$ such that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n k}^{2} \leq c, \tag{35.37}
\end{equation*}
$$

which in fact suffices for the application to Theorem 35.11.
Write $S_{k}=\sum_{j=1}^{k} Y_{n j}\left(S_{0}=0\right), S_{\infty}=\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} Y_{n j}, \Sigma_{k}=\sum_{j=1}^{k} \sigma_{n j}^{2}\left(\Sigma_{0}=0\right)$, and $\Sigma_{\infty}=\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n j}^{2}$; the dependence on $n$ is suppressed in the notation. To prove $E\left[e^{i t S_{\mathrm{x}}}\right] \rightarrow e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma^{2}}$, observe first that

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \left|E\left[e^{i t S_{x}}-e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma^{2}}\right]\right| \\
& \quad=\left|E\left[e^{i t S_{x}}\left(1-e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{x}} e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma^{2}}\right)+e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma^{2}}\left(e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{x}} e^{i t S_{x}}-1\right)\right]\right| \\
& \left.\quad \leq E\left[1-e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{x}} e^{-\frac{1}{1} t^{2} \sigma^{2}}\right]\right]+\left|E\left[e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{x}} e^{i t S_{x}}-1\right]\right|=A+B
\end{aligned}
$$

The term $A$ on the right goes to 0 as $n \rightarrow \infty$, because by (35.35) and (35.37) the integrand is bounded and goes to 0 in probability.

The integrand in $B$ is

$$
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} e^{i t S_{k-1}}\left(e^{i t Y_{, k}}-e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}}\right) e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{k}},
$$

because the $m$ th partial sum here telescopes to $e^{i l S_{m}} e^{\frac{1}{3} t^{2} \Sigma_{m}}-1$. Since, by (35.37), this partial sum is bounded uniformly in $m$, and since $S_{k-1}$ and $\Sigma_{k}$ are measurable $\mathscr{F}_{r, k-1}$, it follows (Theorem 16.7) that

$$
\begin{aligned}
B & =\left|\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} E\left[e^{i t S_{k-1}} e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{k}}\left(e^{i t Y_{n k}}-e^{-\frac{1}{3} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}}\right)\right]\right| \\
& \leq \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \left\lvert\, E\left[\left.e^{i t S_{k-1}} e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \Sigma_{k}} E\left[e^{i t Y_{n k}}-e^{-\frac{1}{3} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right] \right\rvert\,\right.\right. \\
& \leq e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} c} \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} E\left[\left|E\left[e^{i t Y_{n k}}-e^{-\frac{1}{3} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]\right|\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

To complete the proof (under the temporary assumption (35.37)), it is enough to show that this last sum goes to 0 .

By (26.42),

$$
\begin{equation*}
e^{i l Y_{n k}}=1+i t Y_{n k}-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} Y_{n k}^{2}+\theta, \tag{35.38}
\end{equation*}
$$

where (write $I_{n k}=I_{\left[\left|Y_{n k}\right| \geq \epsilon\right]}$ and let $K_{t}$ bound $t^{2}$ and $|t|^{3}$ )

$$
|\theta| \leq \min \left\{\left|t Y_{n k}\right|^{3},\left|t Y_{n k}\right|^{2}\right\} \leq K_{t}\left(Y_{n k}^{2} I_{n k}+\epsilon Y_{n k}^{2}\right) .
$$

And

$$
\begin{equation*}
e^{-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}}=1-\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}+\theta^{\prime}, \tag{38.39}
\end{equation*}
$$

where (use (27.15) and increase $K_{i}$ )

$$
\left|\theta^{\prime}\right| \leq\left(\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}\right)^{2} e^{\frac{1}{2} t^{2} \sigma_{n k}^{2}} \leq t^{4} \sigma_{n k}^{4} e^{\frac{1}{t} t^{2} c} \leq K_{t} \sigma_{n k}^{4} .
$$

Because of the condition $E\left[Y_{n k} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]=0$ and the definition of $\sigma_{n k}^{2}$, the right sides of (35.38) and (35.39), minus $\theta$ and $\theta^{\prime}$, respectively, have the same conditional expected value given $\mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}^{-}$. By (35.37), therefore,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} & E\left[\left|E\left[e^{i t Y_{n k}}-e^{-\frac{1}{t^{2}} \sigma_{n k}^{2} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}}\right]\right|\right] \\
& \leq K_{t} \sum_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(E\left[Y_{n k}^{2} I_{n k}\right]+\epsilon E\left[\sigma_{n k}^{2}\right]+E\left[\sigma_{n k}^{4}\right]\right) \\
& \leq K_{r}\left(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} E\left[Y_{n k}^{2} I_{n k}\right]+\epsilon C+c E\left[\sup _{k \geq 1} \sigma_{n k}^{2}\right]\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $\sigma_{n k}^{2} \leq E\left[\epsilon^{2}+Y_{n k}^{2} I_{n k} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right] \leq \epsilon^{2}+\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} E\left[Y_{n j}^{2} I_{n j} \| \mathscr{F}_{n, j-1}\right]$, it follows by (35.36) that the last expression above is, in the limit, at most $K_{I}\left(\epsilon c+c \epsilon^{2}\right)$. Since $\epsilon$ is arbitrary, this completes the proof of the theorem under the assumption (35.37).

To remove this assumption, take $c>\sigma^{2}$, define $A_{n k}=\left[\sum_{j=1}^{k} \sigma_{n j}^{2} \leq c\right]$ and $A_{n \infty}=\left[\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n j}^{2} \leq c\right]$, and take $Z_{n k}=Y_{n k} I_{A_{n k}}$. From $A_{n k} \in \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}$ follow $E\left[Z_{n k}| | \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]=0$ and $\tau_{n k}^{2}=E\left[Z_{n k}^{2} \mid \mathscr{F}_{n, k-1}\right]=I_{A_{n k}} \sigma_{n k}^{2}$. Since $\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} \tau_{n j}^{2}$ is $\sum_{j=1}^{k} \sigma_{n j}^{2}$ on $A_{n k}-A_{n, k+1}$ and $\sum_{j=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{n j}^{2}$ on $A_{n \infty}$, the $Z$-array satisfies (35.37). Now $P\left(A_{n \infty}\right) \rightarrow 1$ by (35.35), and on $A_{n \infty}, \tau_{n k}^{2}=\sigma_{n k}^{2}$ for all $k$, so that the $Z$-array satisfies (35.35). And it satisfies (35.36) because $\left|Z_{n k}\right| \leq\left|Y_{n k}\right|$. Therefore, by the case already treated, $\Sigma_{k=1}^{\infty} Z_{n k} \Rightarrow \sigma N$. But since $\Sigma_{k=1}^{\infty} Y_{n k}$ coincides with this last sum on $A_{n^{\infty}}$, it, too, is asymptotically normal. $\square$

## PROBLEMS

35.1. Suppose that $\Delta_{1}, \Delta_{2}, \ldots$ are independent random variables with mean 0 . Let $X_{1}=\Delta_{1}$ and $X_{n+1}=X_{n}+\Delta_{n+1} f_{n}\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$, and suppose that the $X_{n}$ are integrable. Show that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a martingale. The martingales of gambling have this form.
35.2. Let $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ be independent random variables with mean 0 and variance $\sigma^{2}$. Let $X_{n}=\left(\sum_{k=1}^{n} Y_{k}\right)^{2}-n \sigma^{2}$ and show that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a martingale.
35.3. Suppose that $\left(Y_{n}\right)$ is a finite-state Markov chain with transition matrix $\left[p_{i j}\right]$. Suppose that $\sum_{j} p_{i j} x(j)=\lambda x(i)$ for all $i$ (the $x(i)$ are the components of a right eigenvector of the transition matrix). Put $X_{n}=\lambda^{-n} x\left(Y_{n}\right)$ and show that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a martingale.
35.4. Suppose that $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ are independent, positive random variables and that $E\left[Y_{n}\right]=1$. Put $X_{n}=Y_{1} \cdots Y_{n}$.
(a) Show that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a martingale and converges with probability 1 to an integrable $X$.
(b) Suppose specifically that $Y_{n}$ assumes the values $\frac{1}{2}$ and $\frac{3}{2}$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each. Show that $X=0$ with probability 1 . This gives an example where $E\left[\Pi_{n=1}^{\infty} Y_{n}\right] \neq \Pi_{n=1}^{\infty} E\left[Y_{n}\right]$ for independent, integrable, positive random variables. Show, however, that $E\left[\Pi_{n=1}^{\infty} Y_{n}\right] \leq \Pi_{n=1}^{\infty} E\left[Y_{n}\right]$ always holds.
35.5. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is a martingale satisfying $E\left[X_{1}\right]=0$ and $E\left[X_{n}^{2}\right]<\infty$. Show that $E\left[\left(X_{n+r}-X_{n}\right)^{2}\right]=\sum_{k=1}^{r} E\left[\left(X_{n+k}-X_{n+k-1}\right)^{2}\right]$ (the variance of the sum is the sum of the variances). Assume that $\sum_{n} E\left[\left(X_{n}-X_{n-1}\right)^{2}\right]<\infty$ and prove that $X_{n}$ converges with probability 1. Do this first by Theorem 35.5 and then (see Theorem 22.6) by Theorem 35.3.
35.6. Show that a submartingale $X_{n}$ can be represented as $X_{n}=Y_{n}+Z_{n}$, where $Y_{n}$ is a martingale and $0 \leq Z_{1} \leq Z_{2} \leq \cdots$. Hint Take $X_{0}=0$ and $\Delta_{n}=X_{n}-X_{n-1}$, and define $Z_{n}=\sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[\Delta_{k} \|_{k-1}^{\mathscr{F}_{k-1}}\right]\left(\mathscr{F}_{0}=\{0, \Omega\}\right)$.
35.7. If $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is a martingale and bounded either above or below, then $\sup _{n} E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right]<\infty$.
35.8. $\uparrow$ Let $X_{n}=\Delta_{1}+\cdots+\Delta_{n}$, where the $\Delta_{n}$ are independent and assume the values $\pm 1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each. Let $\tau$ be the smallest $n$ such that $X_{n}=1$ and define $X_{n}^{*}$ by (35.19). Show that the hypotheses of Theorem 35.5 are satisfied by $\left\{X_{n}^{*}\right\}$ but that it is impossible to integrate to the limit. Hint: Use (7.8) and Problem 35.7.
35.9. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be a martingale, and assume that $\left|X_{1}(\omega)\right|$ and $\left|X_{n}(\omega)-X_{n-1}(\omega)\right|$ are bounded by a constant independent of $\omega$ and $n$. Let $\tau$ be a stopping time with finite mean. Show that $X_{\tau}$ is integrable and that $E\left[X_{\tau}\right]=E\left[X_{1}\right]$.
35.10. $35.835 .9 \uparrow$ Use the preceding result to show that the $\tau$ in Problem 35.8 has infinite mean. Thus the waiting time until a symmetric random walk moves one step up from the starting point has infinite expected value.
35.11. Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be a Markov chain with countable state space $S$ and transition probabilities $p_{i j}$. A function $\varphi$ on $S$ is excessive or superharmonic if $\varphi(i) \geq \sum_{j} p_{i j} \varphi(j)$. Show by martingale theory that $\varphi\left(X_{n}\right)$ converges with probability 1 if $\varphi$ is bounded and excessive. Deduce from this that if the chain is irreducible and persistent, then $\varphi$ must be constant. Compare Problem 8.34.
35.12. $\uparrow$ A function $\varphi$ on the integer lattice in $R^{k}$ is superharmonic if for each lattice point $x, \varphi(x) \geq(2 k)^{-1} \sum \varphi(y)$, the sum extending over the $2 k$ nearest neighbors $y$. Show for $k=1$ and $k=2$ that a bounded superharmonic function is constant. Show for $k \geq 3$ that there exist nonconstant bounded harmonic functions.
35.13. $32.732 .9 \uparrow$ Let $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ be a probability space, let $\nu$ be a finite measure on $\mathscr{F}$, and suppose that $\mathscr{F}_{n} \uparrow \mathscr{F}_{\infty} \subset \mathscr{F}$. For $n \leq \infty$, let $X_{n}$ be the RadonNikodym derivative with respect to $P$ of the absolutely continuous part of $\nu$ when $P$ and $\nu$ are both restricted to $\mathscr{F}_{n}$. The problem is to extend Theorem 35.7 by showing that $X_{n} \rightarrow X_{\infty}$ with probability 1 .
(a) For $n \leq \infty$, let

$$
\nu(A)=\int_{A} X_{n} d P+\sigma_{n}(A), \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{n}
$$

be the decomposition of $\nu$ into absolutely continuous and singular parts with respect to $P$ on $\mathscr{F}_{n}$. Show that $X_{1}, X_{2} \ldots$ is a supermartingale and converges with probability 1 .
(b) Let

$$
\sigma_{\infty}(A)=\int_{A} Z_{n} d P+\sigma_{n}^{\prime}(A), \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{n}
$$

be the decomposition of $\sigma_{\infty}$ into absolutely continuous and singular parts with respect to $P$ on $\mathscr{F}_{n}$. Let $Y_{n}=E\left[X_{\infty} \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right]$, and prove

$$
\int_{A}\left(Y_{n}+Z_{n}\right) d P+\sigma_{n}^{\prime}(A)=\int_{A} X_{n} d P+\sigma_{n}(A), \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{n}
$$

Conclude that $Y_{n}+Z_{n}=X_{n}$ with probability 1 . Since $Y_{n}$ converges to $X_{\infty}, Z_{n}$ converges with probability 1 to some $Z$. Show that $\int_{A} Z d P \leq \sigma_{\infty}(A)$ for $A \in \mathscr{F}_{\infty}$, and conclude that $Z=0$ with probability 1 .
35.14. (a) Show that $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is a martingale with respect to $\left\{\mathscr{F}_{n}\right\}$ if and only if, for all $n$ and all stopping times $\tau$ such that $\tau \leq n, E\left[X_{n} \|_{\tau}\right]=X_{\tau}$.
(b) Show that, if $\left(X_{n}\right)$ is a martingale and $\tau$ is a bounded stopping time, then $E\left[X_{\tau}\right]=E\left[X_{1}\right]$.
35.15. 31.9 ↑ Suppose that $\mathscr{F}_{n} \uparrow \mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ and $A \in \mathscr{F}_{\infty}$, and prove that $P\left[A \| \mathscr{F}_{n}\right] \rightarrow I_{A}$ with probability 1 . Compare Lebesgue's density theorem.
35.16. Theorems 35.6 and 35.9 have analogues in Hilbert space. For $n \leq \infty$, let $P_{n}$ be the perpendicular projection on a subspace $M_{n}$. Then $P_{n} x \rightarrow P_{\infty} x$ for all $x$ if either (a) $M_{1} \subset M_{2} \subset \cdots$ and $M_{\infty}$ is the closure of $\bigcup_{n<\infty} M_{n}$ or (b) $M_{1} \supset M_{2} \supset \cdots$ and $M_{\infty}=\bigcap_{n<\infty} M_{n}$.
35.17. Suppose that $\theta$ has an arbitrary distribution, and suppose that, conditionally on $\theta$, the random variables $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and normally distributed with mean $\theta$ and variance $\sigma^{2}$. Construct such a sequence $\left\{\theta, Y_{1}, Y_{2}, \ldots\right\}$. Prove (35.31).
35.18. It is shown on p. 471 that optional stopping has no effect on likelihood ratios This is not true of tests of significance. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed and assume the values 1 and 0 with probabilities $p$ and $1-p$. Consider the null hypothesis that $p=\frac{1}{2}$ and the alternative that $p>\frac{1}{2}$. The usual .05 -level test of significance is to reject the null
hypothesis if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{2}{\sqrt{n}}\left(X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}-\frac{1}{2} n\right)>1.645 . \tag{35.40}
\end{equation*}
$$

For this test the chance of falsely rejecting the null hypothesis is approximately $P[N>1.645] \approx .05$ if $n$ is large and fixed. Suppose that $n$ is not fixed in advance of sampling, and show by the law of the iterated logarithm that, even if $p$ is, in fact, $\frac{1}{2}$, there are with probability 1 infinitely many $n$ for which (35.40) holds.
35.19. (a) Suppose that (35.32) and (35.33) hold. Suppose further that, for constants $s_{n}^{2}, s_{n}^{-2} \sum_{k=1}^{n} \sigma_{k}^{2} \rightarrow_{p} 1$ and $s_{n}^{-2} \sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[Y_{k}^{2} I_{\left[\left|Y_{k}\right| \geq s_{n}\right]}\right] \rightarrow 0$, and show that $s_{n}^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} Y_{k} \Rightarrow N$. Hint: Simplify the proof of Theorem 35.11.
(b) The Lindeberg-Lévy theorem for martingales Suppose that

$$
\ldots, Y_{-1}, Y_{0}, Y_{1}, \ldots
$$

is stationary and ergodic (p. 494) and that

$$
E\left[Y_{k}^{2}\right]<\infty \quad \text { and } \quad E\left[Y_{k} \| Y_{k-1}, Y_{k-2}, \ldots\right]=0 .
$$

Prove that $\sum_{k=1}^{n} Y_{k} / \sqrt{n}$ is asymptotically normal. Hint: Use Theorem 36.4 and the remark following the statement of Lindeberg's Theorem 27.2.
35.20. $24.4 \uparrow$ Suppose that the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$ in Problem 24.4 is trivial. Deduce from Theorem 35.9 that $P\left[A \| T^{-n} \mathscr{F}\right] \rightarrow P\left[A \| \mathscr{F}_{\infty}\right]=P(A)$ with probability 1, and conclude that $T$ is mixing.

## CHAPTER7

## Stochastic Processes

## SECTION 36. KOLMOGOROV'S EXISTENCE THEOREM

## Stochastic Processes

A stochastic process is a collection $\left[X_{t}: t \in T\right]$ of random variables on a probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ). The sequence of gambler's fortunes in Section 7, the sequences of independent random variables in Section 22, the martingales in Section 35-all these are stochastic processes for which $T=\{1,2, \ldots\}$. For the Poisson process $\left[N_{1}: t \geq 0\right]$ of Section 23, $T=[0, \infty)$. For all these processes the points of $T$ are thought of as representing time. In most cases, $T$ is the set of integers and time is discrete, or else $T$ is an interval of the line and time is continuous. For the general theory of this section, however, $T$ can be quite arbitrary.

## Finite-Dimensional Distributions

A process is usually described in terms of distributions it induces in Euclidean spaces. For each $k$-tuple ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ ) of distinct elements of $T$, the random vector ( $X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}$ ) has over $R^{k}$ some distribution $\mu_{t_{1}, t_{k}}$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{l_{1}} \quad l_{k}(H)=P\left[\left(X_{l_{1}}, \ldots, X_{l_{k}}\right) \in H\right], \quad H \in \mathscr{R}^{k} . \tag{36.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

These probability measures $\mu_{l_{1}, l_{k}}$ are the finite-dimensional distributions of the stochastic process $\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$. The system of finite-dimensional distributions does not completely determine the properties of the process. For example, the Poisson process $\left[N_{I}: t \geq 0\right]$ as defined by (23.5) has sample paths (functions $N_{l}(\omega)$ with $\omega$ fixed and $t$ varying) that are step functions. But (23.28) defines a process that has the same finite-dimensional distributions and has sample paths that are not step functions. Nevertheless, the first step in a general theory is to construct processes for given systems of finitedimensional distributions.

Now (36.1) implies two consistency properties of the system $\mu_{1_{1} . .1_{k}}$. Suppose the $H$ in (36.1) has the form $H=H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\left(H_{i} \in \mathscr{R}^{1}\right)$, and consider a permutation $\pi$ of $(1,2, \ldots, k)$. Since $\left[\left(X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}\right) \in\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times\right.\right. \left.\left.H_{k}\right)\right]$ and $\left[\left(X_{I_{\pi!}}, \ldots, X_{i_{\pi k}}\right) \in\left(H_{\pi 1} \times \cdots \times H_{\pi k}\right)\right]$ are the same event, it follows by (36.1) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{l_{1} \quad I_{k}}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\right)=\mu_{l_{\pi i} \quad I_{\pi k}}\left(H_{\pi 1} \times \cdots \times H_{\pi k}\right) . \tag{36.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

For example, if $\mu_{s, t}=\nu \times \nu^{\prime}$, then necessarily $\mu_{t, s}=\nu^{\prime} \times \nu$.
The second consistency condition is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{t_{1}} \quad t_{k-1}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k-1}\right)=\mu_{t_{1}} \quad t_{k-1 t_{k}}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k-1} \times R^{1}\right) . \tag{36.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is clear because ( $X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k-1}}$ ) lies in $H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k-1}$ if and only if $\left(X_{i_{1}}, \ldots, X_{i_{k-1}}, X_{i_{k}}\right)$ lies in $H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k-1} \times R^{1}$.

Measures $\mu_{t_{1} \ldots t_{k}}$ coming from a process $\left[X_{l}: t \in T\right]$ via (36.1) necessarily satisfy (36.2) and (36.3). Kolmogorcv's existence theorem says conversely that if a given system of measures satisfies the two consistency conditions, then there exists a stochastic process having these finite-dimensional distributions. The proof is a construction, one which is more easily understood if (36.2) and (36.3) are combined into a single condition.

Define $\varphi_{\pi}: R^{k} \rightarrow R^{k}$ by

$$
\varphi_{\pi}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=\left(x_{\pi-1_{1}}, \ldots, x_{\pi-1_{k}}\right) ;
$$

$\varphi_{\pi}$ applies the permutation $\pi$ to the coordinates (for example, if $\pi$ sends $x_{3}$ to first position, then $\pi^{-1} 1=3$ ). Since $\varphi_{\pi}^{-1}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\right)=H_{\pi!} \times \cdots \times H_{\pi k}$, it follows from (36.2) that

$$
\mu_{t_{\pi i} \quad I_{\pi k}} \varphi_{\pi}^{-1}(H)=\mu_{t_{1} \quad \ldots,}(H)
$$

for rectangles $H$. But then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{i_{1}, i_{k}}=\mu_{i_{\pi 1} .} l_{\pi k} \varphi_{\pi}^{-1} . \tag{36.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

Similarly, if $\varphi: R^{k} \rightarrow R^{k-1}$ is the projection $\varphi\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k-1}^{\prime}\right)$, then (36.3) is the same thing as

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{l_{1}, . l_{k-1}}=\mu_{l_{1}, l_{k}} \varphi^{-1} \tag{36.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

The conditions (36.4) and (36.5) have a common extension. Suppose that ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ) is an $m$-tuple of distinct elements of $T$ and that each element of ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ ) is also an element of ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ). Then ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ ) must be the initial segment of some permutation of ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ); that is, $k \leq m$ and there
is a permutation $\pi$ of $(1,2, \ldots, m)$ such that

$$
\left(u_{\pi^{-1}}, \ldots, u_{\pi^{-1} m}\right)=\left(t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}, t_{k+1}, \ldots, t_{m}\right),
$$

where $t_{k+1}, \ldots, t_{m}$ are elements of ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ) that do not appear in ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ ). Define $\psi: R^{m} \rightarrow R^{k}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
\psi\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{m}\right)=\left(x_{\pi^{-1} 1}, \ldots, x_{\pi^{-1} k}\right) ; \tag{36.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

$\psi$ applies $\pi$ to the coordinates and then projects onto the first $k$ of them. Since $\psi\left(X_{u_{1}}, \ldots, X_{u_{m}}\right)=\left(X_{i_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}\right)$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{l_{1}} \quad \imath_{k}=\mu_{u_{1} .} \quad u_{m} \psi^{-1} \tag{36.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

This contains (36.4) and (36.5) as special cases, but as $\psi$ is a coordinate permutation followed by a sequence of projections of the form $\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{l}\right) \rightarrow$ ( $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{1-1}$ ), it is also a consequence of these special cases.

## Product Spaces

The standard construction of the general process involves product spaces. Let $T$ be an arbitrary index set, and let $R^{T}$ be the collection of all real functions on $T$-all maps from $T$ into the real line. If $T=\{1,2, \ldots, k\}$, a real function on $T$ can be identified with a $k$-tuple ( $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}$ ) of real numbers, and so $R^{T}$ can be identified with $k$-dimensional Euclidean space $R^{k}$. If $T=\{1,2, \ldots\}$, a real function on $T$ is a sequence $\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right\}$ of real numbers. If $T$ is an interval, $R^{T}$ consists of all real functions, however irregular, on the interval. The theory of $R^{T}$ is an elaboration of the theory of the analogous but simpler space $S^{\infty}$ of Section 2 (p. 27).

Whatever the set $T$ may be, an element of $R^{T}$ will be denoted $x$. The value of $x$ at $t$ will be denoted $x(t)$ or $x_{i}$, depending on whether $x$ is viewed as a function of $t$ with domain $T$ or as a vector with components indexed by the elements $t$ of $T$. Just as $R^{k}$ can be regarded as the Cartesian product of $k$ copies of the real line, $R^{T}$ can be regarded as a product space-a product of copies of the real line, one copy for each $t$ in $T$.

For each $t$ define a mapping $Z_{t}: R^{T} \rightarrow R^{1}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
Z_{l}(x)=x(t)=x_{l} . \tag{36.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

The $Z_{t}$ are called the coordinate functions or projections. When later on a probability measure has been defined on $R^{T}$, the $Z_{t}$ will be random variables, the coordinate variables. Frequently, the value $Z_{t}(x)$ is instead denoted $Z(t, x)$. If $x$ is fixed, $Z(\cdot, x)$ is a real function on $T$ and is, in fact, nothing other than $x(\cdot)$-that is, $x$ itself. If $t$ is fixed, $Z(t, \cdot)$ is a real function on $R^{T}$ and is identical with the function $Z_{1}$ defined by (36.8).

There is a natural generalization to $R^{T}$ of the idea of the $\sigma$-field of $k$-dimensional Borel sets. Let $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by all the coordinate functions $Z_{t}, t \in T: \mathscr{R}^{T}=\sigma\left[Z_{t}: t \in T\right]$. It is generated by the sets of the form

$$
\left[x \in R^{T}: Z_{l}(x) \in H\right]=\left[x \in R^{T}: x_{l} \in H\right]
$$

for $t \in T$ and $H \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$. If $T=\{1,2, \ldots, k\}$, then $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ coincides with $\mathscr{R}^{k}$.
Consider the class $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ consisting of the sets of the form

$$
\begin{align*}
A & =\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(Z_{t_{1}}(x), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(x)\right) \in H\right]  \tag{36.9}\\
& =\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{t_{1}}, \ldots, x_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right]
\end{align*}
$$

where $k$ is an integer, $\left(t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}\right)$ is a $k$-tuple of distinct points of $T$, and $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$. Sets of this form, elements of $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$, are called finite-dimensional sets, or cylinders. Of course, $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ generates $\mathscr{R}^{T}$. Now $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ is not a $\sigma$-field, does not coincide with $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ (unless $T$ is finite), but the following argument shows that it is a field.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-015.jpg?height=560&width=843&top_left_y=1433&top_left_x=488)

If $T$ is an interval, the cylinder $\left[x \in R^{T}: \alpha_{1}<x\left(t_{1}\right) \leq \beta_{1}, \alpha_{2}<x\left(t_{2}\right) \leq \beta_{2}\right]$ consists of the functions that go through the two gates shown; $y$ lies in the cylinder and $z$ does not (they need not be continuous functions, of course)

The complement of (36.9) is $R^{T}-A=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{t_{1}}, \ldots, x_{t_{k}}\right) \in R^{k}-H\right]$, and so $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ is closed under complementation. Suppose that $A$ is given by (36.9) and $B$ is given by

$$
\begin{equation*}
B=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{s_{1}}, \ldots, x_{s_{j}}\right) \in I\right], \tag{36.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $I \in \mathscr{R}^{j}$. Let ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ) be an $m$-tuple containing all the $t_{\alpha}$ and all the $s_{\beta}$. Now $\left(t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}\right)$ must be the initial segment of some permutation of ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ), and if $\psi$ is as in (36.6) and $H^{\prime}=\psi^{-1} H$, then $H^{\prime} \in \mathscr{R}^{m}$ and $A$ is
given by

$$
\begin{equation*}
A=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{u_{1}}, \ldots, x_{u_{m}}\right) \in H^{\prime}\right] \tag{36.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

as well as by (36.9). Similarly, $B$ can be put in the form

$$
\begin{equation*}
B=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{u_{1}}, \ldots, x_{u_{m}}\right) \in I^{\prime}\right], \tag{36.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $I^{\prime} \in \mathscr{R}^{m}$. But then

$$
\begin{equation*}
A \cup B=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{u_{1}}, \ldots, x_{u_{m}}\right) \in H^{\prime} \cup I^{\prime}\right] . \tag{36.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $H^{\prime} \cup I^{\prime} \in \mathscr{R}^{m}, A \cup B$ is a cylinder. This proves that $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ is a field such that $\mathscr{R}^{T}=\sigma\left(\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}\right)$.

The $Z_{1}$ are measurable functions on the measurable space ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ). If $P$ is a probability measure on $\mathscr{R}^{T}$, then $\left[Z_{l}: t \in T\right]$ is a stochastic process on ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}, P$ ), the coordinate-variable process.

## Kolmogorov's Existence Theorem

The existence theorem can be stated two ways:
Theorem 36.1. If $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$ are a system of distributions satisfying the consistency conditions (36.2) and (36.3), then there is a probability measure $P$ on $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ such that the coordinate-variable process $\left[Z_{t}: t \in T\right]$ on $\left(R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}, P\right)$ has the $\mu_{l_{1}} \quad l_{k}$ as its finite-dimensional distributions.

Theorem 36.2. If $\mu_{I_{1}}, I_{k}$ are a system of distributions satisfying the consistency conditions (36.2) and (36.3), then there exists on some probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ a stochastic process $\left[X_{1}: t \in T\right]$ having the $\mu_{t_{1}} ._{t_{k}}$ as its finitedimensional distributions.

For many purposes the underlying probability space is irrelevant, the joint distributions of the variables in the process being all that matters, so that the two theorems are equally useful. As a matter of fact, they are equivalent anyway. Obviously, the first implies the second. To prove the converse, suppose that the process $\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$ on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ has finite-dimensional distributions $\mu_{t_{1} \ldots t_{k}}$, and define a map $\xi: \Omega \rightarrow R^{T}$ by the requirement

$$
\begin{equation*}
Z_{l}(\xi(\omega))=X_{l}(\omega), \quad t \in T . \tag{36.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

For each $\omega, \xi(\omega)$ is an element of $R^{T}$, a real function on $T$, and the
requirement is that $X_{l}(\omega)$ be its value at $t$. Clearly,

$$
\begin{align*}
\xi^{-1}[ & \left.x \in R^{T}:\left(Z_{t_{1}}(x), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(x)\right) \in H\right]  \tag{36.15}\\
& =\left[\omega \in \Omega:\left(Z_{t_{1}}(\xi(\omega)), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(\xi(\omega))\right) \in H\right] \\
& =\left[\omega \in \Omega:\left(X_{t_{1}}(\omega), \ldots, X_{t_{k}}(\omega)\right) \in H\right] ;
\end{align*}
$$

since the $X_{1}$ are random variables, measurable $\mathscr{F}$, this set lies in $\mathscr{F}$ if $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$. Thus $\xi^{-1} A \in \mathscr{F}$ for $A \in \mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$, and so (Theorem 13.1) $\xi$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{R}^{T}$. By (36.15) and the assumption that $\left[X_{t}: t \in T\right]$ has finitedimensional distributions $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}, P \xi^{-1}$ (see (13.7)) satisfies

$$
\begin{align*}
& P \xi^{-1}\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(Z_{t_{1}}(x), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(x)\right) \in H\right]  \tag{36.16}\\
& \quad=P\left[\omega \in \Omega:\left(X_{t_{1}}(\omega), \ldots, X_{t_{k}}(\omega)\right) \in H\right]=\mu_{t_{1}} \quad t_{k}(H)
\end{align*}
$$

Thus the coordinate-variable process [ $Z_{t}: t \in T$ ] on $\left(R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}, P \xi^{-1}\right)$ also has finite-dimensional distributions $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$.

Therefore, to prove either of the two versions of Kolmogorov's existence theorem is to prove the other one as well.

Example 36.1. Suppose that $T$ is finite, say $T=\{1,2, \ldots, k\}$. Then ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ) is ( $R^{k}, \mathscr{R}^{k}$ ), and taking $P=\mu_{1,2, \ldots, k}$ satisfies the requirements of Theorem 36.1. $\square$

Example 36.2. Suppose that $T=\{1,2, \ldots\}$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{t_{1}, \ldots t_{k}}=\mu_{t_{1}} \times \cdots \times \mu_{t_{k}}, \tag{36.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $\mu_{1}, \mu_{2}, \ldots$ are probability distributions on the line. The consistency conditions are easily checked, and the probability measure $P$ guaranteed by Theorem 36.1 is product measure on the product space ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ). But by Theorem 20.4 there exists on some ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) an independent sequence $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ of random variables with respective distributions $\mu_{1}, \mu_{2}, \ldots$; then (36.17) is the distribution of $\left(X_{1_{1}}, \ldots, X_{1_{n}}\right)$. For the special case (36.17), Theorem 36.2 (and hence Theorem 36.1) was thus proved in Section 20. The existence of independent sequences with prescribed distributions was the measure-theoretic basis of all the probabilistic developments in Chapters 4, 5, and 6: even dependent processes like the Poisson were constructed from independent sequences. The existence of independent sequences can also be made the basis of a proof of Theorems 36.1 and 36.2 in their full generality; see the second proof below. $\square$

Example 36.3. The preceding example has an analogue in the space $S^{\infty}$ of sequences (2.15). Here the finite set $S$ plays the role of $R^{1}$, the $z_{n}(\cdot)$ are analogues of the $Z_{n}(\cdot)$, and the product measure defined by (2.21) is the analogue of the product measure specified by (36.17) with $\mu_{i}=\mu$. See also Example 24.2. The theory for $S^{\infty}$ is simple because $S$ is finite: see Theorem 2.3 and the lemma it depends on. $\square$

Example 36.4. If $T$ is a subset of the line, it is convenient to use the order structure of the line and take the $\mu_{s_{1}} s_{k}$ to be specified initially only for $k$-tuples $\left(s_{1}, \ldots, s_{k}\right)$ that are in increasing order:

$$
s_{1}<s_{2}<\cdots<s_{k} .
$$

It is natural for example to specify the finite-dimensional distributions for the Poisson processes for increasing sequences of time points alone; see (23.27).

Assume that the $\mu_{s_{i}} \quad s_{k}$ for $k$-tuples satisfying (36.18) have the consistency property

$$
\begin{align*}
\mu_{s_{1}} & s_{i-1} s_{i+1} \quad s_{k}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{i-1} \times H_{i+1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\right)  \tag{36.19}\\
& =\mu_{s_{1}, s_{k}}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{i-1} \times R^{1} \times H_{i+1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\right) .
\end{align*}
$$

For given $s_{1}, \ldots, s_{k}$ satisfying (36.18), take ( $X_{s_{1}}, \ldots, X_{s_{k}}$ ) to have distribution $\mu_{s_{i}} s_{k}$. If $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ is a permutation of $s_{1}, \ldots, s_{k}$, take $\mu_{t_{i} . t_{k}}$ to be the distribution of $\left(X_{l_{1}}, \ldots, X_{l_{k}}\right)$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{i_{1}} \quad i_{k}\left(H_{1} \times \cdots \times H_{k}\right)=P\left[X_{i_{i}} \in H_{i}, i \leq k\right] . \tag{36.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

This unambiguously defines a collection of finite-dimensional distributions. Are they consistent?

If $t_{\pi 1}, \ldots, t_{\pi k}$ is a permutation of $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$, then it is also a permutation of $s_{1}, \ldots, s_{k}$, and by the definition (36.20), $\mu_{t_{\pi i}} t_{\pi k}$ is the distribution of ( $X_{t_{\pi i}}, \ldots, X_{t_{\pi k}}$ ), which immediately gives (36.2), the first of the consistency conditions. Because of (36.19), $\mu_{s_{i}, s_{i-1} s_{i+1}}, s_{k}$ is the distribution of $\left(X_{s_{1}}, \ldots, X_{s_{i-1}}, X_{s_{i+1}}, \ldots, X_{s_{k}}\right)$, and if $t_{k}=s_{i}$, then $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k-1}$ is a perrnutation of $s_{1}, \ldots, s_{i-1}, s_{i+1}, \ldots, s_{k}$, which are in increasing order. By the definition (36.20) applied to $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k-1}$ it therefore follows that $\mu_{t_{1}, t_{k-1}}$ is the distribution of ( $X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k-1}}$ ). But this gives (36.3), the second of the consistency conditions.

It will therefore follow from the existence theorem that if $T \subset R^{1}$ and $\mu_{s_{i}} . s_{k}$ is defined for all $k$-tuples in increasing order, and if (36.19) holds, then there exists a stochastic process [ $X_{1}: t \in T$ ] satisfying (36.1) for increasing $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$. $\square$

Two proofs of Kolmogorov's existence theorem will be given. The first is based on the extension theorem of Section 3.

First proof of Kolmogorov's theorem. Consider the first formulation, Theorem 36.1. If $A$ is the cylinder (36.9), define

$$
\begin{equation*}
P(A)=\mu_{t_{1}, \quad i_{k}}(H) . \tag{36.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

This gives rise to the question of consistency because $A$ will have other representations as a cylinder. Suppose, in fact, that $A$ coincides with the cylinder $B$ defined by ( 36.10 ). As observed before, if ( $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{m}$ ) contains all the $t_{\alpha}$ and $s_{\beta}, A$ is also given by (36.11), where $H^{\prime}=\psi^{-1} H$ and $\psi$ is defined in (36.6). Since the consistency conditions (36.2) and (36.3) imply the more general one (36.7), $P(A)=\mu_{i_{1}} i_{k}(H)=\mu_{u_{1} \ldots u_{m}}\left(H^{\prime}\right)$. Similarly, (36.10) has the form (36.12), and $P(B)=\mu_{s_{i}} s_{i}(I)=\mu_{u_{i} . . u_{m}}\left(I^{\prime}\right)$. Since the $u_{\gamma}$ are distinct, for any real numbers $z_{1}, \ldots, z_{m}$ there are points $x$ of $R^{T}$ for which $\left(x_{u_{1}}, \ldots, x_{u_{m}}\right)=\left(z_{1}, \ldots, z_{m}\right)$. From this it follows that if the cylinders (36.11) and (36.12) coincide, then $H^{\prime}=I^{\prime}$. Hence $A=B$ implies that $P(A)= \mu_{u_{1}} \quad u_{m}\left(H^{\prime}\right)=\mu_{u_{1}} \quad u_{m}\left(I^{\prime}\right)=P(B)$, and the definition (36.21) is indeed consistent.

Now consider disjoint cylinders $A$ and $B$. As usual, the index sets may be taken identical. Assume then that $A$ is given by (36.11) and $B$ by (36.12), so that (36.13) holds. If $H^{\prime} \cap I^{\prime}$ were nonempty, then $A \cap B$ would be nonempty as well. Therefore, $H^{\prime} \cap I^{\prime}=\varnothing$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
P(A \cup B) & =\mu_{u_{1}} \quad u_{m}\left(H^{\prime} \cup I^{\prime}\right) \\
& =\mu_{u_{1}} \quad u_{m}\left(H^{\prime}\right)+\mu_{u_{1} . . u_{m}}\left(I^{\prime}\right)=P(A)+P(B) .
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, $P$ is finitely additive on $\mathscr{P}_{0}^{T}$. Clearly, $P\left(R^{T}\right)=1$.
Suppose that $P$ is shown to be countably additive on $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$. By Theorem 3.1, $P$ will then extend to a probability measure on $\mathscr{R}^{T}$. By the way $P$ was defined on $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(Z_{l_{1}}(x), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(x)\right) \in H\right]=\mu_{l_{1}} \quad l_{k}(H), \tag{36.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

and therefore the coordinate process [ $Z_{l}: t \in T$ ] will have the required finite-dimensional distributions.

It suffices, then, to prove $P$ countably additive on $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$, and this will follow if $A_{n} \in \mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$ and $A_{n} \downarrow \varnothing$ together imply $P\left(A_{n}\right) \downarrow 0$ (see Example 2.10). Suppose that $A_{1} \supset A_{2} \supset \cdots$ and that $P\left(A_{n}\right) \geq \epsilon>0$ for all $n$. The problem is to show that $\cap_{n} A_{n}$ must be nonempty. Since $A_{n} \in \mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}$, and since the index set involved in the specification of a cylinder can always be permuted and
expanded, there exists a sequence $t_{1}, t_{2}, \ldots$ of points in $T$ for which

$$
A_{n}=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{l_{1}}, \ldots, x_{l_{n}}\right) \in H_{n}\right],
$$

where ${ }^{\dagger} H_{n} \in \mathscr{R}^{n}$.
Of course, $P\left(A_{n}\right)=\mu_{I_{1}} \ldots t_{n}\left(H_{n}\right)$. By Theorem 12.3 (regularity), there exists inside $H_{n}$ a compact set $K_{n}$ such that $\mu_{I_{i}} t_{n}\left(H_{n}-K_{n}\right)<\epsilon / 2^{n+1}$. If $B_{n}=\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(x_{1_{1}}, \ldots, x_{1_{n}}\right) \in K_{n}\right]$, then $P\left(A_{n}-B_{n}\right)<\epsilon / 2^{n+1}$. Put $C_{n}= \cap_{k=1}^{n} B_{k}$. Then $C_{n} \subset B_{n} \subset A_{n}$ and $P\left(A_{n}-C_{n}\right)<\epsilon / 2$, so that $P\left(C_{n}\right)>\epsilon / 2 >0$. Therefore, $C_{n} \subset C_{n-1}$ and $C_{n}$ is nonempty.

Choose a point $x^{(n)}$ of $R^{T}$ in $C_{n}$. If $n \geq k$, then $x^{(n)} \in C_{n} \subset C_{k} \subset B_{k}$ and hence $\left(x_{1_{i}}^{(n)}, \ldots, x_{1_{k}}^{(n)}\right) \in K_{k}$. Since $K_{k}$ is bounded, the sequence $\left\{x_{1_{k}}^{(1)}, x_{t_{k}}^{(2)}, \ldots\right\}$ is bounded for each $k$. By the diagonal method [A14] select an increasing sequence $n_{1}, n_{2}, \ldots$ of integers such that $\lim _{i} x_{I_{k}}^{\left(n_{i}\right)}$ exists for each $k$. There is in $R^{T}$ some point $x$ whose $t_{k}$ th coordinate is this limit for each $k$. But then, for each $k,\left(x_{t_{1}}, \ldots, x_{t_{k}}\right)$ is the limit as $i \rightarrow \infty$ of $\left(x_{t_{1}^{\prime}}^{\left(n_{i}\right)}, \ldots, x_{i_{k}}^{\left(n_{i}\right)}\right)$ and hence lies in $K_{k}$. But that means that $x$ itself lies in $B_{k}$ and hence in $A_{k}$. Thus $x \in \bigcap_{k=1}^{\infty} A_{k}$, which completes the proof. ${ }^{\ddagger}$

The second proof of Kolmogorov's theorem goes in two stages, first for countable $T$, then for gcneral $T$.*

Second proof for countable T. The result for countable $T$ will be proved in its second formulation, Theorem 36.2. It is no restriction to enumerate $T$ as $\left\{t_{1}, t_{2}, \ldots\right\}$ and then to identify $t_{n}$ with $n$; in other words, it is no restriction to assume that $T=\{1,2, \ldots\}$. Write $\mu_{n}$ in place of $\mu_{1,2 .}, n^{*}$.

By Theorem 20.4 there exists on a probability space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ (which can be taken to be the unit interval) an independent sequence $U_{1}, U_{2}, \ldots$ of random variables each uniformly distributed over $(0,1)$. Let $F_{1}$ be the distribution function corresponding to $\mu_{1}$. If the "inverse" $g_{1}$ of $F_{1}$ is defined over $(0,1)$ by $g_{1}(s)=\inf \left[x: s \leq F_{1}(x)\right]$, then $X_{1}=-g_{1}\left(U_{1}\right)$ has distribution $\mu_{1}$ by the usual argument: $P\left[g_{1}\left(U_{1}\right) \leq x\right]=P\left[U_{1} \leq F_{1}(x)\right] =F_{1}(x)$.

The problem is to construct $X_{2}, X_{3}, \ldots$ inductively in such a way that

$$
\begin{equation*}
X_{k}=h_{k}\left(U_{1}, \ldots, U_{k}\right) \tag{36.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

for a Borel function $h_{k}$ and $\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$ has the distribution $\mu_{n}$. Assume that $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}$ have been defined ( $n \geq 2$ ): they have joint distribution $\mu_{n-1}$ and (36.23) holds for $k \leq n-1$. The idea now is to construct an appropriate conditional distribution function $F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$; here $F_{n}\left(x \mid X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{n-1}(\omega)\right)$ will have the value $P\left[X_{n} \leq x \| X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right]_{\omega}$ would have if $X_{n}$ were already defined. If $g_{n}\left(\cdot \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$
${ }^{\dagger}$ In general, $A_{n}$ will involve indices $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{a_{n}}$, where $a_{1}<a_{2}<\cdots$. For notational simplicity $a_{n}$, is taken as $n$. As a matter of fact, this can be arranged anyway: Take $A_{a_{n}}^{\prime}=A_{n}, A_{k}^{\prime}=[x$ : $\left.\left(x_{t_{1}}, \ldots, x_{t_{k}}\right) \in R^{k}\right]=R^{T}$ for $k<a_{1}$, and $A_{k}^{\prime}=\left[x:\left(x_{t_{1}}, \ldots, x_{t_{k}}\right) \in H_{n} \times R^{k-a_{n}}\right]=A_{n}$ for $a_{n}< k<a_{n+1}$. Now relabel $A_{n}^{\prime}$ as $A_{n}$.
${ }^{\ddagger}$ The last part of the argument is, in effect, the proof that a countable product of compact sets is compact.
*This second proof, which may be omitted, uses the conditional-probability theory of Section 33.
is the "inverse" function, then $X_{n}(\omega)=g_{n}\left(U_{n}(\omega) \mid X_{1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{n-1}(\omega)\right)$ will by the usual argument have the right conditional distribution given $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}$, so that ( $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}, X_{n}$ ) will have the right distribution over $R^{n}$.

To construct the conditional distribution function, apply Theorem 33.3 in ( $R^{n}, \mathscr{R}^{n}, \mu_{n}$ ) to get a conditional distribution of the last coordinate of ( $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}$ ) given the first $n-1$ of them. This will have (Theorem 20.1) the form $\nu\left(H ; \dot{x}_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$; it is a probability measure as $H$ varies over $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \int_{\left(x_{1}, \quad, x_{n-1}\right) \in M} \nu\left(H ; x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) d \mu_{n}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right) \\
& =\mu_{n}\left[x \in R^{n,}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \in M, x_{n} \in H\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the integrand involves only $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}$, and since $\mu_{n}$ by consistency projects to $\mu_{n-1}$ under the map $\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right) \rightarrow\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$, a change of variable gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \int_{M} \nu\left(H ; x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) d \mu_{n-1}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \\
& \quad=\mu_{n}\left[x \in R^{n}:\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \in M, x_{n} \in H\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Define $F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)=\nu\left((-\infty, x] ; x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$. Then $F_{n}\left(\cdot \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$ is a probability distribution function over the line, $F_{n}(x \mid \cdot)$ is a Borel function over $R^{n-1}$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \int_{M} F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) d \mu_{n-1}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \\
& \quad=\mu_{n}\left[x \in R^{n}:\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \in M, x_{n} \leq x\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Put $g_{n}\left(u \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)=\inf \left[x: u \leq F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)\right]$ for $0<u<1$. Since $F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$ is nondecreasing and right-continuous in $x, g_{n}\left(u \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \leq x$ if and only if $u \leq F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)$. Set $X_{n}=g_{n}\left(U_{n} \mid X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right)$. Since ( $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}$ ) has distribution $\mu_{n-1}$ and by (36.23) is independent of $U_{n}$, an application of (20.30) gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left[\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right) \in M, X_{n} \leq x\right] \\
& \quad=P\left[\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right) \in M, U_{n} \leq F_{n}\left(x \mid X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}\right)\right] \\
& \quad=\int_{M} P\left[U_{n} \leq F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right)\right] d \mu_{n-1}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \\
& \quad=\int_{M} F_{n}\left(x \mid x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) d \mu_{n-1}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \\
& \quad=\mu_{n}\left[x \in R^{n}:\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n-1}\right) \in M, x_{n} \leq x\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus $\left(X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}\right)$ has distribution $\mu_{n}$. Note that $X_{n}$, as a function of $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n-1}$ and $U_{n}$, is a function of $U_{1}, \ldots, U_{n}$ because (36.23) was assumed to hold for $k<n$. Hence (36.23) holds for $k=n$ as well.

Second proof for general T. Consider ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ) once again. If $S \subset T$, let $\mathscr{F}_{S}=\sigma\left[Z_{t}: t \in S\right]$. Then $\mathscr{F}_{S} \subset \mathscr{F}_{T}=\mathscr{R}^{T}$.

Suppose that $S$ is countable. By the case just treated, there exists a process [ $X_{t}$ : $t \in S]$ on some $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$-the space and the process depend on $S$-such that ( $X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}$ ) has distribution $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$ for every $k$-tuple ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ ) from $S$. Define a map $\xi: \Omega \rightarrow R^{T}$ by requiring that

$$
Z_{t}(\xi(\omega))= \begin{cases}X_{t}(\omega) & \text { if } t \in S, \\ 0 & \text { if } t \notin S .\end{cases}
$$

Now (36.15) holds as before if $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ all lie in $S$, and so $\xi$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{F}_{S}$. Further, (36.16) holds for $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ in $S$. Put $P_{S}=P \xi^{-1}$ on $\mathscr{F}_{S}$ Then $P_{S}$ is a probability measure on ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{F}_{S}$ ), and

$$
\begin{equation*}
P_{S}\left[x \in R^{T}:\left(Z_{t_{k}}(x), \ldots, Z_{t_{k}}(x)\right) \in H\right]=\mu_{t_{k}} \quad{ }_{t_{k}}(H) \tag{36.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

if $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$ and $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ all lie in $S$. (The various spaces ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) and processes [ $X_{t}: t \in S$ ] now become irrelevant.)

If $S_{0} \subset S$, and if $A$ is a cylinder (36.9) for which the $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ lie in $S_{0}$, then $P_{S_{0}}(A)$ and $P_{S}(A)$ coincide, their common value being $\mu_{r_{1}, r_{k}}(H)$. Since these cylinders generate $\mathscr{F}_{S_{11}}, P_{S_{0}}(A)=P_{S}(A)$ for all $A$ in $\mathscr{F}_{S_{0}}$. If $A$ lies both in $\mathscr{F}_{S_{1}}$ and $\mathscr{F}_{S_{2}}$, then $P_{S_{1}}(A)=P_{S_{1} \cup S_{2}}(A)=P_{S_{2}}(A)$. Thus $P(A)=P_{S}(A)$ consistently defines a set function on the class $\cup_{S} \mathscr{F}_{S}$, the union extending over the countable subsets $S$ of $T$. If $A_{n}$ lies in this union and $A_{n} \in \mathscr{F}_{S_{n}}$ ( $S_{n}$ countable), then $S=\bigcup_{n} S_{n}$ is countable and $\bigcup_{n} A_{n}$ lies in $\mathscr{F}_{S}$. Thus $\cup_{S} \mathscr{F}_{S}$ is a $\sigma$-field and so must coincide with $\mathscr{R}^{T}$. Therefore, $P$ is a probability measure on $\mathscr{R}^{T}$, and by (36.24) the coordinate process has under $P$ the required finite-dimensional distributions.

## The Inadequacy of $\mathscr{R}^{T}$

Theorem 36.3. Let $\left[X_{t}: t \in T\right]$ be a family of real functions on $\Omega$.
(i) If $A \in \sigma\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$ and $\omega \in A$, and if $X_{i}(\omega)=X \cdot\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$ for all $t \in T$, then $\omega^{\prime} \in A$.
(ii) If $A \in \sigma\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$, then $A \in \sigma\left[X_{i}: t \in S\right]$ for some countable subset $S$ of $T$.

Proof. Define $\xi: \Omega \rightarrow R^{T}$ by $Z_{l}(\xi(\omega))=X_{l}(\omega)$. Let $\mathscr{F}=\sigma\left[X_{l}: t \in T\right]$. By (36.15), $\xi$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{R}^{T}$ and hence $\mathscr{F}$ contains the class $\left[\xi^{-1} M\right.$ : $M \in \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ]. The latter class is a $\sigma$-field, however, and by (36.15) it contains the sets $\left[\omega \in \Omega:\left(X_{t_{1}}(\omega), \ldots, X_{t_{k}}(\omega)\right) \in H\right], H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}$, and hence contains the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$ they generate. Therefore

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sigma\left[X_{t}: t \in T\right]=\left[\xi^{-1} M: M \in \mathscr{R}^{T}\right] . \tag{36.25}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is an infinite-dimensional analogue of Theorem 20.1(i).

As for (i), the hypotheses imply that $\omega \in A=\xi^{-1} M$ and $\xi(\omega)=\xi\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$, so that $\omega^{\prime} \in A$ certainly follows.

For $S \subset T$, let $\mathscr{F}_{S}=\sigma\left[X_{i}: t \in S\right]$; (ii) says that $\mathscr{F}=\mathscr{F}_{T}$ coincides with $\mathscr{E}=\bigcup_{S} \mathscr{F}_{S}$, the union extending over the countable subsets $S$ of $T$. If $A_{1}, A_{2}, \ldots$ lie in $\mathscr{G}, A_{n}$ lies in $\mathscr{F}_{S_{n}}$ for some countable $S_{n}$, and so $\cup_{n} A_{n}$ lies in $\mathscr{I}$ because it lies in $\mathscr{F}_{S}$ for $S=\bigcup_{n} S_{n}$. Thus $\mathscr{I}$ is a $\sigma$-field, and since it contains the sets $\left[X_{1} \in H\right]$, it contains the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$ they generate. (This part of the argument was used in the second proof of the existence theorem.) $\square$

From this theorem it follows that various important sets lie outside the class $\mathscr{R}^{T}$. Suppose that $T=[0, \infty)$. Of obvious interest is the subset $C$ of $R^{T}$ consisting of the functions continuous over $[0, \infty)$. But $C$ is not in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$. For suppose it were. By part (ii) of the theorem (let $\Omega=R^{T}$ and put [ $Z_{i}: t \in T$ ] in the role of $\left[X_{1}: t \in T\right]$ ), $C$ would lie in $\sigma\left[Z_{1}: t \in S\right]$ for some countable $S \subset[0, \infty)$. But then by part (i) of the theorem (let $\Omega=R^{T}$ and put $\left[Z_{i}: t \in S\right]$ in the role of $\left[X_{l}: t \in T\right]$ ), if $x \in C$ and $Z_{l}(x)=Z_{l}(y)$ for all $t \in S$, then $y \in C$. From the assumption that $C$ lies in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ thus follows the existence of a countable set $S$ such that, if $x \in C$ and $x(t)=y(t)$ for all $t$ in $S$, then $y \in C$. But whatever countable set $S$ may be, for every continuous $x$ there obviously exist functions $y$ that have discontinuities but agree with $x$ on $S$. Therefore, cannot lie in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$.

What the argument shows is this: A set $A$ in $R^{T}$ cannot lie in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ unless there exists a countable subset $S$ of $T$ with the property that, if $x \in A$ and $x(t)=y(t)$ for all $t$ in $S$, then $y \in A$. Thus $A$ cannot lie in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ if it effectively involves all the points $t$ in the sense that, for each $x$ in $A$ and each $t$ in $T$, it is possible to move $x$ out of $A$ by changing its value at $t$ alone. And $C$ is such a set. For another, consider the set of functions $x$ over $T=[0, \infty)$ that are nondecreasing and assume as values $x(t)$ only nonnegative integers:

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left[x \in R^{(0, \infty)}: x(s) \leq x(t), x \leq t ; x(t) \in\{0,1, \ldots\}, t \geq 0\right] . \tag{36.26}
\end{equation*}
$$

This, too, lies outside $\mathscr{R}^{1}$.
In Section 23 the Poisson process was defined as follows: Let $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ be independent and identically distributed with the exponential distribution (the probability space $\Omega$ on which they are defined may by Theorem 20.4 be taken to be the unit interval with Lebesgue measure). Put $S_{0}=0$ and $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$. If $S_{n}(\omega)<S_{n+1}(\omega)$ for $n \geq 0$ and $S_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow \infty$, put $N(t, \omega) =N_{t}(\omega)=\max \left[n: S_{n}(\omega) \leq t\right]$ for $t \geq 0$; otherwise, put $N(t, \omega)=N_{t}(\omega)=0$ for $t \geq 0$. Then the stochastic process $\left[N_{i}: t \geq 0\right]$ has the finite-dimensional distributions described by the equations (23.27). The function $N(\cdot, \omega)$ is the path function or sample function ${ }^{\dagger}$ corresponding to $\omega$, and by the construction every path function lies in the set (36.26). This is a good thing if the

[^2]process is to be a model for, say, calls arriving at a telephone exchange: The sample path represents the history of the calls, its value at $t$ being the number of arrivals up to time $t$, and so it ought to be nondecreasing and integer-valued.

According to Theorem 36.1, there exists a measure $P$ on $R^{T}$ for $T=[0, \infty)$ such that the coordinate process $\left[Z_{1}: t \geq 0\right]$ on $\left(R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}, P\right)$ has the finitedimensional distributions of the Poisson process. This time does the path function $Z(\cdot, x)$ lie in the set (36.26) with probability 1 ? Since $Z(\cdot, x)$ is just $x$ itself, the question is whether the set ( 36.26 ) has $P$-measure 1 . But this set does not lie in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$, and so it has no measure at all.

An application of Kolmogorov's existence theorem will always yield a stochastic process with prescribed finite-dimensional distributions, but the process may lack certain path-function properties that it is reasonable to require of it as a model for some natural phenomenon. The special construction of Section 23 gers around this difficulty for the Poisson process, and in the next section a special construction will yield a model for Brownian motion with continuous paths. Section 38 treats a general method for producing stochastic processes that have prescribed finite-dimensional distributions and at the same time have path functions with desirable regularity properties.

## A Return to Ergodic Theory*

Write $R^{\infty}, \mathscr{R}_{0}^{\infty}, \mathscr{R}^{\infty}$ for $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}_{0}^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ in the case where the index set $\{0, \pm 1, \pm 2, \ldots\}$ consists of all the integers. Then $R^{\infty}$ is analogous to $S^{\infty}$ (Sections 2 and 24), except that here the sequences are doubly infinite:

$$
x=\left(\ldots, Z_{-1}(x), Z_{0}(x), Z_{1}(x), \ldots\right) .
$$

Let $T$ (not an index set) denote the shift: $Z_{k}(T x)=Z_{k+1}(x), k=0, \pm 1, \ldots$. This is like the shift in Section 24. Since $A \in \mathscr{R}_{0}^{\infty}$ implies $T^{-1} A \in \mathscr{H}_{0}^{\infty}, T$ is measurable $\mathscr{R}^{\infty} / \mathscr{R}^{\infty}$. Clearly, it is invertible.

For a stochastic process $X=\left(\ldots, X_{-1}, X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots\right)$ on $\left(\Omega, \mathscr{F}^{F}, P\right)$, define $\xi: \Omega \rightarrow R^{\infty}$ by (36.14): $\xi \omega=X(\omega)=\left(\ldots, X_{-1}(\omega), X_{0}(\omega), X_{1}(\omega), \ldots\right)$. The measure $P \xi^{-1}=P X^{-1}$ on ( $R^{\infty}, \mathscr{R}^{\infty}$ ) can be viewed as the distribution of $X$. Suppose that $X$ is stationary in the sense that, for each $k \geq 1$ and $H \in \mathscr{R}^{k}, P\left[\left(X_{n+1}, \ldots, X_{n+k}\right) \in H\right]$ is the same for all $n=0, \pm 1, \ldots$. Then the shift preserves $P \xi^{-1}$ (use (36.16) and Lemma 1, p. 311). The process $X$ is defined to be ergodic if under $P \xi^{-1}$ the shift is ergodic in the sense of Section 24.

In the ergodic case, it follows by the ergodic theorem that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(T^{k} x\right) \rightarrow \int_{R^{\infty}} f(x) P \xi^{-1}(d x) \tag{36.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

[^3]on a set of $P \xi^{-1}$-measure 1 , provided $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{R}^{\infty}$ and integrable. Carry (36.27) back to ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) by the inverse set mapping $\xi^{-1}$. Then
$$
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} f\left(\ldots, X_{k-1}, X_{k}, X_{k+1}, \ldots\right) \rightarrow E\left[f\left(\ldots, X_{-1}, X_{0}, X_{1}, \ldots\right)\right] \tag{36.28}
\end{equation*}
$$
with probability 1 : (36.28) holds at $\omega$ if and only if (36.27) holds at $x=\zeta \omega=X(\omega)$. It is understood that on the left in (36.28), $X_{k}$ is the center coordinate (the 0th coordinate) of the argument of $f$, and on the right, $X_{0}$ is the center coordinate: For stationary, ergodic $X$ and integrable $f,(36.28)$ holds with probability 1.

If the $X_{k}$ are independent, then the $Z_{k}$ are independent under $P \xi^{-1}$. In this case, $\lim _{n} P \xi^{-1}\left(A \cap T^{-n} B\right)=P \xi^{-1}(A) P \xi^{-1}(B)$ for $A$ and $B$ in $\mathscr{R}_{0}^{\infty}$, because for large enough $n$ the cylinders $A$ and $T^{-n} B$ depend on disjoint sets of time indices and hence are independent. But then it follows by approximation (Corollary 1 to Theorem 11.4) that the same limit holds for all $A$ and $B$ in $\mathscr{R}^{\infty}$. But for invariant $B$, this implies $P \xi^{-1}\left(B^{c} \cap B\right)=P \xi^{-1}\left(B^{c}\right) P \xi^{-1}(B)$, so that $P \xi^{-1}(B)$ is 0 or 1 , and the shift is ergodic under $P \xi^{-1}$ : If $X$ is stationary and independent, then it is ergodic.

If $f$ depends on just one coordinate of $x$, then (36.28) is in the independent case a consequence of the strong law of large numbers, Theorem 22.1. But (36.28) follows by the ergodic theorem even if $f$ involves all the coordinates in some complicated way.

Consider now a measurable real function $\phi$ on $R^{\infty}$. Define $\psi: R^{\infty} \rightarrow R^{\infty}$ by

$$
\psi(x)=\left(\ldots, \phi\left(T^{-1} x\right), \phi(x), \phi(T x), \ldots\right)
$$

here $\phi(x)$ is the center coordinate: $Z_{k}(\psi(x))=\phi\left(T^{k} x\right)$. It is easy to show that $\psi$ is measurable $\mathscr{R}^{\infty} / \mathscr{R}^{\infty}$ and commutes with the shift in the sense of Example 24.6. Therefore, $T$ preserves $P \xi^{-1} \psi^{-1}$ if it preserves $P \xi^{-1}$, and it is ergodic under $P \xi^{-1} \psi^{-1}$ if it is ergodic under $P \xi^{-1}$.

This translates immediately into a result on stochastic processes. Define $Y= \left(\ldots, Y_{-1}, Y_{0}, Y_{1}, \ldots\right)$ in terms of $X$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
Y_{n}=\phi\left(\ldots, X_{n-1}, X_{n}, X_{n+1}, . .\right), \tag{36.29}
\end{equation*}
$$

that is to say, $Y(\omega)=\psi(X(\omega))=\psi \xi \omega$. Since $P \xi^{-1}$ is the distribution of $X, P \xi^{-1} \psi^{-1} =P(\psi \xi)^{-1}=P Y^{-1}$ is the distribution of $Y$ :

Theorem 36.4. If $X$ is stationary and ergodic, in particular if the $X_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed, then $Y$ as defined by (36.29) is stationary and ergodic.

This theorem fails if $Y$ is not defined in terms of $X$ in a time-invariant way-if the $\phi$ in (36.29) is not the same for all $n$ : If $\phi_{n}(x)=Z_{-n}(x)$ and $\phi$ is replaced by $\phi_{n}$ in (36.29), then $Y_{n} \equiv X_{0}$, in this case $Y$ happens to be stationary, but it is not ergodic if the distribution of $X_{0}$ does not concentrate at a single point.

Example 36.5. The autoregressive model. Let $\phi(x)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \beta^{k} Z_{-k}(x)$ on the set where the series converges, and take $\phi(x)=0$ elsewhere. Suppose that $|\beta|<1$ and that the $X_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed with finite second moments. Then by Theorem 22.6, $Y_{n}=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \beta^{k} X_{n-k}$ converges with probability 1 , and by Theorem 36.4, the process $Y$ is ergodic. Note that $Y_{n+1}=\beta Y_{n}+X_{n+1}$ and that $X_{n+1}$ is independent of $Y_{n}$. This is the linear autoregressive model of order 1.

## The Hewitt-Savage Theorem *

Change notation: Let ( $R^{\infty}, \mathscr{R}^{\infty}$ ) be the product space with $\{1,2, \ldots\}$ ) as the index set, the space of one-sided sequences. Let $P$ be a probability measure on $\mathscr{R}^{\infty}$. If the coordinate variables $Z_{n}$ are independent under $P$, then by Theorem 22.3, $P(A)$ is 0 or 1 for each $A$ in the tail $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{T}$. If the $Z_{n}$ are also identically distributed under $P$, a stronger result holds.

Let $\mathscr{L}_{n}$ be the class of $\mathscr{R}^{\infty}$-sets $A$ that are invariant under permutations of the first $n$ coordinates: if $\pi$ is a permutation of $\{1, \ldots, n\}$, then $x$ lies in $A$ if and only if $\left(Z_{\pi 1}(x), \ldots, Z_{\pi n}(x), Z_{n+1}(x), \ldots\right)$ does. Then $\mathscr{L}_{n}$ is a $\sigma$-field. Let $\mathscr{L}=\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} \mathscr{L}_{n}$ be the $\sigma$-field of $\mathscr{R}^{\infty}$-sets invariant under all finite permutations of coordinates. Then $\mathscr{L}$ is larger than $\mathscr{T}$, since, for example, the $x$-set where $\sum_{k=1}^{n} Z_{k}(x)>c_{n}$ infinitely often lies in $\mathscr{P}$ but not in $\mathscr{T}$.

The Hewitt-Savage theorem is a zero-one law for $\mathscr{L}$ in the independent, identically distributed case.

Theorem 36.5. If the $Z_{n}$ are independent and identically distributed under $P$, then $P(A)$ is 0 or 1 for each $A$ in $\mathscr{L}$.

Proof. By Corollary 1 to Theorem 11.4, there are for given $A$ and $\epsilon$ an $n$ and a set $U=\left[\left(Z_{1}, \ldots, Z_{n}\right) \in H\right]\left(H \in \mathscr{R}^{n}\right)$ such that $P(A \Delta U)<\epsilon$. Let $V= \left[\left(Z_{n+1}, \ldots, Z_{2 n}\right) \in H\right]$. If the $Z_{k}$ are independent and identically distributed, then $P(A \Delta U)$ is the same as

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left(\left[\left(Z_{n+1}, \ldots, Z_{2 n}, Z_{1}, \ldots, Z_{n}, Z_{2 n+1}, Z_{2 n+2}, . .\right)\right.\right. & \in A] \\
\Delta\left[\left(Z_{n+1}, \ldots, Z_{2 n}, Z_{1}, \ldots, Z_{n}\right)\right. & \left.\left.\in H \times R^{n}\right]\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

But if $A \in \mathscr{L}_{2 n}$, this is in turn the same as $P(A \Delta V)$. Therefore, $P(A \Delta U)=P(A \Delta V)$.
But then, $P(A \Delta V)<\epsilon$ and $P(A \Delta(U \cap V)) \leq P(A \Delta U)+P(A \Delta V)<2 \epsilon$.
