Since $U$ and $V$ have the same probability and are independent, it follows that $P(A)$ is within $\epsilon$ of $P(U)$ and hence $P^{2}(A)$ is within $2 \epsilon$ of $P^{2}(U)=P(U) P(V)=P(U \cap V)$, which is in turn within $2 \epsilon$ of $P(A)$. Therefore, $\left|P^{2}(A)-P(A)\right|<4 \epsilon$ for all $\epsilon$, and so $P(A)$ must be 0 or 1 . $\square$

## PROBLEMS

36.1. $\uparrow$ Suppose that $\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$ is a stochastic process on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ and $A \in \mathscr{F}$. Show that there is a countable subset $S$ of $T$ for which $P\left[A \| X_{t}, t \in T\right]= P\left[A \| X_{t}, t \in S\right]$ with probability 1 . Replace $A$ by a random variable and prove a similar result.
36.2. Let $T$ be arbitrary and let $K(s, t)$ be a real function over $T \times T$. Suppose that $K$ is symmetric in the sense that $X(s, t)=K(t, s)$ and nonnegative-definite in the sense that $\sum_{i, j=1}^{k} K\left(t_{i}, t_{j}\right) x_{i} x_{j} \geq 0$ for $k \geq 1, t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ in $T$, and $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}$ real. Show that there exists a process $\left[X_{i}: t \in T\right]$ for which $\left(X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}\right)$ has the centered normal distribution with covariances $K\left(t_{i}, t_{j}\right), i, j=1, \ldots, k$.

[^4]36.3. Let $L$ be a Borel set on the line, let $\mathscr{L}$ consist of the Borel subsets of $L$, and let $L^{T}$ consist of all maps from $T$ into $L$. Define the appropriate notion of cylinder, and let $\mathscr{L}^{T}$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by the cylinders. State a version of Theorem 36.1 for $\left(L^{T}, \mathscr{L}^{T}\right)$. Assume $T$ countable, and prove this theorem not by imitating the previous proof but by observing that $L^{T}$ is a subset of $R^{T}$ and lies in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$.
36.4. Suppose that the random variables $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ assume the values 0 and 1 and $P\left[X_{n}=1\right.$ i.o. $]=1$. Let $\mu$ be the distribution over ( 0,1 ] of $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} X_{n} / 2^{n}$. Show that on the unit interval with the measure $\mu$, the digits of the nonterminating dyadic expansion form a stochastic process with the same finite-dimensional distributions as $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$.
36.5. $36.3 \uparrow$ There is an infinite-dimensional version of Fubini's theorem. In the construction in Problem 36.3, let $L=I=(0,1), T=\{1,2, \ldots\}$, let $\mathscr{I}$ consist of the Borel subsets of $I$, and suppose that each $k$-dimensional distribution is the $k$-fold product of Lebesgue measure over the unit interval. Then $I^{T}$ is a countable product of copies of ( 0,1 ), its elements are sequences $x=\left(x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right)$ of points of $(0,1)$, and Kolmogorov's theorem ensures the existence on $\left(I^{T}, \mathscr{I}^{T}\right)$ of a product probability measure $\pi: \pi\left[x: x_{i} \leq \alpha_{i}, i \leq n\right]=\alpha_{1} \cdots \alpha_{n}$ for $0 \leq \alpha_{i} \leq 1$. Let $I^{n}$ denote the $n$-dimensional unit cube.
(a) Define $\psi: I^{n} \times I^{T} \rightarrow I^{T}$ by
$$
\psi\left(\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right),\left(y_{1}, y_{2}, \ldots\right)\right)=\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}, y_{1}, y_{2}, \ldots\right) .
$$

Show that $\psi$ is measurable $\mathscr{I}^{n} \times \mathscr{I}^{T} / \mathscr{I}^{T}$ and $\psi^{-1}$ is measurable $\mathscr{I}^{T} / \mathscr{I}^{n} \times \mathscr{I}^{T}$. Show that $\psi^{-1}\left(\lambda_{n} \times \pi\right)=\pi$, where $\lambda_{n}$ is $n$-dimensional Lebesgue measure restricted to $I^{n}$.
(b) Let $f$ be a furction measurable $\mathscr{I}^{T}$ and, for simplicity, bounded. Define

$$
f_{n}\left(x_{n+1}, x_{n+2}, \ldots\right)=\int_{0}^{1} \cdots \int_{0}^{1} f\left(y_{1}, \ldots, y_{n}, x_{n+1}, \ldots\right) d y_{1} \cdots d y_{n}
$$

in other words, integrate out the coordinates one by one. Show by Problem 34.18 , martingale theory, and the zero-one law that

$$
\begin{equation*}
f_{n}\left(x_{n+1}, x_{n+2}, \ldots\right) \rightarrow \int_{I} f(y) \pi(d y) \tag{36.30}
\end{equation*}
$$

except for $x$ in a set of $\pi$-measure 0 .
(c) Adopting the point of view of part (a), let $g_{n}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right)$ be the result of integrating the variable ( $y_{n+1}, y_{n+2}, \ldots$ ) out (with respect to $\pi$ ) from $f\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}, y_{n+1}, \ldots\right)$. This may suggestively be written as

$$
g_{n}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right)=\int_{0}^{1} \int_{0}^{1} \cdots f\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}, y_{n+1}, y_{n+2}, \ldots\right) d y_{n+1} d y_{n+2} \cdots
$$

Show that $g_{n}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}\right) \rightarrow f\left(x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots\right)$ except for $x$ in a set of $\pi$-measure 0 .
36.6. (a) Let $T$ be an interval of the line. Show that $\mathscr{R}^{T}$ fails to contain the sets of: linear functions, polynomials, constants, nondecreasing functions, functions of bounded variation, differentiable functions, analytic functions, functions con-
tinuous at a fixed $t_{0}$, Borel measurable functions. Show that it fails to contain the set of functions that: vanish somewhere in $T$, satisfy $x(s)<x(t)$ for some pair with $s<t$, have a local maximum anywhere, fail to have a local maximum. (b) Let $C$ be the set of continuous functions on $T=[0, \infty)$. Show that $A \in \mathscr{R}^{r}$ and $A \subset C$ imply that $A=\varnothing$. Show, on the other hand, that $A \in \mathscr{R}^{T}$ and $C \subset A$ do not imply that $A=R^{T}$.
36.7. Not all systems of finite-dimensional distributions can be realized by stochastic processes for which $\Omega$ is the unit interval. Show that there is on the unit interval with Lebesgue measure no process $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ for which the $X_{t}$ are independent and assume the values 0 and 1 with probability $\frac{1}{2}$ each. Compare Problem 1.1.
36.8. Here is an application of the existence theorem in which $T$ is not a subset of the line. Let ( $N, \mathscr{N}, \nu$ ) be a measure space, and take $T$ to consist of the $\mathscr{N}$-sets of finite $\nu$-measure. The problem is to construct a generalized Poisson process, a stochastic process $\left[X_{A}: A \in T\right]$ such that (i) $X_{A}$ has the Poisson distribution with mean $\nu(A)$ and (ii) $X_{A_{1}}, \ldots, X_{A_{n}}$ are independent if $A_{1}, \ldots, A_{n}$ are disjoint. Hint: To define the finite-dimensional distributions, generalize this construction: For $A, B$ in $T$, consider independent random variables $Y_{1}, Y_{2}, Y_{3}$ having Poisson distributions with means $\nu\left(A \cap B^{c}\right), \nu(A \cap B), \nu\left(A^{c} \cap B\right)$, take $\mu_{A \cdot B}$ to be the distribution of $\left(Y_{1}+Y_{2}, Y_{2}+Y_{3}\right)$.

## SECTION 37. BROWNIAN MOTION

## Definition

A Brownian motion or Wiener process is a stochastic process $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$, on some ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ), with these three properties:
(i) The process starts at 0 :

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[W_{0}=0\right]=1 \tag{37.1}
\end{equation*}
$$

(ii) The increments are independent: If

$$
\begin{equation*}
0 \leq t_{0} \leq t_{1} \leq \cdots \leq t_{k}, \tag{37.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[W_{t_{i}}-W_{t_{i-1}} \in H_{i}, i \leq k\right]=\prod_{i \leq k} P\left[W_{t_{i}}-W_{t_{i-1}} \in H_{i}\right] . \tag{37.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

(iii) For $0 \leq s<t$ the increment $W_{t}-W_{s}$ is normally distributed with mean 0 and variance $t-s$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[W_{\imath}-W_{s} \in H\right]=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi(t-s)}} \int_{H}^{e^{-x^{2} / 2(t-s)}} d x \tag{37.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

The existence of such processes will be proved.
Imagine suspended in a fluid a particle bombarded by molecules in thermal motion. The particle will perform a seemingly random movement
first described by the nineteenth-century botanist Robert Brown. Consider a single component of this motion-imagine it projected on a vertical axis-and denote by $W_{t}$ the height at time $t$ of the particle above a fixed horizontal plane. Condition (i) is merely a convention: the particle starts at 0 . Condition (ii) reflects a kind of lack of memory. The displacements $W_{t_{1}}-W_{t_{0}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k-1}} -W_{t_{k-2}}$ the particle undergoes during the intervals $\left[t_{0}, t_{1}\right], \ldots,\left[t_{k-2}, t_{k-1}\right]$ in no way influence the displacement $W_{t_{k}}-W_{t_{k-1}}$ it undergoes during $\left[t_{k-1}, t_{k}\right]$. Although the future behavior of the particle depends on its present position, it does not depend on how the particle got there. As for (iii), that $W_{t}-W_{s}$ has mean 0 reflects the fact that the particle is as likely to go up as to go down-there is no drift. The variance grows as the length of the interval $[s, t]$; the particle tends to wander away from its position at time $s$, and having done so suffers no force tending to restore it to that position. To Norbert Wiener are due the mathematical foundations of the theory of this kind of random motion.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-029.jpg?height=1318&width=1090&top_left_y=1151&top_left_x=365)
A Brownian motion path.

The increments of the Brownian motion process are stationary in the sense that the distribution of $W_{t}-W_{s}$ depends only on the difference $t-s$. Since $W_{0}=0$, the distribution of these increments is described by saying that
$W_{t}$ is normally distributed with mean 0 and variance $t$. This implies (37.1). If $0 \leq s \leq t$, then by the independence of the increments, $E\left[W_{s} W_{t}\right]=E\left[\left(W_{s}\left(W_{t}\right.\right.\right. \left.\left.-W_{s}\right)\right]+E\left[W_{s}^{2}\right]=E\left[W_{s}\right] E\left[W_{t}-W_{s}\right]+E\left[W_{s}^{2}\right]=s$. This specifies all the means, variances, and covariances:

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[W_{t}\right]=0, \quad E\left[W_{t}^{2}\right]=t, \quad E\left[W_{s} W_{t}\right]=\min \{s, t\} . \tag{37.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$, the joint density of ( $W_{t_{1}}, W_{t_{2}}-W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}-W_{t_{k-1}}$ ) is by (20.25) the product of the corresponding normal densities. By the Jacobian formula (20.20), $\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right)$ has density

$$
\begin{equation*}
f_{i_{i}} \quad \iota_{k}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=\prod_{i=1}^{k} \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi\left(t_{i}-t_{i-1}\right)}} \exp \left[-\frac{\left(x_{i}-x_{i-1}\right)^{2}}{2\left(t_{i}-t_{i-1}\right)}\right], \tag{37.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $t_{0}=x_{0}=0$.
Sometimes $W_{1}$ will be denoted $W(t)$, and its value at $\omega$ will be $W(t, \omega)$. The nature of the path functions $W(\cdot, \omega)$ will be of great importance.

The existence of the Brownian motion process follows from Kolmogorov's theorem. For $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$ let $\mu_{t_{1}}$. $t_{k}$ be the distribution in $R^{k}$ with density (37.6). To put it another way, let $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$ be the distribution of $\left(S_{1}, \ldots, S_{k}\right)$, where $S_{i}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{i}$ and where $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{k}$ are independent, normally distributed random variables with mean 0 and variances $t_{1}, t_{2}-t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}-t_{k-1}$. If $g\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)=\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{i-1}, x_{i+1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)$, then $g\left(S_{1}, \ldots, S_{k}\right)=\left(S_{1}, \ldots, S_{i-1}, S_{i+1}, \ldots, S_{k}\right)$ has the distribution prescribed for $\mu_{t_{1}} \quad t_{i-1} t_{i+1}, t_{k}$. This is because $X_{i}+X_{i+1}$ is normally distributed with mean 0 and variance $t_{i+1}-t_{i-1}$; see Example 20.6. Therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mu_{t_{1}} \quad, t_{i-1} t_{i+1}, \quad r_{k}=\mu_{t_{1}} \quad r_{k} g^{-1} \tag{37.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

The $\mu_{r_{1}} r_{k}$ defined in this way for increasing, positive $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ thus satisfy the conditions for Kolmogorov's existence theorem as modified in Example 36.4; (37.7) is the same thing as (36.19). Therefore, there does exist a process $\left[W_{t}: t>0\right]$ corresponding to the $\mu_{t_{t}, t_{k}}$. Taking $W_{t}=0$ for $t=0$ shows that there exists on some $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ a process $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ with the finite-dimensional distributions specified by the conditions (i), (ii), and (iii).

## Continuity of Paths

If the Brownian motion process is to represent the motion of a particle, it is natural to require that the path functions $W(\cdot, \omega)$ be continuous. But Kolmogorov's theorem does not guarantee continuity. Indeed, for $T=[0, \infty)$, the space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}$ ) in the proof of Kolmogorov's theorem is ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ), and as shown in the last section, the set of continuous functions does not lie in $\mathscr{R}^{T}$.

A special construction gets around this difficulty. The idea is to use for dyadic rational $t$ the random variables $W_{1}$ as already defined and then to redefine the other $W_{t}$ in such a way as to ensure continuity. To carry this through requires proving that with probability 1 the sample path is uniformly continuous for dyadic rational arguments in bounded intervals.

Fix a space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) and on it a process $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ having the finitedimensional distributions prescribed for Brownian motion. Let $D$ be the set of nonnegative dyadic rationals, let $I_{n k}=\left[k 2^{-n},(k+2) 2^{-n}\right]$, and put

$$
\begin{align*}
M_{n k}(\omega) & =\sup _{r \in I_{n k} \cap D}\left|W(r, \omega)-W\left(k 2^{-n}, \omega\right)\right|  \tag{37.8}\\
M_{n}(\omega) & =\max _{0 \leq k<n 2^{n}} M_{n k}(\omega) .
\end{align*}
$$

Suppose it is shown that $\sum P\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right]$ converges. The first Borel-Cantelli lemma will then imply that $B=\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right.$ i.o. $]$ has probability 0 . But suppose $\omega$ lies outside $B$. Then for every $t$ and $\epsilon$ there exists an $n$ such that $t<n, 2 n^{-1}<\epsilon$, and $M_{n}(\omega) \leq n^{-1}$. Take $\delta=2^{-n}$. Suppose that $r$ and $r^{\prime}$ are dyadic rationals in $[0, t]$ and $\left|r-r^{\prime}\right|<\delta$. Then $r$ and $r^{\prime}$ must for some $k<n 2^{n}$ lie in a common interval $I_{n k}$ (length $2 \times 2^{-n}$ ), in which case $\mid W(r, \omega) -W\left(r^{\prime}, \omega\right) \mid \leq 2 M_{n k}(\omega) \leq 2 M_{n}(\omega) \leq 2 n^{-1}<\epsilon$. Therefore, $\omega \notin B$ implies that $W(r, \omega)$ is for every $t$ uniformly continuous as $r$ ranges over the dyadic rationals in $[0, t]$, and hence it will have a continuous extension to $[0, \infty)$.

To prove $\sum P\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right]<\infty$, use Etemadi's maximal inequality (22.10), which applies because of the independence of the increments. This, together with Markov's inequality, gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left[\max _{i \leq 2^{m}}\left|W\left(t+\delta i 2^{-m}\right)-W(t)\right|>\alpha\right] \\
& \quad \leq 3 \max _{i \leq 2^{m}} P\left[\left|W\left(t+\delta i 2^{-m}\right)-W(t)\right| \geq \alpha / 3\right] \\
& \quad \leq \frac{3}{(\alpha / 3)^{4}} E\left[(W(t+\delta)-W(t))^{4}\right]=\frac{3^{5}}{\alpha^{4}} \cdot 3 \delta^{2}=\frac{K \delta^{2}}{\alpha^{4}}
\end{aligned}
$$

(see (21.7) for the moments of the normal distribution). The sets on the left here increase with $m$, and letting $m \rightarrow \infty$ leads to

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\sup _{\substack{0 \leq r \leq 1 \\ r \in D}}|W(t+r \delta)-W(t)|>\alpha\right] \leq \frac{K \delta^{2}}{\alpha^{4}} . \tag{37.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

Therefore,

$$
P\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right] \leq n 2^{n} \frac{K\left(2 \times 2^{-n}\right)^{2}}{\left(n^{-1}\right)^{4}}=\frac{4 K n^{5}}{2^{n}}
$$

and $\sum P\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right]$ does converge.

Therefore, there exists a measurable set $B$ such that $P(B)=0$ and such that for $\omega$ outside $B, W(r, \omega)$ is uniformly continuous as $r$ ranges over the dyadic rationals in any bounded interval. If $\omega \notin B$ and $r$ decreases to $t$ through dyadic rational values, then $W(r, \omega)$ has the Cauchy property and hence converges. Put

$$
W_{t}^{\prime}(\omega)=W^{\prime}(t, \omega)= \begin{cases}\lim _{r \downarrow t} W(r, \omega) & \text { if } \omega \notin B, \\ 0 & \text { if } \omega \in B,\end{cases}
$$

where $r$ decreases to $t$ through the set $D$ of dyadic rationals. By construction, $W^{\prime}(t, \omega)$ is continuous in $t$ for each $\omega$ in $\Omega$. If $\omega \notin B$, then $W(r, \omega)= W^{\prime}(r, \omega)$ for dyadic rationals, and $W^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is the continuous extension to all of $[0, \infty)$.

The next thing is to show that the $W_{t}^{\prime}$ have the same joint distributions as the $W_{t}$. It is convenient to prove this by a lemma which will be used again further on.

Lemma 1. Let $X_{n}$ and $X$ be $k$-dimensional random vectors, and let $F_{n}(x)$ be the distribution function of $X_{n}$. If $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 and $F_{n}(x) \rightarrow F(x)$ for all $x$, then $F(x)$ is the distribution function of $X$.

Proof. ${ }^{\dagger}$ Let $X$ have distribution function $H$. By two applications of Theorem 4.1, if $h>0$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
F\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) & =\lim \sup _{n} F_{n}\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) \leq H\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right) \\
& \leq \lim \inf _{n} F_{n}\left(x_{1}+h, \ldots, x_{k}+h\right) \\
& =F\left(x_{1}+h, \ldots, x_{k}+h\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

It follows by continuity from above that $F$ and $H$ agree.
Now, for $0<t_{1}<\cdots<t_{k}$, choose dyadic rationals $r_{i}(n)$ decreasing to the $t_{i}$. Apply Lemma 1 with ( $W_{r_{1}(n)}, \ldots, W_{r_{k}(n)}$ ) and ( $W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}$ ) in the roles of $X_{n}$ and $X$, and with the distribution function with density (37.6) in the role of $F$. Since (37.6) is continuous in the $t_{i}$, it follows by Scheffé's theorem that $F_{n}(x) \rightarrow F(x)$, and by construction $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 . By the lemma, ( $W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}$ ) has distribution function $F$, which of course is also the distribution function of ( $W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}$ ).

Thus $\left[W_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a stochastic process, on the same probability space as $\left[W_{1}, t \geq 0\right]$, which has the finite-dimensional distributions required for Brownian motion and moreover has a continuous sample path $W^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ for every $\omega$.

[^5]By enlarging the set $B$ in the definition of $W_{t}^{\prime}(\omega)$ to include all the $\omega$ for which $W(0, \omega) \neq 0$, one can also ensure that $W^{\prime}(0, \omega)=0$. Now discard the original random variables $W_{t}$ and relabel $W_{t}^{\prime}$ as $W_{t}$. The new $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a stochastic process satisfying conditions (i), (ii), and (iii) for Brownian motion and this one as well:
(iv) For each $\omega, W(t, \omega)$ is continuous in $t$ and $W(0, \omega)=0$.

From now on, by a Brownian motion will be meant a process satisfying (iv) as well as (i), (ii), and (iii). What has been proved is this:

Theorem 37.1. There exist processes $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ satisfying conditions (i), (ii), (iii), and (iv)-Brownian motion processes.

In the construction above, $W_{r}$ for dyadic $r$ was used to define $W_{t}$ in general. For that reason it suffices to apply Kolmogorov's theorem for a countable index set. By the second proof of that theorem, the space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) can be taken as the unit interval with Lebesgue measure.

The next section treats a general scheme for dealing with path-function questions by in effect replacing an uncountable time set by a countable one.

## Measurable Processes

Let $T$ be a Borel set on the line, let $\left[X_{t}: t \in T\right]$ be a stochastic process on an ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ), and consider the mapping

$$
\begin{equation*}
(t, \omega) \rightarrow X_{t}(\omega)=X(t, \omega) \tag{37.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

carrying $T \times \Omega$ into $R^{1}$. Let $\mathscr{T}$ be the $\sigma$-field of Borel subsets of $T$. The process is said to be measurable if the mapping (37.10) is measurable $\mathscr{T} \times \mathscr{F} / \mathscr{R}^{1}$.

In the presence of measurability, each sample path $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is measurable $\mathscr{T}$ by Theorem 18.1. Then, for example, $\int_{a}^{b} \varphi(X(t, \omega)) d t$ makes sense if $(a, b) \subset T$ and $\varphi$ is a Borel function, and by Fubini's theorem

$$
E\left[\int_{a}^{b} \varphi(X(t, \cdot)) d t\right]=\int_{a}^{b} E\left[\varphi\left(X_{1}\right)\right] d t \quad \text { if } \int_{a}^{b} E\left[\left|\varphi\left(X_{1}\right)\right|\right] d t<\infty .
$$

Hence the usefulness of this result:

Theorem 37.2. Brownian motion is measurable.

Proof. If

$$
\begin{array}{r}
W^{(n)}(t, \omega)=W\left(k 2^{-n}, \omega\right) \quad \text { for } \quad k 2^{-n} \leq t<(k+1) 2^{-n} \\
k=0,1,2, \ldots,
\end{array}
$$

then the mapping $(t, \omega) \rightarrow W^{(n)}(t, \omega)$ is measurable $\mathscr{T} \times \mathscr{F}$. But by the continuity of the sample paths, this mapping converges to the mapping (37.10) pointwise (for every $(t, \omega)$ ), and so by Theorem 13.4(ii) the latter mapping is also measurable $\mathscr{T} \times \mathscr{F} / \mathscr{R}^{1}$. $\square$

## Irregularity of Brownian Motion Paths

Starting with a Brownian motion $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ define

$$
\begin{equation*}
W_{t}^{\prime}(\omega)=c^{-1} W_{c^{2} t}(\omega), \tag{37.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $c>0$. Since $t \rightarrow c^{2} t$ is an increasing function, it is easy to see that the process $\left[W_{1}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ has independent increments. Moreover, $W_{1}^{\prime}-W_{s}^{\prime}= c^{-1}\left(W_{c^{2},}-W_{c^{2} s}\right)$, and for $s \leq t$ this is normally distributed with mean 0 and variance $c^{-2}\left(c^{2} t-c^{2} s\right)=t-s$. Since the paths $W^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ all start from 0 and are continuous, $\left[W_{1}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is another Brownian motion. In (37.11) the time scale is contracted by the factor $c^{2}$, but the other scale only by the factor $c$.

That the transformation (37.11) preserves the properties of Brownian motion implies that the paths, although continuous, must be highly irregular. It seems intuitively clear that for $c$ large enough the path $W(\cdot, \omega)$ must with probability nearly 1 have somewhere in the time interval $[0, c]$ a chord with slope exceeding, say, 1 . But then $W^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ has in $\left[0, c^{-1}\right]$ a chord with slope exceeding $c$. Since the $W_{t}^{\prime}$ are distributed as the $W_{t}$, this makes it plausible that $W(\cdot, \omega)$ must in arbitrarily small intervals $[0, \delta]$ have chords with arbitrarily great slopes, which in turn makes it plausible that $W(\cdot, \omega)$ cannet be differentiable at 0 . More generally, mild irregularities in the path will become ever more extreme under the transformation (37.11) with ever larger values of $c$. It is shown below that, in fact, the paths are with probability 1 nowhere differentiable.

Also interesting in this connection is the transformation

$$
W_{1}^{\prime \prime}(\omega)= \begin{cases}t W_{1 / 1}(\omega) & \text { if } t>0,  \tag{37.12}\\ 0 & \text { if } t=0 .\end{cases}
$$

Again it is easily checked that the increments are independent and normally distributed with the means and variances appropriate to Brownian motion. Moreover, the path $W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous except possibly at $t=0$. But (37.9) holds with $W_{s}^{\prime \prime}$ in place of $W_{s}$ because it depends only on the finite-dimensional distributions, and by the continuity of $W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ over ( $0, \infty$ ) the supremum is the same if not restricted to dyadic rationals. Therefore, $P\left[\sup _{s \leq n^{-3}}\left|W_{s}^{\prime \prime}\right|>n^{-1}\right] \leq K / n^{2}$, and it follows by the first Borel-Cantelli lemma that $W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous also at 0 for $\omega$ outside a set $M$ of probability 0 . For $\omega \in M$, redefine $W^{\prime \prime}(t, \omega) \equiv 0$; then $\left[W_{1}^{\prime \prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a Brownian motion and (37.12) holds with probability 1.

The behavior of $W(\cdot, \omega)$ near 0 can be studied through the behavior of $W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ near $\infty$ and vice versa. Since $\left(W_{t}^{\prime \prime}-W_{0}^{\prime \prime}\right) / t=W_{1 /,}, W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ cannot have a derivative at 0 if $W(\cdot, \omega)$ has no limit at $\infty$. Now, in fact,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\inf _{n} W_{n}=-\infty, \quad \sup _{n} W_{n}=+\infty \tag{37.13}
\end{equation*}
$$

with probability 1 . To prove this, note that $W_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$, where the $X_{k}=W_{k}-W_{k-1}$ are independent. Consider

$$
\left[\sup _{n} W_{n}<\infty\right]=\bigcup_{u=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{m=1}^{\infty}\left[\max _{i \leq m} W_{i} \leq u\right] ;
$$

this is a tail set and hence by the zero-one law has probability 0 or 1 . Now $-X_{1},-X_{2}, \ldots$ have the same joint distributions as $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$, and so this event has the same probability as

$$
\left[\inf _{n} W_{n}>-\infty\right]=\bigcup_{u=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{m=1}^{\infty}\left[\max _{i \leq m}\left(-W_{i}\right) \leq u\right] .
$$

If these two sets have probability 1 , so has $\left[\sup _{n}\left|W_{n}\right|<\infty\right]$, so that $P\left[\sup _{n}\left|W_{n}\right|\right. <x]>0$ for some $x$. But $P\left[\left|W_{n}\right|<x\right]=P\left[\left|W_{1}\right|<x / n^{1 / 2}\right] \rightarrow 0$. This proves (37.13).

Since (37.13) holds with probability $1, W^{\prime \prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ has with probability 1 upper and lower right derivatives of $+\infty$ and $-\infty$ at $t=0$. The same must be true of every Brownian motion. A similar argument shows that, for each fixed $t, W(\cdot, \omega)$ is nondifferentiable at $t$ with probability 1 . In fact, $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is nowhere differentiable:

Theorem 37.3. For $\omega$ outside a set of probability $0, W(\cdot, \omega)$ is nowhere differentiable.

Proof. The proof is direct-makes no use of the transformations (37.11) and (37.12). Let

$$
\begin{align*}
X_{n k}=\max \left\{\left|W\left(\frac{k+1}{2^{n}}\right)-W\left(\frac{k}{2^{n}}\right)\right|,\left|W\left(\frac{k+2}{2^{n}}\right)-W\left(\frac{k+1}{2^{n}}\right)\right|,\right.  \tag{37.14}\\
\left.\left|W\left(\frac{k+3}{2^{n}}\right)-W\left(\frac{k+2}{2^{n}}\right)\right|\right\} .
\end{align*}
$$

By independence and the fact that the differences here have the distribution of $2^{-n / 2} W_{1}, P\left[X_{n k} \leq \epsilon\right]=P^{3}\left[\left|W_{1}\right| \leq 2^{n / 2} \epsilon\right]$; since the standard normal density is bounded by $1, P\left[X_{n k} \leq \epsilon\right] \leq\left(2 \times 2^{n / 2} \epsilon\right)^{3}$. If $Y_{n}=\min _{k \leq n 2^{n}} X_{n k}$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[Y_{n} \leq \epsilon\right] \leq n 2^{n}\left(2 \times 2^{n / 2} \epsilon\right)^{3} . \tag{37.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

Consider now the upper and lower right-hand derivatives

$$
\begin{aligned}
& D^{W}(t, \omega)=\underset{h \downarrow 0}{\limsup } \frac{W(t+h, \omega)-W(t, \omega)}{h} \\
& D_{W}(t, \omega)=\underset{h \downarrow 0}{\liminf } \frac{W(t+h, \omega)-W(t, \omega)}{h}
\end{aligned}
$$

Define $E$ (not necessarily in $\mathscr{F}$ ) as the set of $\omega$ such that $D^{W}(t, \omega)$ and $D_{W}(t, \omega)$ are both finite for some value of $t$. Suppose that $\omega$ lies in $E$, and suppose specifically that

$$
-K<D_{W}(t, \omega) \leq D^{W}(t, \omega)<K .
$$

There exists a positive $\delta$ (depending on $\omega, t$, and $K$ ) such that $t \leq s \leq t+\delta$ implies $|W(s, \omega)-W(t, \omega)| \leq K|s-t|$. If $n$ exceeds some $n_{0}$ (depending on $\delta$, $K$, and $t$ ), then

$$
4 \times 2^{-n}<\delta, \quad 8 K<n, \quad n>t .
$$

Given such an $n$, choose $k$ so that $(k-1) 2^{-n} \leq t<k 2^{-n}$. Then $\left|i 2^{-n}-t\right|<\delta$ for $i=k, k+1, k+2, k+3$, and therefore $X_{n k}(\omega) \leq 2 K\left(4 \times 2^{-n}\right)<n 2^{-n}$. Since $k-1 \leq t 2^{n}<n 2^{n}, Y_{n}(\omega) \leq n 2^{-n}$.

What has been shown is that if $\omega$ lies in $E$, then $\omega$ lies in $A_{n}=\left[Y_{n} \leq n 2^{-n}\right]$ for all sufficiently large $n: E \subset \lim \inf _{n} A_{n}$. By (37.15),

$$
P\left(A_{n}\right) \leq n 2^{n}\left(2 \times 2^{n / 2} \times n 2^{-n}\right)^{3} \rightarrow 0 .
$$

By Theorem 4.1, $\liminf _{n} A_{n}$ has probability 0 , and outside this set $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is nowhere differentiable-in fact, nowhere does it have finite upper and lower right-hand derivatives. (Similarly, outside a set of probability 0 , nowhere does $W(\cdot, \omega)$ have finite upper and lower left-hand derivatives.) $\square$

If $A$ is the set of $\omega$ for which $W(\cdot, \omega)$ has a derivative somewhere, what has been shown is that $A \subset B$ for a measurable $B$ such that $P(B)=0$; $P(A)=0$ if $A$ is measurable, but this has not been proved. To avoid such problems in the study of continuous-time processes, it is convenient to work in a complete probability space. The space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) is complete (see p. 44) if $A \subset B, B \in \mathscr{F}$, and $P(B)=0$ together imply that $A \in \mathscr{F}$ (and then, of course, $P(A)=0$ ). If the space is not already complete, it is possible to enlarge $\mathscr{F}$ to a new $\sigma$-field and extend $P$ to it in such a way that the new space is complete. The following assumption therefore entails no loss of generality: For the rest of this section the space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) on which the Brownian motion is defined is assumed complete. Theorem 37.3 now becomes: $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is with probability 1 nowhere differentiable.

A nowhere-differentiable path represents the motion of a particle that at no time has a velocity. Since a function of bounded variation is differentiable almost everywhere (Section 31), $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is of unbounded variation with probability 1. Such a path represents the motion of a particle that in its wanderings back and forth travels an infinite distance in finite time. The Brownian motion model thus does not in its fine structure represent physical reality. The irregularity of the Brownian motion paths is of considerable mathematical interest, however. A continuous, nowhere-differentiable function is regarded as pathological, or used to be, but from the Brownian-motion point of view such functions are the rule not the exception. ${ }^{\dagger}$

The set of zeros of the Brownian motion is also interesting. By property (iv), $t=0$ is a zero of $W(\cdot, \omega)$ for each $\omega$. Now $\left[W_{1}^{\prime \prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ as defined by (37.12) is another Brownian motion, and so by (37.13) the sequence $\left\{W_{n}^{\prime \prime \prime}:\right. n=1,2, \ldots\}=\left\{n W_{1 / n}: n=1,2, \ldots\right\}$ has supremum $+\infty$ and infimum $-\infty$ for $\omega$ outside a set of probability 0 ; for such an $\omega, W(\cdot, \omega)$ changes sign infinitely often near 0 and hence by continuity has zeros arbitrarily near 0 . Let $Z(\omega)$ denote the set of zeros of $W(\cdot, \omega)$. What has just been shown is that $0 \in Z(\omega)$ for each $\omega$ and that 0 is with probability 1 a limit of positive points in $Z(\omega)$. From (37.13) it also follows that $Z(\omega)$ is with probability 1 unbounded above. More is true:

Theorem 37.4. The set $Z(\omega)$ is with probability 1 perfect [A15], unbounded, nowhere dense, and of Lebesgue measure 0.

Proof. Since $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous, $Z(\omega)$ is closed for every $\omega$. Let $\lambda$ denote Lebesgue measure. Since Brownian motion is measurable (Theorem 37.2), Fubini's theorem applies:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{\Omega} \lambda(Z(\omega)) P(d \omega) & =(\lambda \times P)[(t, \omega): W(t, \omega)=0] \\
& =\int_{0}^{\infty} P[\omega: W(t, \omega)=0] d t=0
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus $\lambda(Z(\omega))=0$ with probability 1 .
If $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is nowhere differentiable, it cannot vanish throughout an interval $I$ and hence must by continuity be nonzero throughout some subinterval of $I$. By Theorem 37.3, then, $Z(\omega)$ is with probability 1 nowhere dense.

It remains to show that each point of $Z(\omega)$ is a limit of other points of $Z(\omega)$. As observed above, this is true of the point 0 of $Z(\omega)$. For the general point of $Z(\omega)$, a stopping-time argument is required. Fix $r \geq 0$ and let

[^6]$\tau(\omega)=\inf [t: t \geq r, W(t, \omega)=0]$; note that this set is nonempty with probability 1 by (37.13). Thus $\tau(\omega)$ is the first zero following $r$. Now
$$
\mid \omega: \tau(\omega) \leq t]=\left[\omega: \inf _{r \leq s \leq t}|W(s, \omega)|=0\right],
$$
and by continuity the infimum here is unchanged if $s$ is restricted to rationals. This shows that $\tau$ is a random variable and that
$$
[\omega . \tau(\omega) \leq t] \in \sigma\left[W_{u}: u \leq t\right] .
$$

A nonnegative random variable with this property is a stopping time.
To know the value of $\tau$ is to know at most the values of $W_{u}$ for $u \leq \tau$. Since the increments are independent, it therefore seems intuitively clear that the process

$$
\begin{equation*}
W_{t}^{*}(\omega)=W_{\tau(\omega)+t}(\omega)-W_{\tau(\omega)}(\omega)=W_{\tau(\omega)+t}(\omega), \quad t \geq 0, \tag{37.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

ought itself to be a Brownian motion. This is, in fact, true by the next result, Theorem 37.5. What is proved there is that the finite-dimensional distributions of $\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq 0\right]$ are the right ones for Brownian motion. The other properties are obvious: $W^{*}(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous and vanishes at 0 by construction, and the space on which $\left[W_{1}^{*} ; t \geq 0\right]$ is defined is complete because it is the original space $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$, assumed complete.

If $\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq 0\right]$ is indeed a Brownian motion, then, as observed above, for $\omega$ outside a set $B_{r}$ of probability 0 there is a positive sequence $\left\{t_{n}\right\}$ such that $t_{n} \rightarrow 0$ and $W^{*}\left(t_{n}, \omega\right)=0$. But then $W\left(\tau(\omega)+t_{n}, \omega\right)=0$, so that $\tau(\omega)$, a zero of $W(\cdot, \omega)$, is the limit of other larger zeros of $W(\cdot, \omega)$. Now $\tau(\omega)$ was the first zero following $r$. (There is a different stopping time $\tau$ for each $r$, but the notation does not show this.) If $B$ is the union of the $B_{r}$ for rational $r$, the first point of $Z(\omega)$ following $r$ is a limit of other, larger points of $Z(\omega)$. Suppose that $\omega \notin B$ and $t \in Z(\omega)$, where $t>0$; it is to be shown that $t$ is a limit of other points of $Z(\omega)$. If $t$ is the limit of smaller points of $Z(\omega)$, there is of course nothing to prove. Otherwise, there is a rational $r$ such that $r<t$ and $W(\cdot, \omega)$ does not vanish in $[r, t)$; but then, since $\omega \notin B_{r}, t$ is a limit of larger points $s$ that lie in $Z(\omega)$. This completes the proof of Theorem 37.4 under the provisional assumption that (37.16) is a Brownian motion. $\square$

## The Strong Markov Property

Fix $t_{0} \geq 0$ and put

$$
\begin{equation*}
W_{t}^{\prime}=W_{t_{0}+t}-W_{t_{0}}, \quad t \geq 0 . \tag{37.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

It is easily checked that $\left[W_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ has the finite-dimensional distributions
appropriate to Brownian motion. As the other properties are obvious, it is in fact a Brownian motion.

Let

$$
\begin{equation*}
\mathscr{F}_{i}=\sigma\left[W_{s}: s \leq t\right] . \tag{37.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

The random variables (37.17) are independent of $\mathscr{F}_{t_{0}}$. To see this, suppose that $0 \leq s_{1} \leq \cdots \leq s_{j} \leq t_{0}$ and $0 \leq t_{1} \leq \cdots \leq t_{k}$. Put $u_{i}=t_{0}+t_{i}$. Since the increments are independent, $\left(W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, W_{t_{2}}^{\prime}-W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}-W_{t_{k-1}}^{\prime}\right)=\left(W_{u_{1}}-\right. W_{t_{0}}, W_{u_{2}}-W_{u_{1}}, \ldots, W_{u_{k}}-W_{u_{k-1}}$ ) is independent of ( $W_{s}, W_{s_{2}}-W_{s_{1}}, \ldots, W_{s_{j}}- W_{s_{j-1}}$ ). But then ( $W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, W_{t_{2}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}$ ) is independent of ( $W_{s_{1}}, W_{s_{2}}, \ldots, W_{s_{j}}$ ). By Theorem 4.2, $\left(W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}\right)$ is independent of $\mathscr{F}_{t_{0}}$. Thus

$$
\begin{align*}
& P\left(\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}\right) \in H\right] \cap A\right)  \tag{37.19}\\
& \quad=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{\prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime}\right) \in H\right] P(A) \\
& \quad=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right] P(A), \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{t_{0}},
\end{align*}
$$

where the second equality follows because (37.17) is a Brownian motion. This holds for all $H$ in $\mathscr{R}^{k}$.

The problem now is to prove all this when $t_{0}$ is replaced by a stopping time $\tau$-a nonnegative random variable for which

$$
\begin{equation*}
[\omega: \tau(\omega) \leq t] \in \mathscr{F}_{l}, \quad t \geq 0 . \tag{37.20}
\end{equation*}
$$

It will be assumed that $\tau$ is finite, at least with probability 1 . Since $[\tau=t]= [\tau \leq t]-\bigcup_{n}\left[\tau \leq t-n^{-1}\right]$, (37.20) implies that

$$
\begin{equation*}
[\omega: \tau(\omega)=t] \in \mathscr{F}_{1}, \quad t \geq 0 . \tag{37.21}
\end{equation*}
$$

The conditions (37.20) and (37.21) are analogous to the conditions (7.18) and (35.18), which prevent prevision on the part of the gambler.

Now $\mathscr{F}_{t_{0}}$ contains the information on the past of the Brownian motion up to time $t_{0}$, and the analogue for $\tau$ is needed. Let $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ consist of all measurable sets $M$ for which

$$
\begin{equation*}
M \cap[\omega: \tau(\omega) \leq t] \in \mathscr{F}_{t} \tag{37.22}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $t$. (See (35.20) for the analogue in discrete time.) Note that $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ is a $\sigma$-field and $\tau$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$. Since $M \cap[\tau=t]=M \cap[\tau=t] \cap[\tau \leq t]$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
M \cap[\omega: \tau(\omega)=t] \in \mathscr{F}_{t} \tag{37.23}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $M$ in $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$. For example, $\tau=\inf \left[t: W_{1}=1\right]$ is a stopping time and $\left[\inf _{s \leq \tau} W_{s}>-1\right]$ is in $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$.

Theorem 37.5. Let $\tau$ be a stopping time, and put

$$
\begin{equation*}
W_{t}^{*}(\omega)=W_{\tau(\omega)+t}(\omega)-W_{\tau(\omega)}(\omega), \quad t \geq 0 \tag{37.24}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then $\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq\right]$ is a Brownian motion, and it is independent of $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$-that is, $\sigma\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq 0\right]$ is independent of $\mathscr{F}_{\mathrm{T}}:$

$$
\begin{align*}
& P\left(\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{*}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{*}\right) \in H\right] \cap M\right)  \tag{37.25}\\
& \quad=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{*}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{*}\right) \in H\right] P(M)=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right] P(M)
\end{align*}
$$

for $H$ in $\mathscr{R}^{k}$ and $M$ in $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$.
That the transformation (37.24) preserves Brownian motion is the strong Markov property. ${ }^{\dagger}$ Part of the conclusion is that the $W_{t}{ }^{*}$ are random variables.

Proof. Suppose first that $\tau$ has countable range $V$ and let $t_{0}$ be the general point of $V$. Since

$$
\left[\omega: W_{1}^{*}(\omega) \in H\right]=\bigcup_{t_{0} \in V}\left[\omega: W_{t_{0}+1}(\omega)-W_{t_{0}}(\omega) \in H, \tau(\omega)=t_{0}\right]
$$

$W_{t}^{*}$ is a random variable. Also,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left(\left[\left(W_{1_{1}}^{*}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{*}\right) \in H\right] \cap M\right) \\
& \quad=\sum_{t_{0} \in V} P\left(\left[\left(W_{1_{1}}^{*}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{*}\right) \in H\right] \cap M \cap\left[\tau=t_{0}\right]\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

If $M \in \mathscr{F}_{1}$, then $M \cap\left[\tau=t_{0}\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{t_{0}}$ by (37.23). Further, if $\tau=t_{0}$, then $W_{t}^{*}$ coincides with $W_{t}^{\prime}$ as defined by (37.17). Therefore, (37.19) reduces this last sum to

$$
\begin{gathered}
\sum_{t_{0} \in V} P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right] P\left(M \cap\left[\tau=t_{0}\right]\right) \\
=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right] P(M)
\end{gathered}
$$

This proves the first and third terms in ( 37.25 ) equal; to prove equality with the middle term, simply consider the case $M=\Omega$.
${ }^{\dagger}$ Since the Brownian motion has independent increments, it is a Markov process (see Examples 33.9 and 33.10); hence the terminology.

Thus the theorem holds if $\tau$ has countable range. For the general $\tau$, put

$$
\tau_{n}= \begin{cases}k 2^{-n} & \text { if }(k-1) 2^{-n}<\tau \leq k 2^{-n}, k=1,2, \ldots  \tag{37.26}\\ 0 & \text { if } \tau=0 .\end{cases}
$$

If $k 2^{-n} \leq t<(k+1) 2^{-n}$, then $\left[\tau_{n} \leq t\right]=\left[\tau \leq k 2^{-n}\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{k 2^{-n}} \subset \mathscr{F}_{t}$. Thus each $\tau_{n}$ is a stopping time. Suppose that $M \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ and $k 2^{-n} \leq t<(k+1) 2^{-n}$. Then $M \cap\left[\tau_{n} \leq t\right]=M \cap\left[\tau \leq k 2^{-n}\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{k 2^{-n}} \subset \mathscr{F}_{1}$. Thus $\mathscr{F}_{\tau} \subset \mathscr{F}_{\tau_{n}}$. Let $W_{t}^{(n)}(\omega)=W_{\tau_{n}(\omega)+t}(\omega)-W_{\tau_{n}(\omega)}(\omega)$-that is, let $W_{t}^{(n)}$ be the $W_{t}^{*}$ corresponding to the stopping time $\tau_{n}$. If $M \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ then $M \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau_{n}}$, and by an application of (37.25) to the discrete case already treated,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{(n)}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{(n)}\right) \in H\right] \cap M\right)=P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}\right) \in H\right] P(M) . \tag{37.27}
\end{equation*}
$$

But $\tau_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow \tau(\omega)$ for each $\omega$, and by continuity of the sample paths, $W_{t}^{(n)}(\omega) \rightarrow W_{t}^{*}(\omega)$ for each $\omega$. Condition on $M$ and apply Lemma 1 with $\left(W_{t_{1}}^{(n)}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{(n)}\right)$ for $X_{n},\left(W_{t_{1}}^{*}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{*}\right)$ for $X$, and the distribution function of ( $\left.W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{t_{n}}\right)^{k}$ for $F=F_{n}$. Then (37.25) follows from (37.27). $\square$

The $\tau$ in the proof of Theorem 37.4 is a stopping time, and so (37.16) is a Brownian motion, as required in that proof. Further applications will be given below.

If $\mathscr{F}^{*}=\sigma\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq 0\right]$, then according to (37.25) (and Theorem 4.2) the $\sigma$-fields $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$ and $\mathscr{F}^{*}$ are independent:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P(A \cap B)=P(A) P(B), \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau}, \quad B \in \mathscr{F}^{*} . \tag{37.28}
\end{equation*}
$$

For fixed $t$ define $\tau_{n}$ by (37.26) but with $t 2^{-n}$ in place of $2^{-n}$ at each occurrence. Then $\left[W_{\tau}<x\right] \cap[\tau \leq t]$ is the limit superior of the sets $\left[W_{\tau_{n}}<x\right] \cap[\tau \leq t]$, each of which lies in $\mathscr{F}_{1}$. This proves that $\left[W_{\tau}<x\right]$ lies in $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}^{n}$ and hence that $W_{\tau}$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$. Since $\tau$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}_{\tau}$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left[\left(\tau, W_{\tau}\right) \in H\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{\tau} \tag{37.29}
\end{equation*}
$$

for planar Borel sets $H$.

## The Reflection Principle

For a stopping time $\tau$, define

$$
W_{t}^{\prime \prime}= \begin{cases}W_{t} & \text { if } t \leq \tau,  \tag{37.30}\\ W_{\tau}-\left(W_{t}-W_{\tau}\right) & \text { if } t \geq \tau .\end{cases}
$$

The sample path for $\left[W_{t}^{\prime \prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is the same as the sample path for $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ up to $\tau$, and beyond that it is reflected through the point $W_{T}$. See the figure.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-042.jpg?height=641&width=1290&top_left_y=230&top_left_x=269)

The process defined by ( 37.30 ) is a Brownian motion, and to prove it, one need only check the finite-dimensional distributions: $P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}, \ldots, W_{k}\right) \in H\right]= P\left[\left(W_{t_{1}}^{\prime \prime}, \ldots, W_{t_{k}}^{\prime \prime}\right) \in H\right]$. By the argument starting with (37.26), it is enough to consider the case where $\tau$ has countable range, and for this it is enough to check the equation when the sets are intersected with $\left[\tau=t_{0}\right]$.

Consider for notational simplicity a pair of points:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}, W_{t}\right) \in H\right]=P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}^{\prime \prime}, W_{t}^{\prime \prime}\right) \in H\right] . \tag{37.31}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $s \leq t \leq t_{0}$, this holds because the two events are identical. Suppose next that $s \leq t_{0} \leq t$. Since $\left[\tau=t_{0}\right]$ lies in $\mathscr{F}_{t_{0}}$, it follows by the independence of the increments, symmetry, and the definition (37.30) that

$$
\begin{aligned}
P[\tau & \left.=t_{0},\left(W_{s}, W_{t_{0}}\right) \in I, W_{t}-W_{t_{0}} \in J\right] \\
& =P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}, W_{t_{0}}\right) \in I,-\left(W_{t}-W_{t_{0}}\right) \in J\right] \\
& =P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}^{\prime \prime}, W_{t_{0}}^{\prime \prime}\right) \in I, W_{t}^{\prime \prime}-W_{t_{0}}^{\prime \prime} \in J\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

If $K=I \times J$, this is

$$
P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}, W_{t_{0}}, W_{t}-W_{t_{0}}\right) \in K\right]=P\left[\tau=t_{0},\left(W_{s}^{\prime \prime}, W_{t_{0}}^{\prime \prime}, W_{t}^{\prime \prime}-W_{t_{0}}^{\prime \prime}\right) \in K\right],
$$

and by $\pi-\lambda$ it follows for all $K \in \mathscr{R}^{3}$. For the appropriate $K$, this gives (37.31). The remaining case, $t_{0} \leq s \leq t$, is similar.

These ideas can be used to derive in a very simple way the distribution of $M_{t}=\sup _{s \leq t} W_{s}$. Suppose that $x>0$. Let $\tau=\inf \left[s: W_{s} \geq x\right]$, define $W^{\prime \prime}$ by (37.30), and put $\tau^{\prime \prime}=\inf \left[s: W_{s}^{\prime \prime} \geq x\right]$ and $M_{t}^{\prime \prime}=\sup _{s \leq t} W_{s}^{\prime \prime}$. Since $\tau^{\prime \prime}=\tau$ and $W^{\prime \prime}$ is another Brownian motion, reflection through the point $W_{\tau}=x$ shows
that

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[M_{t} \geq x\right] & =P[\tau \leq t] \\
& =P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \leq x\right]+P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right] \\
& =P\left[\tau^{\prime \prime} \leq t, W_{t}^{\prime \prime} \leq x\right]+P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right] \\
& =P\left[\tau^{\prime \prime} \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right]+P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right] \\
& =P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right]+P\left[\tau \leq t, W_{t} \geq x\right]=2 P\left[W_{t} \geq x\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[M_{t} \geq x\right]=\frac{2}{\sqrt{2 \pi}}=\int_{x / \sqrt{t}}^{\infty} e^{-u^{2} / 2} d u \tag{37.32}
\end{equation*}
$$

This argument, an application of the reflection principle, ${ }^{\dagger}$ becomes quite transparent when referred to the diagram.

## Skorohod Embedding*

Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed random variables with mean 0 and variance $\sigma^{2}$. A powerful method, due to Skorohod, of studying the partial sums $S_{n}=X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$ is to construct an increasing sequence $\tau_{0}=0, \tau_{1}, \tau_{2}, \ldots$ of stopping times such that $W\left(\tau_{n}\right)$ has the same distribution as $S_{n}$. The differences $\tau_{k}-\tau_{k-1}$ will turn out to be independent and identically distributed with mean $\sigma^{2}$, so that by the law of large numbers $n^{-1} \tau_{n}=n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n}\left(\tau_{k}-\tau_{k-1}\right)$ is likely to be near $\sigma^{2}$. But if $\tau_{n}$ is near $n \sigma^{2}$, then by the continuity of Brownian motion paths $W\left(\tau_{n}\right)$ will be near $W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)$, and so the distribution of $S_{n} / \sigma \sqrt{n}$, which coincides with the distribution of $W\left(\tau_{n}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$, will be near the distribution of $W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$ -that is, will be near the standard normal distribution. The method will thus yield another proof of the central limit theorem, one independent of the characteristic-function arguments of Section 27.

But it will also give more. For example, the distribution of $\max _{k \leq n} S_{k} / \sigma \sqrt{n}$ is exactly the distribution of $\max _{k \leq n} W\left(\tau_{k}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$, and this in turn is near the distribution of $\sup _{t \leq n \sigma^{2}} W(t) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$, which can be written down explicitly because of (37.32). It will thus be possible to derive the limiting distribution of $\max _{k \leq n} S_{k}$. The joint behavior of the partial sums is closely related to the behavior of Brownian motion paths.

The Skorohod construction involves the class $\mathscr{T}$ of stopping times for which

$$
\begin{align*}
E\left[W_{\tau}\right] & =0,  \tag{37.33}\\
E[\tau] & =E\left[W_{\tau}^{2}\right], \tag{37.34}
\end{align*}
$$

[^7]and
$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[\tau^{2}\right] \leq 4 E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right] . \tag{37.35}
\end{equation*}
$$

Lemma 2. All bounded stopping times are members of $\mathscr{T}$.
Proof. Define $Y_{\theta, t}=\exp \left(\theta W_{t}-\frac{1}{2} \theta^{2} t\right)$ for all $\theta$ and for $t \geq 0$. Suppose that $s \leq t$ and $A \in \mathscr{F}_{s}$. Since Brownian motion has independent increments,

$$
\int_{A} Y_{\theta, t} d P=\int_{A} e^{\theta W_{s}-\theta^{2} s / 2} d P \cdot E\left[e^{\theta\left(W_{f}-W_{s}\right)-\theta^{2}(t-s) / 2}\right]
$$

and a calculation with moment generating functions (see Example 21.2) shows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{A} Y_{\theta, s} d P=\int_{A} Y_{\theta, t} d P, \quad s \leq t, \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{s} \tag{37.36}
\end{equation*}
$$

This says that for $\theta$ fixed, $\left[Y_{\theta, r}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a continuous-time martingale adapted to the $\sigma$-fields $\mathscr{F}_{r}$. It is the moment-generating-function martingale associated with the Brownian motion.

Let $f(\theta, t)$ denote the right side of (37.36). By Theorem 16.8,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\partial}{\partial \theta} f(\theta, t) & =\int_{A} Y_{\theta, t}\left(W_{t}-\theta t\right) d P \\
\frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial \theta^{2}} f(\theta, t) & =\int_{A} Y_{\theta, t}\left[\left(W_{t}-\theta t\right)^{2}-t\right] d P \\
\frac{\partial^{4}}{\partial \theta^{4}} f(\theta, t) & =\int_{A} Y_{\theta, t}\left[\left(W_{t}-\theta t\right)^{4}-6\left(W_{t}-\theta t\right)^{2} t+3 t^{2}\right] d P
\end{aligned}
$$

Differentiate the other side of the equation (37.36) the same way and set $\theta=0$. The result is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{A} W_{s} d P & =\int_{A} W_{t} d P, & s \leq t, & A \in \mathscr{F}, \\
\int_{A}\left(W_{s}^{2}-s\right) d P & =\int_{A}\left(W_{t}^{2}-t\right) d P, & s \leq t, & A \in \mathscr{F}, \\
\int_{A}\left(W_{s}^{4}-6 W_{s}^{2} s+3 s^{2}\right) d P & =\int_{A}\left(W_{t}^{4}-6 W_{t}^{2} t+3 t^{2}\right) d P, & s \leq t, & A \in \mathscr{F}_{s}
\end{aligned}
$$

This gives three more martingales: If $Z_{t}$ is any of the three random variables

$$
\begin{equation*}
W_{t}, \quad W_{t}^{2}-t, \quad W_{t}^{4}-6 W_{t}^{2} t+3 t^{2}, \tag{37.37}
\end{equation*}
$$

then $Z_{0}=0, Z_{t}$ is integrable and measurable $\mathscr{F}_{t}$, and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\int_{A} Z_{s} d P=\int_{A} Z_{t} d P, \quad s \leq t, \quad A \in \mathscr{F}_{s} . \tag{37.38}
\end{equation*}
$$

In particular, $E\left[Z_{t}\right]=\left[Z_{0}\right]=0$.
If $\tau$ is a stopping time with finite range $\left\{t_{1}, \ldots, t_{m}\right\}$ bounded by $t$, then (37.38) implies that

$$
E\left[Z_{\tau}\right]=\sum_{i} \int_{\left[\tau=t_{i}\right]} Z_{t_{i}} d P=\sum_{i} \int_{\left[\tau=t_{i}\right]} Z_{t} d P=E\left[Z_{t}\right]=0 .
$$

Suppose that $\tau$ is bounded by $t$ but does not necessarily have finite range. Put $\tau_{n}=k 2^{-n} t$ if $(k-1) 2^{-n} t<\tau \leq k 2^{-n} t, 1 \leq k \leq 2^{n}$, and put $\tau_{n}=0$ if $\tau=0$. Then $\tau_{n}$ is a stopping time and $E\left[Z_{\tau_{n}}\right]=0$. For each of the three possibilities (37.37) for $Z_{t}, \sup _{s \leq t}\left|Z_{s}\right|$ is integrable because of (37.32). It therefore follows by the dominated convergence theorem that $E\left[Z_{\tau}\right]= \lim _{n} E\left[Z_{\tau_{n}}\right]=0$.

Thus $\tilde{E}\left[Z_{\tau}\right]=0$ for every bounded stopping time $\tau$. The three cases (37.37) give

$$
E\left[W_{\tau}\right]=E\left[W_{\tau}^{2}-\tau\right]=E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}-6 W_{\tau}^{2} \tau+3 \tau^{2}\right]=0 .
$$

This implies (37.33), (37.34), and

$$
\begin{aligned}
0 & =E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]-6 E\left[W_{\tau}^{2} \tau\right]+3 E\left[\tau^{2}\right] \\
& \geq E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]-6 E^{1 / 2}\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right] E^{1 / 2}\left[\tau^{2}\right]+3 E\left[\tau^{2}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

If $C=E^{1 / 2}\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]$ and $x=E^{1 / 2}\left[\tau^{2}\right]$, the inequality is $0 \geq q(x)=3 x^{2}-6 C x+ C^{2}$. Each zero of $q$ is at most $2 C$, and $q$ is negative only between these two zeros. Therefore, $x \leq 2 C$, which implies (37.35). $\square$

Lemma 3. Suppose that $\tau$ and $\tau_{n}$ are stopping times, that each $\tau_{n}$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$, and that $\tau_{n} \rightarrow \tau$ with probability 1 . Then $\tau$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$ if (i) $E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}\right] \leq E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]<\infty$ for all $n$, or if (ii) the $W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}$ are uniformly integrable.

Proof. Since Brownian motion paths are continuous, $W_{\tau_{n}} \rightarrow W_{\tau}$ with probability 1 . Each of the two hypotheses (i) and (ii) implies that $E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}\right]$ is bounded and hence that $E\left[\tau_{n}^{2}\right]$ is bounded, and it follows (see (16.28)) that the sequences $\left\{\tau_{n}\right\},\left\{W_{\tau_{n}}\right\}$, and $\left\{W_{\tau_{n}}^{2}\right\}$ are uniformly integrable. Hence (37.33) and (37.34) for $\tau$ follow by Theorem 16.14 from the same relations for the $\tau_{n}$. The first hypothesis implies that $\liminf _{n} E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}\right] \leq E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]$, and the second implies that $\lim _{n} E\left[W_{T}^{4}\right]=E\left[W_{T}^{4}\right]$. In either case it follows by Fatou's lemma that $E\left[\tau^{2}\right] \leq \liminf _{n} E\left[\tau_{n}^{2}\right] \leq 4 \liminf _{n} E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}\right] \leq 4 E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]$. $\square$

Suppose that $a, b \geq 0$ and $a+b>0$, and let $\tau(a, b)$ be the hitting time for the set $(-a, b): \tau(a, b)=\inf \left[t: W_{t} \in\{-a, b\}\right]$. By (37.13), $\tau(a, b)$ is finite with probability 1 , and it is a stopping time because $\tau(a, b) \leq t$ if and only if for every $m$ there is a rational $r \leq t$ for which $W_{r}$ is within $m^{-1}$ of $-a$ or of $b$. From $|W(\min \{\tau(a, b), n\})| \leq \max \{a, b\}$ it follows by Lemma 3(ii) that $\tau(a, b)$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$. Since $W_{\tau(a, b)}$ assumes only the values $-a$ and $b, E\left[W_{\tau(a, b)}\right] =0$ implies that

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[W_{\tau(a, b)}=-a\right]=\frac{b}{a+b}, \quad P\left[W_{\tau(a, b)}=b\right]=\frac{a}{a+b} . \tag{37.39}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is obvious on grounds of symmetry in the case $a=b$.
Let $\mu$ be a probability measure on the line with mean 0 . The program is to construct a stopping time $\tau$ for which $W_{\tau}$ has distribution $\mu$. Assume that $\mu\{0\}<1$, since otherwise $\tau \equiv 0$ obviously works. If $\mu$ consists of two point masses, they must for some positive $a$ and $b$ be a mass of $b /(a+b)$ at $-a$ and a mass of $a /(a+b)$ at $b$; in this case $\tau_{(a, b)}$ is by (37.39) the required stopping time. The general case will be treated by adding together stopping times of this sort.

Consider a random variable $X$ having distribution $\mu$. (The probability space for $X$ has nothing to do with the space the given Brownian motion is defined on.) The technique will be to represent $X$ as the limit of a martingale $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ of a simple form and then to duplicate the martingale by $W_{\tau_{1}}, W_{\tau_{2}}, \ldots$ for stopping times $\tau_{n}$; the $\tau_{n}$ will have a limit $\tau$ such that $W_{\tau}$ has the same distribution as $X$.

The first step is to construct sets

$$
\Delta_{n}: a_{0}^{(n)}<a_{1}^{(n)}<\cdots<a_{r_{n}}^{(n)}
$$

and corresponding partitions

$$
\mathscr{P}_{n}:\left\{\begin{array}{l}
I_{0}^{n}=\left(-\infty, a_{0}^{(n)}\right] \\
I_{k}^{n}=\left(a_{k-1}^{(n)}, a_{k}^{(n)}\right], 1 \leq k \leq r_{n} \\
I_{r_{n}+1}^{n}=\left(a_{r_{n}}^{(n)}, \infty\right)
\end{array}\right.
$$

Let $M(H)$ be the conditional mean:

$$
M(H)=\frac{1}{\mu(H)} \int_{H} x \mu(d x) \quad \text { if } \mu(H)>0
$$

Let $\Delta_{1}$ consist of the single point $M\left(R^{1}\right)=E[X]=0$, so that $\mathscr{P}_{1}$ consists of $I_{0}^{1}=(-\infty, 0]$ and $I_{1}^{1}=(0, \infty)$. Suppose that $\Delta_{n}$ and $\mathscr{P}_{n}$ are given. If $\mu\left(\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)^{\circ}\right) >0$, split $I_{k}^{n}$ by adding to $\Delta_{n}$ the point $M\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)$, which lies in $\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)^{\circ}$; if $\mu\left(\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)^{\circ}\right)=0, I_{k}^{n}$ appears again in $\mathscr{P}_{n+1}$.

Let $\mathscr{G}_{n}$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by the sets $\left[X \in I_{k}^{n}\right]$, and put $X_{n}= E\left[X \| \mathscr{E}_{n}\right]$. Then $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is a martingale and $X_{n}=M\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)$ on $\left[X \in I_{k}^{n}\right]$. The $X_{n}$ have finite range, and their joint distributions can be written out explicitly. In fact, $\left[X_{1}=M\left(I_{k_{1}}^{1}\right), \ldots, X_{n}=M\left(I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right)\right]=\left[X \in I_{k_{1}}^{1}, \ldots, X \in I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right]$, and this set is empty unless $I_{k_{1}}^{1} \supset \cdots \supset I_{k_{n}}^{n}$, in which case it is $\left[X_{n}=M\left(I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right)\right]=[X \in \left.I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right]$. Therefore, if $k_{n-1}=j$ and $I_{j}^{n-1}=I_{k-1}^{n} \cup I_{k}^{n}$,

$$
P\left[X_{n}=M\left(I_{k-1}^{n}\right) \| X_{1}=M\left(I_{k_{1}}^{1}\right), \ldots, X_{n-1}=M\left(I_{k_{n-1}}^{n-1}\right)\right]=\frac{\mu\left(I_{k-1}^{n}\right)}{\mu\left(I_{j}^{n-1}\right)}
$$

and

$$
P\left[X_{n}=M\left(I_{k}^{n}\right) \| X_{1}=M\left(I_{k_{1}}^{1}\right), \ldots, X_{n-1}=M\left(I_{k_{n-1}}^{n-1}\right)\right]=\frac{\mu\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)}{\mu\left(I_{j}^{n-1}\right)},
$$

provided the conditioning event has positive probability. Thus the martingale $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ has the Markov property, and if $x=M\left(I_{j}^{n-1}\right), u=M\left(I_{k-1}^{n}\right)$, and $v= M\left(I_{k}^{n}\right)$, then the conditional distribution of $X_{n}$ given $X_{n-1}=x$ is concentrated at the two points $u$ and $v$ and has mean $x$. The structure of $\left\{X_{n}\right\}$ is determined by these conditional probabilities together with the distribution

$$
P\left[X_{1}=M\left(I_{0}^{1}\right)\right]=\mu\left(I_{0}^{1}\right), \quad P\left[X_{1}=M\left(I_{1}^{1}\right)\right]=\mu\left(I_{1}^{1}\right) .
$$

of $X_{1}$.
If $\mathscr{G}=\sigma\left(\cup_{n} \mathscr{G}_{n}\right)$, then $X_{n} \rightarrow E[X \| \mathscr{G}]$ with probability 1 by the martingale theorem (Theorem 35.6). But, in fact, $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 , as the following argument shows. Let $B$ be the union of all open sets of $\mu$-measure 0 . Then $B$ is a countable disjoint union of open intervals; enlarge $B$ by adding to it any endpoints of $\mu$-measure 0 these intervals may have. Then $\mu(B)=0$, and $x \notin B$ implies that $\mu(x-\epsilon, x]>0$ and $\mu[x, x+\epsilon)>0$ for all positive $\epsilon$. Suppose that $x=X(\omega) \notin B$ and let $x_{n}=X_{n}(\omega)$. Let $I_{k_{n}}^{n}$ be the element of $\mathscr{P}_{n}$ containing $x$; then $x_{n+1}=M\left(I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right)$ and $I_{k_{n}}^{n} \downarrow I$ for some interval $I$. Suppose that $x_{n+1}<x-\epsilon$ for $n$ in an infinite sequence $N$ of integers. Then $x_{n+1}$ is the left endpoint of $I_{k_{n+1}}^{n+1}$ for $n$ in $N$ and converges along $N$ to the left endpoint, say $a$, of $I$, and $(x-\epsilon, x] \subset I$. Further, $x_{n+1}=M\left(I_{k_{n}}^{n}\right) \rightarrow M(I)$ along $N$, so that $M(I)=a$. But this is impossible because $\mu(x-\epsilon, x]>0$. Therefore, $x_{n} \geq x-\epsilon$ for large $n$. Similarly, $x_{n} \leq x +\epsilon$ for large $n$, and so $x_{n} \rightarrow x$. Thus $X_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow X(\omega)$ if $X(\omega) \notin B$, the probability of which is 1 .

Now $X_{1}=E\left[X \| \mathscr{G}_{1}\right]$ has mean 0 , and its distribution consists of point masses at $-a=M\left(I_{0}^{1}\right)$ and $b=M\left(I_{1}^{1}\right)$. If $\tau_{1}=\tau(a, b)$ is the hitting time to
$\{-a, b\}$, then (see (37.39)) $\tau_{1}$ is a stopping time, a member of $\mathscr{T}$, and $W_{\tau_{1}}$ has the same distribution as $X_{1}$.

Let $\tau_{2}$ be the infimum of those $t$ for which $t \geq \tau_{1}$ and $W_{t}$ is one of the points $M\left(I_{k}^{2}\right), 0 \leq k \leq r_{2}+1$. By (37.13), $\tau_{2}$ is finite with probability 1 ; it is a stopping time, because $\tau_{2} \leq t$ if and only if for every $m$ there are rationals $r$ and $s$ such that $r \leq s+m^{-1}, r \leq t, s \leq t, W_{r}$ is within $m^{-1}$ of one of the points $M\left(I_{j}^{1}\right)$, and $W_{s}$ is within $m^{-1}$ of one of the points $M\left(I_{k}^{2}\right)$. Since $\left|W\left(\min \left\{\tau_{2}, n\right\}\right)\right|$ is at most the maximum of the values $\left|M\left(I_{k}^{2}\right)\right|$, it follows by Lemma 3(ii) that $\tau_{2}$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$.

Define $W_{t}^{*}$ by (37.24) with $\tau_{1}$ for $\tau$. If $x=M\left(I_{j}^{1}\right)$, then $x$ is an endpoint common to two adjacent intervals $I_{k-1}^{2}$ and $I_{k}^{2}$; put $u=M\left(I_{k-1}^{2}\right)$ and $v= M\left(I_{k}^{2}\right)$. If $W_{\tau_{1}}=x$, then $u$ and $v$ are the only possible values of $W_{\tau_{2}}$. If $\tau^{*}$ is the first time the Brownian motion $\left[W_{t}^{*}: t \geq 0\right]$ hits $u-x$ or $v-x$, then by (37.39),

$$
P\left[W_{\tau^{*}}^{*}=u-x\right]=\frac{v-x}{v-u}, \quad P\left[W_{\tau^{*}}^{*}=v-x\right]=\frac{x-u}{v-u} .
$$

On the set [ $W_{\tau_{1}}=x$ ], $\tau_{2}$ coincides with $\tau_{1}+\tau^{*}$, and it follows by (37.28) that

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[W_{\tau_{1}}=x, W_{\tau_{2}}=v\right] & =P\left[W_{\tau_{1}}=x, x+W_{\tau^{*}}^{*}=v\right] \\
& =P\left[W_{\tau_{1}}=x\right] P\left[W_{\tau^{*}}^{*}=v-x\right]=P\left[W_{\tau_{1}}\right] \frac{x-u}{v-u}
\end{aligned}
$$

This, together with the same computation with $u$ in place of $v$, shows that for $W_{\tau_{1}}=x$ the conditional distribution of $W_{\tau_{2}}$ is concentrated at the two points $u$ and $v$ and has mean $x$. Thus the conditional distribution of $W_{\tau_{2}}$ given $W_{\tau_{1}}$ coincides with the conditional distribution of $X_{2}$ given $X_{1}$. Since $W_{\tau_{1}}$ and $X_{1}$ have the same distribution, the random vectors ( $W_{\tau_{1}}, W_{\tau_{2}}$ ) and ( $X_{1}, X_{2}$ ) also have the same distribution.

An inductive extension of this argument proves the existence of a sequence of stopping times $\tau_{n}$ such that $\tau_{1} \leq \tau_{2} \leq \cdots$, each $\tau_{n}$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$, and for each $n, W_{\tau_{1}}, \ldots, W_{\tau_{n}}$ have the same joint distribution as $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{n}$. Now suppose that $X$ has finite variance. Since $\tau_{n}$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}, E\left[\tau_{n}\right]=E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{2}\right]=E\left[X_{n}^{2}\right]=E\left[E^{2}\left[X \| \mathscr{A}_{n}\right]\right] \leq E\left[X^{2}\right]$ by Jensen's inequality (34.7). Thus $\tau=\lim _{n} \tau_{n}$ is finite with probability 1 . Obviously it is a stopping time, and by path continuity, $W_{\tau_{n}} \rightarrow W_{\tau}$ with probability 1 . Since $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ with probability 1 , it is a consequence of the following lemma that $W_{\tau}$ has the distribution of $X$.

Lemma 4. If $X_{n} \rightarrow X$ and $Y_{n} \rightarrow Y$ with probability 1 , and if $X_{n}$ and $Y_{n}$ have the same distribution, then so do $X$ and $Y$.

Proof. ${ }^{\dagger}$ By two applications of (4.9),

$$
\begin{aligned}
P[X \leq x] & \leq P[X<x+\epsilon] \leq \lim \inf _{n} P\left[X_{n} \leq x+\epsilon\right] \\
& \leq \lim \sup _{n} P\left[Y_{n} \leq x+\epsilon\right] \leq P[Y \leq x+\epsilon] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Let $\epsilon \rightarrow 0: P[X \leq x] \leq P[Y \leq x]$. Now interchange the roles of $X$ and $Y$. $\square$

Since $X_{n}^{2} \leq E\left[X^{2} \| \mathscr{A}_{n}\right]$, the $X_{n}$ are uniformly integrable by the lemma preceding Theorem 35.6. By the monotone convergence theorem and Theorem 16.14, $E[\tau]=\lim _{n} E\left[\tau_{n}\right]=\lim _{n} E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{2}\right]=\lim _{n} E\left[X_{n}^{2}\right]=E\left[X^{2}\right]=E\left[W_{\tau}^{2}\right]$. If $E\left[X^{4}\right]<\infty$, then $E\left[W_{\tau_{n}}^{4}\right]=E\left[X_{n}^{4}\right] \leq E\left[X^{4}\right]=E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]$ (Jensen's inequality again), and so $\tau$ is a member of $\mathscr{T}$. Hence $E\left[\tau^{2}\right] \leq 4 E\left[W_{\tau}^{4}\right]$.

This construction establishes the first of Skorohod's embedding theorems:
Theorem 37.6. Suppose that $X$ is a random variable with mean 0 and finite variance. There is a stopping time $\tau$ such that $W_{\tau}$ has the same distribution as $X, E[\tau]=E\left[X^{2}\right]$, and $E\left[\tau^{2}\right] \leq 4 E\left[X^{4}\right]$.

Of course, the last inequality is trivial unless $E\left[X^{4}\right]$ is finite. The theorem could be stated in terms not of $X$ but of its distribution, the point being that the probability space $X$ is defined on is irrelevant. Skorohod's second embedding theorem is this:

Theorem 37.7. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed random variables with mean 0 and finite variance, and put $S_{n}=X_{1} +\cdots+X_{n}$. There is a nondecreasing sequence $\tau_{1}, \tau_{2}, \ldots$ of stopping times such that the $W_{\tau_{n}}$ have the same joint distributions as the $S_{n}$ and $\tau_{1}, \tau_{2}-\tau_{1}, \tau_{3} -\tau_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and identically distributed random variables satisfying $E\left[\tau_{n}-\tau_{n-1}\right]=E\left[X_{1}^{2}\right]$ and $E\left[\left(\tau_{n}-\tau_{n-1}\right)^{2}\right] \leq 4 E\left[X_{1}^{4}\right]$.

Proof. The method is to repeat the construction above inductively. For notational clarity write $W_{t}=W_{t}^{(1)}$ and put $\mathscr{F}_{t}^{(1)}=\sigma\left[W_{s}^{(1)}: 0 \leq s \leq t\right]$ and $\mathscr{F}^{(1)}=\sigma\left[W_{t}^{(1)}: t \geq 0\right]$. Let $\delta_{1}$ be the stopping time of Theorem 37.6, so that $W_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}$ and $X_{1}$ have the same distribution. Let $\mathscr{F}_{\delta_{1}}{ }^{(1)}$ be the class of $M$ such that $M \cap\left[\delta_{1} \leq t\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{t}^{(1)}$ for all $t$.

Now put $W_{t}^{(2)}=W_{\delta_{i}+t}^{(1)}-W_{\delta_{i}}^{(1)}, \mathscr{F}_{t}^{(2)}=\sigma\left[W_{s}^{(2)}: 0 \leq s \leq t\right]$, and $\mathscr{F}^{(2)}= \sigma\left[W_{t}^{(2)}: t \geq 0\right]$. By another application of Theorem 37.6, construct a stopping time $\delta_{2}$ for the Brownian motion $\left[W_{t}^{(2)}: t \geq 0\right]$ in such a way that $W_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}$ has the same distribution as $X_{1}$. In fact, use for $\delta_{2}$ the very same martingale construction as for $\delta_{1}$, so that ( $\delta_{1}, W_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}$ ) and ( $\delta_{2}, W_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}$ ) have the same distribution. Since $\mathscr{F}_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}$ and $\mathscr{F}^{(2)}$ are independent (see (37.28)), it follows (see (37.29)) that $\left(\delta_{1}, W_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}\right)$ and $\left(\delta_{2}, W_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}\right)$ are independent.

[^8]Let $\mathscr{F}_{\delta}^{(2)}$ be the class of $M$ such that $M \cap\left[\delta_{2} \leq t\right] \in \mathscr{F}_{t}^{(2)}$ for all $t$. If $W_{t}^{(3)}=W_{\delta_{2}+t}^{(2)}-W_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}$ and $\mathscr{F}^{(3)}$ is the $\sigma$-field generated by these random variables, then again $\mathscr{F}_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}$ and $\mathscr{F}^{(3)}$ are independent. These two $\sigma$-fields are contained in $\mathscr{F}^{(2)}$, which is independent of $\mathscr{F}_{\delta_{i}}^{(1)}$. Therefore, the three $\sigma$-fields $\mathscr{F}_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}, \mathscr{F}_{\delta_{2}}^{(2)}, \mathscr{F}^{(3)}$ are independent. The procedure therefore extends inductively to give independent, identically distributed random vectors ( $\delta_{n}, W_{\delta_{n}}^{(n)}$ ). If $\tau_{n}=\delta_{1}+\cdots+\delta_{n}$, then $W_{\tau_{n}}^{(1)}=W_{\delta_{1}}^{(1)}+\cdots+W_{\delta_{n}}^{(n)}$ has the distribution of $X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}$. $\square$

## Invariance*

If $E\left[X_{1}^{2}\right]=\sigma^{2}$, then, since the random variables $\tau_{n}-\tau_{n-1}$ of Theorem 37.7 are independent and identically distributed, the strong law of large numbers (Theorem 22.1) applies and hence so does the weak one:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\left|n^{-1} \tau_{n}-\sigma^{2}\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0 . \tag{37.40}
\end{equation*}
$$

(If $E\left[X_{1}^{4}\right]<\infty$, so that the $\tau_{n}-\tau_{n-1}$ have second moments, this follows immediately by Chebyshev's inequality.) Now $S_{n}$ has the distribution of $W\left(\tau_{n}\right)$, and $\tau_{n}$ is near $n \sigma^{2}$ by (37.40); hence $S_{n}$ should have nearly the distribution of $W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)$, namely the normal distribution with mean 0 and variance $n \sigma^{2}$.

To prove this, choose an increasing sequence of integers $N_{k}$ such that $P\left[\left|n^{-1} \tau_{n}-\sigma^{2}\right| \geq k^{-1}\right]<k^{-1}$ for $n \geq N_{k}$, and put $\epsilon_{n}=k^{-1}$ for $N_{k} \leq n<N_{k+1}$. Then $\epsilon_{n} \rightarrow 0$ and $P\left[\left|n^{-1} \tau_{n}-\sigma^{2}\right| \geq \epsilon_{n}\right]<\epsilon_{n}$. By two applications of (37.32),

$$
\begin{aligned}
\delta_{n}(\epsilon) & =P\left[\frac{\left|W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)-W\left(\tau_{n}\right)\right|}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \geq \epsilon\right] \\
& \leq P\left[\left|n^{-1} \tau_{n}-\sigma^{2}\right| \geq \epsilon_{n}\right]+P\left[\sup _{\left|t-n \sigma^{2}\right| \leq \epsilon_{n} n}\left|W(t)-W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)\right| \geq \epsilon \sigma \sqrt{n}\right] \\
& \leq \epsilon_{n}+4 P\left[\left|W\left(\epsilon_{n} n\right)\right| \geq \epsilon \sigma \sqrt{n}\right],
\end{aligned}
$$

and it follows by Chebyshev's inequality that $\lim _{n} \delta_{n}(\epsilon)=0$. Since $S_{n}$ is distributed as $W\left(\tau_{n}\right)$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
P\left[\frac{W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq x-\epsilon\right]-\delta_{n}(\epsilon) & \leq P\left[\frac{S_{n}}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq x\right] \\
& \leq P\left[\frac{W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq x+\epsilon\right]+\delta_{n}(\epsilon) .
\end{aligned}
$$

[^9]Here $W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$ can be replaced by a random variable $N$ with the standard normal distribution, and letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ and then $\epsilon \rightarrow 0$ shows that

$$
\lim _{n} P\left[\frac{S_{n}}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq x\right]=P[N \leq x] .
$$

This gives a new proof of the central limit theorem for independent, identically distributed random variables with second moments (the Lindeberg-Lévy theorem-Theorem 27.1). Observe that none of the convergence theory of Chapter 5 has been used.

This proof of the central limit theorem is an application of the invariance principle: $S_{n}$ has nearly the distribution of $W\left(n \sigma^{2}\right)$, and the distribution of the latter does not depend on (vary with) the distribution common to the $X_{n}$. More can be said if the $X_{n}$ have fourth moments.

For each $n$, define a stochastic process $\left[Y_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$ by $Y_{n}(0, \omega)=0$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
Y_{n}(t, \omega)=\frac{1}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} S_{k}(\omega) \quad \text { if } \frac{k-1}{n}<t \leq \frac{k}{n}, \quad k=1, \ldots, n . \tag{37.41}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $k / n=t>0$ and $n$ is large, then $k$ is large, too, and $Y_{n}(t)=t^{1 / 2} S_{k} / \sigma \sqrt{k}$ is by the central limit theorem approximately normally distributed with mean 0 and variance $t$. Since the $X_{n}$ are independent, the increments of (37.41) should be approximately independent, and so the process should behave approximately as a Brownian motion does.

Let $\tau_{n}$ be the stopping times of Theorem 37.7, and in analogy with (37.41) put $Z_{n}(0)=0$ and

$$
\begin{equation*}
Z_{n}(t)=\frac{1}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} W\left(\tau_{k}\right) \quad \text { if } \frac{k-1}{n}<t \leq \frac{k}{n}, \quad k=1, \ldots, n . \tag{37.42}
\end{equation*}
$$

By construction, the finite-dimensional distributions of $\left[Y_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$ coincide with those of $\left[Z_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$. It will be shown that the latter process nearly coincides with $\left[W\left(t n \sigma^{2}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}: 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$, which is itself a Brownian motion over the time interval $[0,1]$-see (37.11). Put $W_{n}(t)=W\left(t n \sigma^{2}\right) / \sigma \sqrt{n}$.

Let $B_{n}(\delta)$ be the event that $\left|\tau_{k}-k \sigma^{2}\right| \geq \delta n \sigma^{2}$ for some $k \leq n$. By Kolmogorov's inequality (22.9),

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(B_{n}(\delta)\right) \leq \frac{\operatorname{Var}\left[\tau_{n}\right]}{\delta^{2} n^{2} \sigma^{4}} \leq \frac{4 E\left[X_{1}^{4}\right]}{\delta^{2} n \sigma^{4}} \rightarrow 0 . \tag{37.43}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $(k-1) n^{-1}<t \leq k n^{-1}$ and $n>\delta^{-1}$, then

$$
\left|\frac{\tau_{k}}{n \sigma^{2}}-t\right| \leq\left|\frac{\tau_{k}}{n \sigma^{2}}-\frac{k}{n}\right|+\frac{1}{n} \leq 2 \delta
$$

on the event $\left(B_{n}(\delta)\right)^{c}$, and so

$$
\left|Z_{n}(t)-W_{n}(t)\right|=\left|W_{n}\left(\frac{\tau_{k}}{n \sigma^{2}}\right)-W_{n}(t)\right| \leq \sup _{|s-t| \leq 2 \delta}\left|W_{n}(s)-W_{n}(t)\right|
$$

on $\left(B_{n}(\delta)\right)^{c}$. Since the distribution of this last random variable is unchanged if the $W_{n}(t)$ are replaced by $W(t)$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1}\left|Z_{n}(t)-W_{n}(t)\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \\
& \quad \leq P\left(B_{n}(\delta)\right)+P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1} \sup _{|s-t| \leq 2 \delta}|W(s)-W(t)| \geq \epsilon\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Let $n \rightarrow \infty$ and then $\delta \rightarrow 0$; it follows by (37.43) and the continuity of Brownian motion paths that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{n} P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1}\left|Z_{n}(t)-W_{n}(t)\right| \geq \epsilon\right]=0 \tag{37.44}
\end{equation*}
$$

for positive $\epsilon$. Since the processes (37.41) and (37.42) have the same finitedimensional distributions, this proves the following general invariance principle or functional central limit theorem.

Theorem 37.8. Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent, identically distributed random variables with mean 0 , variance $\sigma^{2}$, and finite fourth moments, and define $Y_{n}(t)$ by (37.41). There exist (on another probability space), for each $n$, processes $\left[Z_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$ and $\left[W_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$ such that the first has the same finite-dimensional distributions as $\left[Y_{n}(t): 0 \leq t \leq 1\right]$, ihe second is a Brownian motion, and $P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1}\left|Z_{n}(t)-W_{n}(t)\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0$ for positive $\epsilon$.

As an application, consider the maximum $M_{n}=\max _{k \leq n} S_{k}$. Now $M_{n} / \sigma \sqrt{n}=\sup _{t} Y_{n}(t)$ has the same distribution as $\sup _{t} Z_{n}(t)$, and it follows by (37.44) that

$$
P\left[\left|\sup _{t \leq 1} Z_{n}(t)-\sup _{t \leq 1} W_{n}(t)\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \rightarrow 0 .
$$

But $P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1} W_{n}(t) \geq x\right]=P\left[\sup _{t \leq 1} W(t) \geq x\right]=2 P[N \geq x]$ for $x \geq 0$ by (37.32). Therefore,

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\frac{M_{n}}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq x\right] \rightarrow 2 P[N \leq x], \quad x \geq 0 . \tag{37.45}
\end{equation*}
$$

## PROBLEMS

37.1. 36.2 ↑ Show that $K(s, t)=\min \{s, t\}$ is nonnegative-definite; use Problem 36.2 to prove the existence of a process with the finite-dimensional distributions prescribed for Brownian motion.
37.2. Let $X(t)$ be independent, standard normal variables, one for each dyadic rational $t$ (Theorem 20.4; the unit interval can be used as the probability space). Let $W(0)=0$ and $W(n)=\sum_{k=1}^{n} X(k)$. Suppose that $W(t)$ is already defined for dyadic rationals of rank $\eta$, and put

$$
W\left(\frac{2 k+1}{2^{n+1}}\right)=\frac{1}{2} W\left(\frac{k}{2^{n}}\right)+\frac{1}{2} W\left(\frac{k+1}{2^{n}}\right)+\frac{1}{2^{1+n / 2}} X\left(\frac{2 k+1}{2^{n+1}}\right) .
$$

Prove by induction that the $W(t)$ for dyadic $t$ have the finite-dimensional distributions prescribed for Brownian motion. Now construct a Brownian motion with continuous paths by the argument leading to Theorem 37.1. This avoids an appeal to Kolmogorov's existence theorem.
37.3. ↑ For each $n$ define new variables $W_{n}(t)$ by setting $W_{n}\left(k / 2^{n}\right)=W\left(k / 2^{n}\right)$ for dyadics of order $n$ and interpolating linearly in between. Set $\delta_{n}= \sup _{t \leq n}\left|W_{n+1}(t)-W_{n}(t)\right|$, and show that

$$
\delta_{n}=\max _{0 \leq k<n 2^{n}}\left|W\left(\frac{2 k+1}{2^{n+1}}\right)-\left[\frac{1}{2} W\left(\frac{k}{2^{n}}\right)+\frac{1}{2} W\left(\frac{k+1}{2^{n}}\right)\right]\right| .
$$

The construction in the preceding problem makes it clear that the difference here is normal with variance $1 / 2^{n+2}$. Find positive $x_{n}$ such that $\sum x_{n}$ and $\sum P\left[\delta_{n} \geq x_{n}\right]$ both converge, and conclude that outside a set of probability 0 , $W_{n}(t, \omega)$ converges uniformly over bounded intervals. Replace $W(t, \omega)$ by $\lim _{n} W_{n}(t, \omega)$. This gives another construction of a Brownian motion with continuous paths.
37.4. 36.6 ↑ Let $T=[0, \infty)$, and let $P$ be a probability measure on ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}$ ) having the finite-dimensional distributions prescribed for Brownian motion. Let $C$ consist of the continuous elements of $R^{T}$.
(a) Show that $P_{*}(C)=0$, or $P^{*}\left(R^{T}-C\right)=1$ (see (3.9) and (3.10)). Thus completing ( $R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}, P$ ) will not give $C$ probability 1 .
(b) Show that $P^{*}(C)=1$.
37.5. Suppose that $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is some stochastic process having independent, stationary increments satisfying $E\left[W_{t}\right]=0$ and $E\left[W_{t}^{2}\right]=t$. Show that if the finite-dimensional distributions are preserved by the transformation (37.11), then they must be those of Brownian motion.
37.6. Show that $\bigcap_{t>0} \sigma\left[W_{s}: s \geq t\right]$ contains only sets of probability 0 and 1 . Do the same for $\bigcap_{\epsilon>0} \sigma\left[W_{t}: 0<t<\epsilon\right]$; give examples of sets in this $\sigma$-field.
37.7. Show by a direct argument that $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is with probability $\mathbf{1}$ of unbounded variation on $[0,1]$ : Let $Y_{n}=\sum_{i=1}^{2^{n}}\left|W\left(i 2^{-n}\right)-W\left((i-1) 2^{-n}\right)\right|$. Show that $Y_{n}$ has mean $\left.2^{n / 2} E\left[\mid W_{1}\right]\right]$ and variance at most $\operatorname{Var}\left[\left|W_{1}\right|\right]$. Conclude that $\sum P\left[Y_{n}<n\right]<\infty$.
