37.8. Show that the Poisson process as defined by (23.5) is measurable.
37.9. Show that for $T=[0, \infty)$ the coordinate-variable process $\left[Z_{t}: t \in T\right]$ on $\left(R^{T}, \mathscr{R}^{T}\right)$ is not measurable.
37.10. Extend Theorem 37.4 to the set $[t: W(t, \omega)=\alpha]$.
37.11. Let $\tau_{\alpha}$ be the first time the Brownian motion hits $\alpha>0: \tau_{\alpha}=\inf \left[t: W_{t} \geq \alpha\right]$. Show that the distribution of $\tau_{\alpha}$ has over $(0, \infty)$ the density

$$
\begin{equation*}
h_{\alpha}(t)=\frac{\alpha}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \frac{1}{t^{3 / 2}} e^{-\alpha^{2} / 2 t} \tag{37.46}
\end{equation*}
$$

Show that $E\left[\tau_{\alpha}\right]=\infty$. Show that $\tau_{\alpha}$ has the same distribution as $\alpha^{2} / N^{2}$, where $N$ is a standard normal variable.
37.12. $\uparrow$ (a) Show by the strong Markov property that $\tau_{\alpha}$ and $\tau_{\alpha+\beta}-\tau_{\alpha}$ are independent and that the latter has the same distribution as $\tau_{\beta}$. Conclude that $h_{\alpha} * h_{\beta}=h_{\alpha+\beta}$. Show that $\beta \tau_{\alpha}$ has the same distribution as $\tau_{\alpha \sqrt{\beta}}$.
(b) Show that each $h_{\alpha}$ is stable-see Problem 28.10.
37.13. $\uparrow$ Suppose that $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and each has the distribution (37.46).
(a) Show that $\left(X_{1}+\cdots+X_{n}\right) / n^{2}$ also has the distribution (37.46). Contrast this with the law of large numbers.
(b) Show that $P\left[n^{-2} \max _{k \leq n} X_{k} \leq x\right] \rightarrow \exp (-\alpha \sqrt{2 / \pi x})$ for $x>0$. Relate this to Theorem 14.3.
37.14. 37.11 ↑ Let $\rho(s, t)$ be the probability that a Brownian path has at least one zero in ( $s, t$ ). From (37.46) and the Markov property deduce

$$
\begin{equation*}
\rho(s, t)=\frac{2}{\pi} \arccos \sqrt{\frac{s}{t}} \tag{37.47}
\end{equation*}
$$

Hint: Condition with respect to $W_{s}$.
37.15. $\uparrow$ (a) Show that the probability of no zero in $(t, 1)$ is $(2 / \pi) \arcsin \sqrt{t}$ and hence that the position of the last zero preceding 1 is distributed over $(0,1)$ with density $\pi^{-1}(t(1-t))^{-1 / 2}$.
(b) Similarly calculate the distribution of the position of the first zero following time 1.
(c) Calculate the joint distribution of the two zeros in (a) and (b).
37.16. ↑ (a) Show by Theorem 37.8 that $\inf _{s \leq u \leq t} Y_{n}(u)$ and $\inf _{s \leq u \leq t} Z_{n}(u)$ both converge in distribution to $\inf _{s \leq u \leq t} W(u)$ for $0 \leq s \leq t \leq 1$. Prove a similar result for the supremum.
(b) Let $A_{n}(s, t)$ be the event that $S_{k}$, the position at time $k$ in a symmetric random walk, is 0 for at least one $k$ in the range $s n \leq k \leq t n$, and show that $P\left(A_{n}(s, t)\right) \rightarrow(2 / \pi) \arccos \sqrt{s / t}$.
(c) Let $T_{n}$ be the maximum $k$ such that $k \leq n$ and $S_{k}=0$. Show that $T_{n} / n$ has asymptotically the distribution with density $\pi^{-1}(t(1-t))^{-1 / 2}$ over $(0,1)$. As this density is larger at the ends of the interval than in the middle, the last time during a night's play a gambler was even is more likely to be either early or late than to be around midnight.
37.17. $\uparrow$ Show that $\rho(s, t)=\rho\left(t^{-1}, s^{-1}\right)=\rho(c s, c t)$. Check this by (37.47) and also by the fact that the transformations (37.11) and (37.12) preserve the properties of Brownian motion.
37.18. Deduce by the reflection principle that ( $M_{l}, W_{l}$ ) has density

$$
\frac{2(2 y-x)}{t \sqrt{2 \pi t}} \exp \left[-\frac{(2 y-x)^{2}}{2 t}\right]
$$

on the set where $y \geq 0$ and $y \geq x$. Now deduce from Theorem 37.8 the corresponding limit theorem for symmetric random walk.
37.19. Show by means of the transformation (37.12) that for positive $a$ and $b$ the probability is 1 that the process is within the boundary $-a t<W_{1}<b t$ for all sufficiently large $t$. Show that $a /(a+b)$ is the probability that it last touches above rather than below.
37.20. The martingale calculation used for (37.39) also works for slanting boundaries. For positive $a, b, r$, let $\tau$ be the smallest $t$ such that either $W_{t}=-a+r t$ or $W_{t}=b+r t$, and let $p(a, b, r)$ be the probability that the exit is through the upper barrier-that $W_{\tau}=b+r \tau$.
(a) For the martingale $Y_{\theta, t}$ in the proof of Lemma 2, show that $E\left[Y_{\theta, t}\right]=1$. Operating formally at first, conclude that

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[e^{\theta W_{T}-\theta^{2} \tau / 2}\right]=1 . \tag{37.48}
\end{equation*}
$$

Take $\theta=2 r$, and note that $\theta W_{\tau}-\frac{1}{2} \theta^{2} \tau$ is then $2 r b$ if the exit is above (probability $p(a, b, r)$ ) and $-2 r a$ if the exit is below (probability $1-p(a, b, r)$ ). Deduce

$$
p(a, b, r)=\frac{1-e^{2 r a}}{e^{2 r b}-e^{-2 r a}}
$$

(b) Show that $p(a, b, r) \rightarrow a /(a+b)$ as $r \rightarrow 0$, in agreement with (37.39).
(c) It remains to justify (37.48) for $\theta=2 r$. From $E\left[Y_{\theta, t}\right]=1$ deduce

$$
\begin{equation*}
E\left[e^{2 r\left(W_{\sigma}-r^{2}(r)\right.}\right]=1 \tag{37.49}
\end{equation*}
$$

for nonrandom $\sigma$. By the arguments in the proofs of Lemmas 2 and 3, show that (37.49) holds for simple stopping times $\sigma$, for bounded ones, for $\sigma=\tau \wedge n$, for $\sigma=\tau$.

## SECTION 38. NONDENUMERABLE PROBABILITIES*

## Introduction

As observed a number of times above, the finite-dimensional distributions do not suffice to determine the character of the sample paths of a process. To obtain paths with natural regularity properties, the Poisson and Brownian motion processes were constructed by ad hoc methods. It is always possible to ensure that the paths have a certain very general regularity property called separability, and from this property will follow in appropriate circumstances various other desirable regularity properties.

Section 4 dealt with "denumerable" probabilities; questions about path functions involve all the time points and hence concern "nondenumerable" probabilities.

Example 38.1. For a mathematically simple illustration of the fact that path properties are not entirely determined by the finite-dimensional distributions, consider a probability space ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P$ ) on which is defined a positive random variable $V$ with continuous distribution: $P[V=x]=0$ for each $x$. For $t \geq 0$, put $X(t, \omega)=0$ for all $\omega$, and put

$$
Y(t, \omega)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } V(\omega)=t,  \tag{38.1}\\ 0 & \text { if } V(\omega) \neq t .\end{cases}
$$

Since $V$ has continuous distribution, $P\left[X_{t}=Y_{t}\right]=1$ for each $t$, and so $\left[X_{t}\right.$ : $t \geq 0]$ and $\left[Y_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ are stochastic processes with identical finite-dimensional distributions; for each $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$, the distribution $\mu_{t_{1} t_{k}}$ common to $\left(X_{t_{1}}, \ldots, X_{t_{k}}\right)$ and $\left(Y_{t_{1}}, \ldots, Y_{t_{k}}\right)$ concentrates all its mass at the origin of $R^{k}$. But what about the sample paths? Of course, $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is identically 0 , but $Y(\cdot, \omega)$ has a discontinuity-it is 1 at $t=V(\omega)$ and 0 elsewhere. It is because the position of this discontinuity has a continuous distribution that the two processes have the same finite-dimensional distributions. $\square$

## Definitions

The idea of separability is to make a countable set of time points serve to determine the properties of the process. In all that follows, the time set $T$ will for definiteness be taken as $[0, \infty)$. Most of the results hold with an arbitrary subset of the line in the role of $T$.

As in Section 36, let $R^{T}$ be the set of all real functions over $T=[0, \infty)$. Let $D$ be a countable, dense subset of $T$. A function $x$-an element of $R^{T}$-is separable $D$, or separable with respect to $D$, if for each $t$ in $T$ there exists a

[^10]sequence $t_{1}, t_{2}, \ldots$ of points such that
$$
\begin{equation*}
t_{n} \in D, \quad t_{n} \rightarrow t, \quad x\left(t_{n}\right) \rightarrow x(t) . \tag{38.2}
\end{equation*}
$$
(Because of the middle condition here, it was redundant to require $D$ dense at the outset.) For $t$ in $D$, (38.2) imposes no condition on $x$, since $t_{n}$ may be taken as $t$. An $x$ separable with respect to $D$ is determined by its values at the points of $D$. Note, however, that separability requires that (38.2) hold for every $t$-an uncountable set of conditions. It is not hard to show that the set of functions separable with respect to $D$ lies outside $\mathscr{R}^{T}$.

Example 38.2. If $x$ is everywhere continuous or right-continuous, then it is separable with respect to every countable, dense $D$.

Suppose that $x(t)$ is 0 for $t \neq v$ and 1 for $t=v$, where $v>0$. Then $x$ is not separable with respect to $D$ unless $v$ lies in $D$. The paths $Y(\cdot, \omega)$ in Example 38.1 are of this form. $\square$

The condition for separability can be stated another way; $x$ is separable $D$ if and only if for every $t$ and every open interval $I$ containing $t, x(t)$ lies in the closure of $[x(s): s \in I \cap D]$.

Suppose that $x$ is separable $D$ and that $I$ is an open interval in $T$. If $\epsilon>0$, then $x\left(t_{0}\right)+\epsilon>\sup _{t \in I} x(t)=u$ for some $t_{0}$ in $I$. By separability $\left|x\left(s_{0}\right)-x\left(t_{0}\right)\right|<\epsilon$ for some $s_{0}$ in $I \cap D$. But then $x\left(s_{0}\right)+2 \epsilon>u$, so that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sup _{t \in I} x(t)=\sup _{t \in I \cap D} x(t) . \tag{38.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

Similarly,

$$
\begin{equation*}
\inf _{t \in I} x(t)=\inf _{t \in I \cap D} x(t) \tag{38.4}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sup _{t_{0} \leq t<t_{0}+\delta}\left|x(t)-x\left(t_{0}\right)\right|=\sup _{\substack{t_{0} \leq t<t_{0}+\delta \\ t \in D}}\left|x(t)-x\left(t_{0}\right)\right| . \tag{38.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

A stochastic process $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ is separable $D$ if $D$ is a countable, dense subset of $T=[0, \infty)$ and there is an $\mathscr{F}$-set $N$ such that $P(N)=0$ and such that the sample path $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ for $\omega$ outside $N$. Finally, the process is separable if it is separable with respect to some $D$; this $D$ is sometimes called a separant. In these definitions it is assumed for the moment that $X(t, \omega)$ is a finite real number for each $t$ and $\omega$.

Example 38.3. If the sample path $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous for each $\omega$, then the process is separable with respect to each countable, dense $D$. This covers Brownian motion as constructed in the preceding section. $\square$

Example 38.4. Suppose that $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ has the finite-dimensional distributions of Brownian motion, but do not assume as in the preceding section that the paths are necessarily continuous. Assume, however, that $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is separable with respect to $D$. Fix $t_{0}$ and $\delta$. Choose sets $D_{m}=\left\{t_{m 1}, \ldots, t_{m m}\right\}$ of $D$-points such that $t_{0}<t_{m 1}<\cdots<t_{m m}<t_{0}+\delta$ and $D_{m} \uparrow D \cap\left(t_{0}, t_{0}+\delta\right)$. By the argument leading to (37.9),

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left[\sup _{\substack{t_{0} \leq l \leq t_{0}+\delta \\ l \in D}}\left|W_{t}-W_{t_{0}}\right|>\alpha\right] \leq \frac{K \delta^{2}}{\alpha^{4}} . \tag{38.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

For sample points outside the $N$ in the definition of separability, the supremum in (38.6) is unaltered, because of (38.5), if the restriction $t \in D$ is dropped. Since $P(N)=0$,

$$
P\left[\sup _{t_{0} \leq \imath \leq l_{0}+\delta}\left|W_{\imath}-W_{t_{0}}\right|>\alpha\right] \leq \frac{K \delta^{2}}{\alpha^{4}} .
$$

Define $M_{n}$ by (37.8) but with $r$ ranging over all the reals (not just over the dyadic rationals) in $\left[k 2^{-n},(k+2) 2^{-n}\right]$. Then $P\left[M_{n}>n^{-i}\right] \leq 4 K n^{5} / 2^{n}$ follows just as before. But for $\omega$ outside $B=\left[M_{n}>n^{-1}\right.$ i.o.], $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous. Since $P(B)=0, W(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous for $\omega$ outside an $\mathscr{F}$-set of probability 0 . If $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ is complete, then the set of $\omega$ for which $W(\cdot, \omega)$ is continuous is an $\mathscr{F}$-set of probability 1 . Thus paths are continuous with probability 1 for any separable process having the finite-dimensional distributions of Brownian motion-provided that the underlying space is complete, which can of course always be arranged. $\square$

As it will be shown below that there exists a separable process with any consistently prescribed set of finite-dimensional distributions, Example 38.4 provides another approach to the construction of continuous Brownian motion. The value of the method lies in its generality. It must not, however, be imagined that separability automatically ensures smooth sample paths:

Example 38.5. Suppose that the random variables $X_{t}, t \geq 0$, are independent, each having the standard normal distribution. Let $D$ be any countable set dense in $T=[0, \infty)$. Suppose that $I$ and $J$ are open intervals with rational endpoints. Since the random variables $X_{t}$ with $t \in D \cap I$ are independent, and since the value common to the $P\left[X_{I} \in J\right]$ is positive, the second Borel-Cantelli lemma implies that with probability $1, X_{1} \in J$ for some $t$ in $D \cap I$. Since there are only countably many pairs $I$ and $J$ with rational endpoints, there is an $\mathscr{F}$-set $N$ such that $P(N)=0$ and such that for $\omega$ outside $N$ the set $[X(t, \omega): t \in D \cap I]$ is everywhere dense on the line for every open interval $I$ in $T$. This implies that $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ is separable with respect to $D$. But also of course it implies that the paths are highly irregular.

This irregularity is not a shortcoming of the concept of separability-it is a necessary consequence of the properties of the finite-dimensional distributions specified in this example. $\square$

Example 38.6. The process $\left[Y_{r}: t \geq 0\right]$ in Example 38.1 is not separable: The path $Y(\cdot, \omega)$ is not separable $D$ unless $D$ contains the point $V(\omega)$. The set of $\omega$ for which $Y(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable $D$ is thus contained in $[\omega: V(\omega) \in D]$, a set of probability 0 , since $D$ is countable and $V$ has a continuous distribution. $\square$

## Existence Theorems

It will be proved in stages that for every consistent system of finite-dimensional distributions there exists a separable process having those distributions. Define $x$ to be separable $D$ at the point $t$ if there exist points $t_{n}$ in $D$ such that $t_{n} \rightarrow t$ and $x\left(t_{n}\right) \rightarrow x(t)$. Note that this is no restriction on $x$ if $t$ lies in $D$, and note that separability is the same thing as separability at every $t$.

Lemma 1. Let $\left[X_{r}: t \geq 0\right]$ be a stochastic process on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$. There exists a countable, dense set $D$ in $[0, \infty)$, and there exists for each $t$ an $\mathscr{F}$-set $N(t)$, such that $P(N(t))=0$ and such that for $\omega$ outside $N(t)$ the path function $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable $D$ at $t$.

Proof. Fix open intervals $I$ and $J$, and consider the probability

$$
p(U)=P\left(\bigcap_{s \in U}\left[X_{s} \notin J\right]\right)
$$

for countable subsets $U$ of $I \cap T$. As $U$ increases, the intersection here decreases and so does $p(U)$. Choose $U_{n}$ so that $p\left(U_{n}\right) \rightarrow \inf _{U} p(U)$. If $U(I, J)=\cup_{n} U_{n}$, then $U(I, J)$ is a countable subset of $I \cap T$ making $p(U)$ minimal:

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\bigcap_{s \in U(I, J)}\left[X_{s} \notin J\right]\right) \leq P\left(\bigcap_{s \in U}\left[X_{s} \notin J\right]\right) \tag{38.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

for every countable subset $U$ of $I \cap T$. If $t \in I \cap T$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
P\left(\left[X_{\imath} \in J\right] \cap \bigcap_{s \in U(I, J)}\left[X_{s} \notin J\right]\right)=0, \tag{( 38.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

because otherwise (38.7) would fail for $U=U(I, J) \cup\{t\}$.

Let $D=\cup U(I, J)$, where the union extends over all open intervals $I$ and $J$ with rational endpoints. Then $D$ is a countable, dense subset of $T$. For each $t$ let

$$
\begin{equation*}
N(t)=\bigcup\left(\left[X_{i} \in J\right] \cap \bigcap_{s \in U(I, J)}\left[X_{s} \notin J\right]\right), \tag{38.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

where the union extends over all open intervals $J$ that have rational endpoints and over all open intervals $I$ that have rational endpoints and contain $t$. Then $N(t)$ is by (38.8) an $\mathscr{F}$-set such that $P(N(t))=0$.

Fix $t$ and $\omega \notin N(t)$. The problem is to show that $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ at $t$. Given $n$, choose open intervals $I$ and $J$ that have rational endpoints and lengths less than $n^{-1}$ and satisfy $t \in I$ and $X(t, \omega) \in J$. Since $\omega$ lies outside (38.9), there must be an $s_{n}$ in $U(I, J)$ such that $X\left(s_{n}, \omega\right) \in J$. But then $s_{\eta} \in D,\left|s_{n}-t\right|<n^{-1}$, and $\left|X\left(s_{n}, \omega\right)-X(t, \omega)\right|<n^{-1}$. Thus $s_{n} \rightarrow t$ and $X\left(s_{n}, \omega\right) \rightarrow X(t, \omega)$ for a sequence $s_{1}, s_{2}, \ldots$ in $D$.

For any countable $D$, the set of $\omega$ for which $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ at $\dot{t}$ is

$$
\begin{equation*}
\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} \bigcup_{\substack{|s-|<n^{-1} \\ s \in D}}\left[\omega:|X(t, \omega)-X(s, \omega)|<n^{-1}\right] . \tag{38.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

This set lies in $\mathscr{F}$ for each $t$, and the point of the lemma is that it is possible to choose $D$ in such a way that each of these sets has probability 1.

Lemma 2. Let $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ be a stochastic process on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$. Suppose that for all $t$ and $\omega$

$$
\begin{equation*}
a<X(t, \omega)<b \tag{38.11}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then there exists on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ a process $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ having these three properties:
(i) $P\left[X_{i}^{\prime}=X_{i}\right]=1$ for each $t$.
(ii) For some countable, dense subset $D$ of $[0, \infty), X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable $D$ for every $\omega$ in $\Omega$.
(iii) For all $t$ and $\omega$,

$$
\begin{equation*}
a \leq X^{\prime}(t, \omega) \leq b \tag{38.12}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proof. Choose a countable, dense set $D$ and $\mathscr{F}$-sets $N(t)$ of probability 0 as in Lemma 1. If $t \in D$ or if $\omega \notin N(t)$, define $X^{\prime}(t, \omega)=X(t, \omega)$. If $t \notin D$,
fix some sequence $\left\{s_{n}^{(t)}\right\}$ in $D$ for which $\lim _{n} s_{n}^{(t)}=t$, and define $X^{\prime}(t, \omega)= \limsup _{n} X\left(s_{n}^{(t)}, \omega\right)$ for $\omega \in N(t)$. To sum up,

$$
X^{\prime}(t, \omega)= \begin{cases}X(t, \omega) & \text { if } t \in D \text { or } \omega \notin N(t),  \tag{38.13}\\ \limsup _{n} X\left(s_{n}^{(l)}, \omega\right) & \text { if } t \notin D \text { and } \omega \in N(t) .\end{cases}
$$

Since $N(t) \in \mathscr{F}, X_{t}^{\prime}$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}$ for each $t$. Since $P(N(t))=0, P\left[X_{t}=\right. \left.X_{t}^{\prime}\right]=1$ for each $t$.

Fix $t$ and $\omega$. If $t \in D$, then certainly $X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable $D$ at $t$, and so assume $t \notin D$. If $\omega \notin N(t)$, then by the construction of $N(t), X(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ at $t$, so that there exist points $s_{n}$ in $D$ such that $s_{n} \rightarrow t$ and $X\left(s_{n}, \omega\right) \rightarrow X(t, \omega)$. But $X\left(s_{n}, \omega\right)=X^{\prime}\left(s_{n}, \omega\right)$ because $s_{n} \in D$, and $X(t, \omega)=X^{\prime}(t, \omega)$ because $\omega \notin N(t)$. Hence $X^{\prime}\left(s_{n}, \omega\right) \rightarrow X^{\prime}(t, \omega)$, and so $X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ at $t$. Finally, suppose that $t \notin D$ and $\omega \in N(t)$. Then $X^{\prime}(t, \omega)=\lim _{k} X\left(s_{n_{k}}^{(t)}, \omega\right)$ for some sequence $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ of integers. As $k \rightarrow \infty, s_{n_{k}}^{(t)} \rightarrow t$ and $X^{\prime}\left(s_{n_{k}}^{(t)}, \omega\right)=X\left(s_{n_{k}}^{(t)}, \omega\right) \rightarrow X^{\prime}(t, \omega)$, so that again $X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ is separable with respect to $D$ at $t$. Clearly, (38.11) implies (38.12). $\square$

Example 38.7. One must allow for the possibility of equality in (38.12). Suppose that $V(\omega)>0$ for all $\omega$ and that $V$ has a continuous distribution. Define

$$
f(t)= \begin{cases}e^{-|t|} & \text { if } t \neq 0, \\ 0 & \text { if } t=0,\end{cases}
$$

and put $X(t, \omega)=f(t-V(\omega))$. If $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is any separable process with the same finite-dimensional distributions as $\left[X_{1}: t \geq 0\right]$, then $X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ must with probability 1 assume the value 1 somewhere. In this case (38.11) holds for $a<0$ and $b=1$, and equality in (38.12) cannot be avoided. $\square$

If

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sup _{t, \omega}|X(t, \omega)|<\infty, \tag{38.14}
\end{equation*}
$$

then (38.11) holds for some $a$ and $b$. To treat the case in which (38.14) fails, it is necessary to allow for the possibility of infinite values. If $x(t)$ is $\infty$ or $-\infty$, replace the third condition in (38.2) by $x\left(t_{n}\right) \rightarrow \infty$ or $x\left(t_{n}\right) \rightarrow-\infty$. This extends the definition of separability to functions $x$ that may assume infinite values and to processes $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ for which $X(t, \omega)= \pm \infty$ is a possibility.

Theorem 38.1. If $\left[X_{i}: t \geq 0\right]$ is a finite-valued process on $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$, there exists on the same space a separable process $\left[X_{1}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ such that $P\left[X_{t}^{\prime}=X_{t}\right]=1$ for each $t$.

It is assumed for convenience here that $X(t, \omega)$ is finite for all $t$ and $\omega$, although this is not really necessary. But in some cases infinite values for certain $X^{\prime}(t, \omega)$ cannot be avoided-see Example 38.8.

Proof. If (38.14) holds, the result is an immediate consequence of Lemma 2. The definition of separability allows an exceptional set $N$ of probability 0 ; in the construction of Lemma 2 this set is actually empty, but it is clear from the definition this could be arranged anyway.

The case in which (38.14) may fail could be treated by tracing through the preceding proofs, making slight changes to allow for infinite values. A simple argument makes this unnecessary. Let $g$ be a continuous, strictly increasing mapping of $R^{1}$ onto ( 0,1 ). Let $Y(t, \omega)=g(X(t, \omega))$. Lemma 2 applies to [ $Y_{t}$ : $t \geq 0]$; there exists a separable process $\left[Y_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ such that $P\left[Y_{t}^{\prime}=Y_{t}\right]=1$. Since $0<Y(t, \omega)<1$, Lemma 2 ensures $0 \leq Y^{\prime}(t, \omega) \leq 1$. Define

$$
X^{\prime}(t, \omega)= \begin{cases}-\infty & \text { if } Y^{\prime}(t, \omega)=0, \\ g^{-1}\left(Y^{\prime}(t, \omega)\right) & \text { if } 0<Y^{\prime}(t, \omega)<1, \\ +\infty & \text { if } Y^{\prime}(t, \omega)=1 .\end{cases}
$$

Then $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ satisfies the requirements. Note that $P\left[X_{t}^{\prime}= \pm \infty\right]=0$ for each $t$. $\square$

Example 38.8. Suppose that $V(\omega)>0$ for all $\omega$ and $V$ has a continuous distribution. Define

$$
h(t)= \begin{cases}|t|^{-1} & \text { if } t \neq 0, \\ 0 & \text { if } t=0,\end{cases}
$$

and put $X(t, \omega)=h(t-V(\omega))$. This is analogous to Example 38.7. If $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}\right.$ : $t \geq 0]$ is separable and has the finite-dimensional distributions of $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$, then $X^{\prime}(\cdot, \omega)$ must with probability 1 assume the value $\infty$ for some $t$. $\square$

Combining Theorem 38.1 with Kolmogorov's existence theorem shows that for any consistent system of finite-dimensional distributions $\mu_{t_{1}} t_{k}$ there exists a separable process with the $\mu_{t_{1}} \mathrm{t}_{k}$ as finite dimensional distributions. As shown in Example 38.4, this leads to another construction of Brownian motion with continuous paths.

## Consequences of Separability

The next theorem implies in effect that, if the finite-dimensional distributions of a process are such that it "should" have continuous paths, then it will in fact have continuous paths if it is separable. Example 38.4 illustrates this. The same thing holds for properties other than continuity.

Let $\bar{R}^{T}$ be the set of functions on $T=[0, \infty)$ with values that are ordinary reals or else $\infty$ or $-\infty$. Thus $\bar{R}^{T}$ is an enlargement of the $R^{T}$ of Section 36, an enlargement necessary because separability sometimes forces infinite values. Define the function $Z_{t}$ on $\bar{R}^{T}$ by $Z_{t}(x)=Z(t, x)=x(t)$. This is just an extension of the coordinate function (36.8). Let $\overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$ be the $\sigma$-field in $\bar{R}^{T}$ generated by the $Z_{t}, t \geq 0$.

Suppose that $A$ is a subset of $\bar{R}^{T}$, not necessarily in $\overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$. For $D \subset T=[0, \infty)$, let $A_{D}$ consist of those elements $x$ of $\bar{R}^{T}$ that agree on $D$ with some element $y$ of $A$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
A_{D}=\bigcup_{y \in A} \bigcap_{t \in D}\left[x \in \bar{R}^{T}: x(t)=y(t)\right] . \tag{38.15}
\end{equation*}
$$

Of course, $A \subset A_{D}$. Let $S_{D}$ denote the set of $x$ in $\bar{R}^{T}$ that are separable with respect to $D$.

In the following theorem, $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ and $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ are processes on spaces $(\Omega, \mathscr{F}, P)$ and $\left(\Omega^{\prime}, \mathscr{F}^{\prime}, P^{\prime}\right)$, which may be distinct; the path functions are $X(\cdot, \omega)$ and $X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right)$.

Theorem 38.2. Suppose of $A$ that for each countable, dense subset $D$ of $T=[0, \infty)$, the set (38.15) satisfies

$$
\begin{equation*}
A_{D} \in \overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}, \quad A_{D} \cap S_{D} \subset A . \tag{38.16}
\end{equation*}
$$

If $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ and $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ have the same finite-dimensional distributions, if $[\omega: X(\cdot, \omega) \in A]$ lies in $\mathscr{F}$ and has $P$-measure 1 , and if $\left[X_{t}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is separable, then $\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A\right]$ contains an $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$-set of $P^{\prime}$-measure 1 .

If ( $\Omega^{\prime}, \mathscr{F}^{\prime}, P^{\prime}$ ) is complete, then of course [ $\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A$ ] is itself an $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$-set of $P^{\prime}$-measure 1.

Proof. Suppose that $\left[X_{i}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is separable with respect to $D$. The difference $\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A_{D}\right]-\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A\right]$ is by (38.16) a subset of $\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in \bar{R}^{T}-S_{D}\right]$, which is contained in an $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$-set of $N^{\prime}$ of $P^{\prime}$-measure 0 . Since the two processes have the same finite-dimensional distributions and hence induce the same distribution on ( $\bar{R}^{T}, \overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$ ), and since $A_{D}$ lies in $\overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$, it follows that $P^{\prime}\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A_{D}\right]=P\left[\omega: X(\cdot, \omega) \in A_{D}\right] \geq P[\omega$ : $X(\cdot, \omega) \in A]=1$. Thus the subset $\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right) \in A_{D}\right]-N^{\prime}$ of $\left[\omega^{\prime}: X^{\prime}\left(\cdot, \omega^{\prime}\right)\right. \in A]$ lies in $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$ and has $P^{\prime}$-measure 1. $\square$

Example 38.9. Consider the set $C$ of finite-valued, continuous functions on $T$. If $x \in S_{D}$ and $y \in C$, and if $x$ and $y$ agree on a dense $D$, then $x$ and $y$ agree everywhere: $x=y$. Therefore, $C_{D} \cap S_{D} \subset C$. Further,

$$
C_{D}=\bigcap_{\epsilon, t} \bigcup_{\delta} \bigcap_{s}\left[x \in \bar{R}^{T}:|x(s)|<\infty,|x(t)|<\infty,|x(s)-x(t)|<\epsilon\right],
$$

where $\epsilon$ and $\delta$ range over the positive rationals, $t$ ranges over $D$, and the inner intersection extends over the $s$ in $D$ satisfying $|s-t|<\delta$. Hence $C_{D} \in \overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$. Thus $C$ satisfies the condition (38.16).

Theorem 38.2 now implies that if a process has continuous paths with probability 1 , then any separable process having the same finite-dimensional distributions has continuous paths outside a set of probability 0 . In particular, a Brownian motion with continuous paths was constructed in the preceding section, and so any separable process with the finite-dimensional distributions of Brownian motion has continuous paths outside a set of probability 0 . The argument in Example 38.4 now becomes supererogatory.

Example 38.10. There is a somewhat similar argument for the step functions of the Poisson process. Let $Z^{+}$be the set of nonnegative integers; let $E$ consist of the nondecreasing functions $x$ in $\bar{R}^{T}$ such that $x(t) \in Z^{+}$for all $t$ and such that for every $n \in Z^{+}$there exists a nonempty interval $I$ such that $x(t)=n$ for $t \in I$. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{D}= & \bigcap_{t \in D}\left[x: x(t) \in Z^{+}\right] \cap \bigcap_{s, t \in D, s<t}[\lambda: x(s) \leq x(t)] \\
& \cap \bigcap_{n=0}^{\infty} \bigcup_{I} \bigcap_{t \in D \cap l}[x: x(t)=n]
\end{aligned}
$$

where $I$ ranges over the open intervals with rational endpoints. Thus $E_{D} \in \overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$. Clearly, $E_{D} \cap S_{D} \subset E$, and so Theorem 38.2 applies.

In Section 23 was constructed a Poisson process with paths in $E$, and therefore any separable process with the same finite-dimensional distributions will have paths in $E$ except for a set of probability 0 . $\square$

Example 38.11. For $E$ as in Example 38.10, let $E_{0}$ consist of the elements of $E$ that are right-continuous; a function in $E$ need not lie in $E_{0}$, although at each $t$ it must be continuous from one side or the other. The Poisson process as defined in Section 23 by $N_{t}=\max \left[n: S_{n} \leq t\right]$ (see (23.5)) has paths in $E_{0}$. But if $N_{t}^{\prime}=\max [n$ : $S_{n}<t$ ], then $\left[N_{i}^{\prime}: t \geq 0\right]$ is separable and has the same finite-dimensional distributions, but its paths are not in $E_{0}$. Thus $E_{0}$ does not satisfy the hypotheses of Theorem 38.2. Separability does not help distinguish between continuity from the right and continuity from the left. $\square$

Example 38.12. The class of sets $A$ satisfying (38.16) is closed under the formation of countable unions and intersections but is not closed under complementation. Define $X_{t}$ and $Y_{t}$ as in Example 38.1, and let $C$ be the set of continuous paths. Then $\left[Y_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ and $\left[X_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ have the same finite-dimensional distributions, and the latter is separable; $Y(\cdot, \omega)$ is in $\bar{R}^{T}-C$ for each $\omega$, and $X(\cdot, \omega)$ is in $\bar{R}^{T}-C$ for no $\omega$. $\square$

Example 38.13. As a final example, consider the set $J$ of functions with discontinuities of at most the first kind; $x$ is in $J$ if it is finite-valued, if $x(t+)=\lim _{s, t} x(s)$ exists (finite) for $t \geq 0$ and $x(t-)=\lim _{s \uparrow t} s(s)$ exists (finite) for $t>0$, and if $x(t)$ lies between $x(t+)$ and $x(t-)$ for $t>0$. Continuous and right-continuous functions are special cases.

Let $V$ denote the general system

$$
\begin{equation*}
V: k ; r_{1}, \ldots, r_{k} ; s_{1}, \ldots, s_{k} ; \alpha_{1}, \ldots, \alpha_{k}, \tag{38.17}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $k$ is an integer, where the $r_{i}, s_{i}$, and $\alpha_{i}$ are rational, and where

$$
0=r_{1}<s_{1}<r_{2}<s_{2}<\cdot \quad<r_{k}<s_{k} .
$$

Define

$$
\begin{aligned}
J(D, V, \epsilon)= & \bigcap_{i=1}^{k}\left[x: \alpha_{i} \leq x(t) \leq \alpha_{i}+\epsilon, t \in\left(r_{i}, s_{i}\right) \cap D\right] \\
& \cap \bigcap_{i=2}^{k}\left[x: \min \left\{\alpha_{i-1}, \alpha_{i}\right\} \leq x(t) \leq \max \left\{\alpha_{i-1}, \alpha_{i}\right\}+\epsilon, t \in\left(s_{i-1}, r_{i}\right) \cap D\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

Let $\mathscr{U}_{m, k, \delta}$ be the class of systems (38.17) that have a fixed value for $k$ and satisfy $r_{i}-s_{i-1}<\delta, i=2, \ldots, k$, and $s_{k}>m$. It will be shown that

$$
\begin{equation*}
J_{D}=\bigcap_{m=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{\epsilon} \bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty} \bigcap_{\delta} \bigcup_{V \in \mathscr{U}_{m, k} \delta} J(D, V, \epsilon), \tag{38.18}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $\epsilon$ and $\delta$ range over the positive rationals. From this it will follow that $J_{D} \in \overline{\mathscr{R}}^{T}$. It will also be shown that $J_{D} \cap S_{D} \subset J$, so that $J$ satisfies the hypothesis of Theorem 38.2.

Suppose that $y \in J$. For fixed $\epsilon$, let $H$ be the set of nonnegative $h$ for which there exist finitely many points $t_{i}$ such that $0=t_{0} \leq t_{1} \leq \cdots \leq t_{r}=h$ and $\left|y(t)-y\left(t^{\prime}\right)\right|<\epsilon$ for $t$ and $t^{\prime}$ in the same interval ( $t_{i-1}, t_{i}$ ). If $h_{n} \in H$ and $h_{n} \uparrow h$, then from the existence of $y(h-)$ follows $h \in H$. Hence $H$ is closed. If $h \in H$, from the existence of $y(h+)$ it follows that $H$ contains points to the right of $h$. Therefore, $H=[0, \infty)$. From this it follows that the right side of (38.18) contains $J_{D}$.

Suppose that $x$ is a member of the right side of (38.18). It is not hard to deduce that for each $t$ the limits

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{s \downarrow t, s \in D} x(s), \quad \lim _{s \uparrow t, s \in D} x(s) \tag{38.19}
\end{equation*}
$$

exist and that $x(t)$ lies between them if $t \in D$. For $t \in D$ take $y(t)=x(t)$, and for $t \notin D$ take $y(t)$ to be the first limit in (38.19). Then $y \in J$ and hence $x \in J_{D}$. This argument also shows that $J_{D} \cap S_{D} \subset J$. $\square$

## Appendix

Gathered here for easy reference are certain definitions and results from set theory and real analysis required in the text. Although there are many newer books, Hausdorff (the early sections) on set theory and Hardy on analysis are still excellent for the general background assumed here.

## Set Theory

A1. The empty set is denoted by $\varnothing$. Sets are variable subsets of some space that is fixed in any one definition, argument, or discussion; this space is denoted either generically by $\Omega$ or by some special symbol (such as $R^{k}$ for Euclidean $k$-space). A singleton is a set consisting of just one point or element. That $A$ is a subset of $B$ is expressed by $A \subset B$. In accordance with standard usage, $A \subset B$ does not preclude $A=B ; A$ is a proper subset of $B$ if $A \subset B$ and $A \neq B$.

The complement of $A$ is always relative to the overall space $\Omega$; it consists of the points of $\Omega$ not contained in $A$ and is denoted by $A^{c}$. The difference between $A$ and $B$, denoted by $A-B$, is $A \cap B^{c}$; here $B$ need not be contained in $A$, and if it is, then $A-B$ is a proper difference. The symmetric difference $A \Delta B=\left(A \cap B^{c}\right) \cup\left(A^{c} \cap B\right)$ consists of the points that lie in one of the sets $A$ and $B$ but not in both.

Classes of sets are denoted by script letters. The power set of $\Omega$ is the class of all subsets of $\Omega$; it is denoted $2^{\Omega}$.

A2. The set of $\omega$ that lie in $A$ and satisfy a given property $p(\omega)$ is denoted $[\omega \in A$ : $p(\omega)]$; if $A=\Omega$, this is usually shortened to $[\omega: p(\omega)]$.

A3. In this book, to say that a collection $\left[A_{\theta}: \theta \in \Theta\right]$ is disjoint always means that it is pairwise disjoint: $A_{\theta} \cap A_{\theta^{\prime}}=0$ if $\theta$ and $\theta^{\prime}$ are distinct elements of the index set $\Theta$. To say that $A$ meets $B$, or that $B$ meets $A$, is to say that they are not disjoint: $A \cap B \neq \varnothing$. The collection [ $A_{\theta}: \theta \in \Theta$ ] covers $B$ if $B \subset \bigcup_{\theta} A_{\theta}$. The collection is a decomposition or partition of $B$ if it is disjoint and $B=\bigcup_{\theta} A_{\theta}$.

A4. By $A_{n} \uparrow A$ is meant $A_{1} \subset A_{2} \subset \cdots$ and $A=\bigcup_{n} A_{n}$; by $A_{n} \downarrow A$ is meant $A_{1} \supset A_{2} \supset \cdots$ and $A=\cap_{n} A_{n}$.

A5. The indicator, or indicator function, of a set $A$ is the function on $\Omega$ that assumes the value 1 on $A$ and 0 on $A^{c}$; it is denoted $I_{A}$. The alternative term "characteristic function" is reserved for the Fourier transform (see Section 26).

A6. De Morgan's laws are $\left(\cup_{\theta} A_{\theta}\right)^{c}=\bigcap_{\theta} A_{\theta}^{c}$ and $\left(\cap_{\theta} A_{\theta}\right)^{c}=\cup_{\theta} A_{\theta}^{c}$. These and the other facts of basic set theory are assumed known: a countable union of countable sets is countable, and so on.

A7. If $T: \Omega \rightarrow \Omega^{\prime}$ is a mapping of $\Omega$ into $\Omega^{\prime}$ and $A^{\prime}$ is a set in $\Omega^{\prime}$, the inverse image of $A^{\prime}$ is $T^{-1} A^{\prime}=\left[\omega \in \Omega: T \omega \in A^{\prime}\right]$. It is easily checked that each of these statements is equivalent to the next: $\omega \in \Omega-T^{-1} A^{\prime}, \omega \notin T^{-1} A^{\prime}, T \omega \notin A^{\prime}, T \omega \in \Omega^{\prime}-A^{\prime}, \omega \in T^{-1}\left(\Omega^{\prime}\right. \left.-A^{\prime}\right)$. Therefore, $\Omega-T^{-1} A^{\prime}=T^{-1}\left(\Omega^{\prime}-A^{\prime}\right)$. Simple considerations of this kind show that $\cup_{\theta} T^{-1} A_{\theta}^{\prime}=T^{-1}\left(\cup_{\theta} A_{\theta}^{\prime}\right)$ and $\cap_{\theta} T^{-1} A_{\theta}^{\prime}=T^{-1}\left(\cap_{\theta} A_{\theta}^{\prime}\right)$, and that $A^{\prime} \cap B^{\prime}=\varnothing$ implies $T^{-1} A^{\prime} \cap T^{-1} B^{\prime}=\varnothing$ (the reverse implication is false unless $T \Omega=\Omega^{\prime}$ ).

If $f$ maps $\Omega$ into another space, $f(\omega)$ is the value of the function $f$ at an unspecified value of the argument $\omega$. The function $f$ itself (the rule defining the mapping) is sometimes denoted $f(\cdot)$. This is especially convenient for a function $f(\omega, t)$ of two arguments: For each fixed $t, f(\cdot, t)$ denotes the function on $\Omega$ with value $f(\omega, t)$ at $\omega$.

A8. The axiom of choice. Suppose that $\left[A_{\theta}: \theta \in \Theta\right]$ is a decomposition of $\Omega$ into nonempty sets. The axiom of choice says that there exists a set (at least one set) $C$ that contains exactly one point from each $A_{\theta}: C \cap A_{\theta}$ is a singleton for each $\theta$ in $\Theta$. The existence of such sets $C$ is assumed in "everyday" mathematics, and the axiom of choice may even seem to be simply true. A careful treatment of set theory, however, is based on an explicit list of such axioms and a study of the relationships between them; see Halmos ${ }_{2}$ or Dudley.

A few of the problems require Zorn's lemma, which is equivalent to the axiom of choice; see Dudley or Kaplansky.

## The Real Line

A9. The real line is denoted by $R^{1} ; x \vee y=\max \{x, y\}$ and $x \wedge y=\min \{x, y\}$. For real $x,\lfloor x\rfloor$ is the integer part of $x$, and $\operatorname{sgn} x$ is $+1,0$, or -1 as $x$ is positive, 0 , or negative. It is convenient to be explicit about open, closed, and half-open intervals:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& (a, b)=[x: a<x<b], \\
& {[a, b]=[x: a \leq x \leq b],} \\
& (a, b]=[x: a<x \leq b], \\
& {[a, b)=[x: a \leq x<b] .}
\end{aligned}
$$

A10. Of course $x_{n} \rightarrow x$ means $\lim _{n} x_{n}=x ; x_{n} \uparrow x$ means $x_{1} \leq x_{2} \leq \cdots$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow x$; $x_{n} \downarrow x$ means $x_{1} \geq x_{2} \geq \cdots$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow x$.

A sequence ( $x_{n}$ ) is bounded if and only if every subsequence ( $x_{n_{k}}$ ) contains a further subsequence $\left\{x_{n_{k(j)}}\right\}$ that converges to some $x: \lim _{j} x_{n_{(j)}}=x$. If $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$ is not bounded, then for each $k$ there is an $n_{k}$ for which $\left|x_{n_{\ell}}\right|>k$; no subsequence of $\left\{x_{n_{k}}\right\}$ can converge. The implication in the other direction is a simple consequence of the fact that every bounded sequence contains a convergent subsequence.

If $\left(x_{n}\right)$ is bounded, and if each subsequence that converges at all converges to $x$, then $\lim _{n} x_{n}=x$. If $x_{n}$ does not converge to $x$, then $\left|x_{n_{k}}-x\right|>\epsilon$ for some positive $\epsilon$ and some increasing sequence $\left\{n_{k}\right\}$ of integers; some subsequence of $\left\{x_{n_{k}}\right\}$ converges, but the limit cannot be $x$.

A11. A set $G$ is defined as open if for each $x$ in $G$ there is an open interval $I$ such that $x \in I \subset G$. A set $F$ is defined as closed if $F^{c}$ is open. The interior of $A$, denoted
$A^{\circ}$, consists of the $x$ in $A$ for which there exists an open interval $I$ such that $x \in I \subset A$. The closure of $A$, denoted $A^{-}$, consists of the $x$ for which there exists a sequence $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$ in $A$ with $x_{n} \rightarrow x$. The boundary of $A$ is $\partial A=A^{-}-A^{\circ}$. The basic facts of real analysis are assumed known: $A$ is open if and only if $A=A^{\circ} ; A$ is closed if and only if $A=A^{-} ; A$ is closed if and only if it contains all limits of sequences in it; $x$ lies in $\partial A$ if and only if there is a sequence $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$ in $A$ and a sequence $\left\{y_{n}\right\}$ in $A^{c}$ such that $x_{n} \rightarrow x$ and $y_{n} \rightarrow x$; and so on.

A12. Every open set $G$ on the line is a countable, disjoint union of open intervals. To see this, define points $x$ and $y$ of $G$ to be equivalent if $x \leq y$ and $[x, y] \subset G$ or $y \leq x$ and $[y, x] \subset G$. This is an equivalence relation. Each equivalence ciass is an interval, and since $G$ is open, each is in fact an open interval. Thus $G$ is a disjoint union of open (nonempty) intervals, and there can be only countably many of them, since each contains a rational.

A13. The simplest form of the Heine-Borel theorem says that if $[a, b] \subset \bigcup_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(a_{k}, b_{k}\right)$, then $[a, b] \subset \bigcup_{k=1}^{n}\left(a_{k}, b_{k}\right]$ for some $n$. A set $A$ is defined to be compact if each cover of it by open sets has a ninite subcover--that is, if [ $G_{\theta}: \theta \in \Theta$ ] covers $A$ and each $G_{\theta}$ is open, then some finite subcollection $\left\{G_{\theta_{1}}, \ldots, G_{\theta_{n}}\right\}$ covers $A$. Equivalent to the Heine-Borel theorem is the assertion that a bounded, closed set is compact. Also equivalent is the assertion that every bounded sequence of real numbers has a convergent subsequence.

A14. The diagonal method. From this last fact follows one of the basic principles of analysis.

Theorem. Suppose that each row of the array

$$
\begin{array}{cccc}
x_{1,1} & x_{1,2} & x_{1,3} & \cdots  \tag{1}\\
x_{2,1} & x_{2,2} & x_{2,3} & \cdots \\
\vdots & \vdots & \vdots &
\end{array}
$$

is a bounded sequence of real numbers. Then there exists an increasing sequence $n_{1}, n_{2}, \ldots$ of integers such that the limit $\lim _{k} x_{r, n_{k}}$ exists for $r=1,2, \ldots$.

Proof. From the first row, select a convergent subsequence

$$
\begin{equation*}
x_{1, n_{1,1}}, x_{1, n_{12}}, x_{1, n_{13}}, \ldots ; \tag{2}
\end{equation*}
$$

here ( $n_{1, k}$ ) is an increasing sequence of integers and $\lim _{k} x_{1, n_{1, k}}$ exists. Look next at the second row of (1) along the sequence $n_{1,1}, n_{1,2}, \ldots$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
x_{2 n_{1,1}}, x_{2, n_{12}}, x_{2, n_{1}}, \ldots . \tag{3}
\end{equation*}
$$

As a subsequence of the second row of (1), (3) is bounded. Select from it a convergent subsequence

$$
x_{2, n_{2} 1}, x_{2, n_{2},}, x_{2, n_{2},}, \ldots ;
$$

here $\left\{n_{2, k}\right\}$ is an increasing sequence of integers, a subsequence of $\left\{n_{1, k}\right\}$, and $\lim _{k} x_{2, n_{2} \alpha}$ exists.

Continue inductively in the same way. This gives an array

$$
\begin{array}{cccc}
n_{1,1} & n_{1,2} & n_{1,3} & \cdots  \tag{4}\\
n_{2,1} & n_{2,2} & n_{2,3} & \cdots \\
\vdots & \vdots & \vdots &
\end{array}
$$

with three properties: (i) Each row of (4) is an increasing sequence of integers. (ii) The $r$ th row is a subsequence of the ( $r-1$ )st. (iii) For each $r, \lim _{k} x_{r, n_{r k}}$ exists. Thus

$$
\begin{equation*}
x_{r, n_{r}, 1}, x_{r, n_{r}, 2}, x_{r, n, 1}, \cdot \tag{5}
\end{equation*}
$$

is a convergent subsequence of the $r$ th row of (1).
Put $n_{k}=n_{k, k}$. Since each row of (4) is increasing and is contained in the preceding row, $n_{1}, n_{2}, n_{3}, \ldots$ is an increasing sequence of integers. Furrhermore, $n_{r}, n_{r+1}, n_{r+2}, \ldots$ is a subsequence of the $r$ th row of (4). Thus $x_{r, n}, x_{r, n_{r+1}}, x_{r, n_{r+2}}, \ldots$ is a subsequence of (5) and is therefore convergent. Thus $\lim _{k} x_{r, n_{k}}$ does exist. $\square$

Since $\left(n_{k}\right)$ is the diagonal of the array (4), application of this theorem is called the diagonal method.

A15. The set $A$ is by definition derse in the set $B$ if for each $x$ in $B$ and each open interval $J$ containing $x, J$ meets $A$. This is the same thing as requiring $B \subset A^{-}$. The set $E$ is by definition nowhere dense if each open interval $I$ contains some open interval $J$ that does not meet $E$. This makes sense: It $I$ contains an open interval $J$ that does not meet $E$, then $E$ is not dense in $I$; the definition requires that $E$ be dense in no interval $I$.

A set $A$ is defined to be perfect if it is closed and for each $x$ in $A$ and positive $\epsilon$, there is a $y$ in $A$ such that $0<|x-y|<\epsilon$. An equivalent requirement is that $A$ be closed and for each $x$ in $A$ there exist a sequence $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$ in $A$ such that $x_{n} \neq x$ and $x_{n} \rightarrow x$. The Cantor set is uncountable, nowhere dense, and perfect.

A set that is nowhere dense is in a sense small. If $A$ is a countable union of sets each of which is nowhere dense, then $A$ is said to be of the first category. This is a weaker notion of smallness. A set that is not of the first category is said to be of the second category.

## Euclidean $\boldsymbol{k}$-Space

A16. Euclidean space of dimension $k$ is denoted $R^{k}$. Points ( $a_{1}, \ldots, a_{k}$ ) and ( $b_{1}, \ldots, b_{k}$ ) determine open, closed, and half-open rectangles in $R^{k}$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
& {\left[x: a_{i}<x_{i}<b_{i}, i=1, \ldots, k\right],} \\
& {\left[x: a_{i} \leq x_{i} \leq b_{i}, i=1, \ldots, k\right],} \\
& {\left[x: a_{i}<x_{i} \leq b_{i}, i=1, \ldots, k\right] .}
\end{aligned}
$$

A rectangle (without a qualifying adjective) is in this book a set of this last form.
The Euclidean distance $\left(\sum_{i=1}^{k}\left(x_{i}-y_{i}\right)^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}$ between $x=\left(x_{1}, \ldots, x_{k}\right)$ and $y=$ ( $y_{1}, \ldots, y_{k}$ ) is denoted by $|x-y|$.

A17. All the concepts in A11 carry over to $R^{k}$ : simply take the $I$ there to be an open rectangle in $R^{k}$. The definition of compact set also carries over word for word, and the Heine-Borel theorem in $R^{k}$ says that a closed, bounded set is compact.

## Analysis

A18. The standard Landau notation is used. Suppose that $\left\{x_{n}\right\}$ and $\left\{y_{n}\right\}$ are real sequences and $y_{n}>0$. Then $x_{n}=O\left(y_{n}\right)$ means $x_{n} / y_{n}$ is bounded; $x_{n}=o\left(y_{n}\right)$ means $x_{n} / y_{n} \rightarrow 0 ; x_{n} \sim y_{n}$ means $x_{n} / y_{n} \rightarrow 1 ; x_{n}=y_{n}$ means $x_{n} / y_{n}$ and $y_{n} / x_{n}$ are both bounded. To write $x_{n}=z_{n}+O\left(y_{n}\right)$, for example, means that $x_{n}=z_{n}+u_{n}$ for some ( $u_{n}$ ) satisfying $u_{n}=O\left(y_{n}\right)$-that is, that $x_{n}-z_{n}=O\left(y_{n}\right)$.

A19. A difference equation. Suppose that $a$ and $b$ are integers and $a<b$. Suppose that $x_{n}$ is defined for $a \leq n \leq b$ and satisfies

$$
\begin{equation*}
x_{n}=p x_{n+1}+q x_{n-1} \quad \text { for } a<n<b, \tag{6}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $p$ and $q$ are positive and $p+q=1$. The general solution of this difference equation has the form

$$
x_{n}=\left\{\begin{array}{lll}
A+B(q / p)^{n} & \text { for } a \leq n \leq b & \text { if } p \neq q,  \tag{7}\\
A+B n & \text { for } a \leq n \leq b & \text { if } p=q .
\end{array}\right.
$$

That (7) always solves (6) is easily checked. Suppose the values $x_{n_{1}}$ and $x_{n_{2}}$ are given, where $a \leq n_{1}<n_{2} \leq b$. If $p \neq q$, the system

$$
A+B(q / p)^{n_{1}}=x_{n_{1}}, \quad A+B(q / p)^{n_{2}}=x_{n_{2}}
$$

can always be solved for $A$ and $B$. If $p=q$, the system

$$
A+B n_{1}=x_{n_{1}}, \quad A+B n_{2}=x_{n_{2}}
$$

can always be solved. Take $n_{1}=a$ and $n_{2}=a+1$; the corresponding $A$ and $B$ satisfy (7) for $n=a$ and for $n=a+1$, and it follows by induction that (7) holds for all $n$. Thus any solution of (6) can indeed be put in the form (7). Furthermore. the equation (6) and any pair of values $x_{n_{1}}$ and $x_{n_{2}}\left(n_{1} \neq n_{2}\right)$ suffice to determine all the $x_{n}$.

If $x_{n}$ is defined for $a \leq n<\infty$ and satisfies (6) for $a<n<\infty$, then there are constants $A$ and $B$ such that (7) holds for $a \leq n<\infty$.

## A20. Cauchy's equation.

Theorem. Let $f$ be a real function on ( $0, \infty$ ), and suppose that $f$ satisfies Cauchy's equation: $f(x+y)=f(x)+f(y)$ for $x, y>0$. If there is some interval on which $f$ is bounded above, then $f(x)=x f(1)$ for $x>0$.

Proof. The problem is to prove that $g(x)=f(x)-x f(1)$ vanishes identically. Clearly, $g(1)=0$, and $g$ satisfies Cauchy's equation and on some interval is bounded above. By induction, $g(n x)=n g(x)$; hence $n g(m / n)=g(m)=m g(1)=0$, so that $g(r)=0$ for positive rational $r$. Suppose that $g\left(x_{0}\right) \neq 0$ for some $x_{0}$. If $g\left(x_{0}\right)<0$, then $g\left(r_{0}-x_{0}\right)=-g\left(x_{0}\right)>0$ for rational $r_{0}>x_{0}$. It is thus no restriction to assume that $g\left(x_{0}\right)>0$. Let $I$ be an open interval in which $g$ is bounded above. Given a number $M$, choose $n$ so that $n g\left(x_{0}\right)>M$, and then choose a rational $r$ so that $n x_{0}+r$ lies in $I$. If $r>0$, then $g\left(r+n x_{0}\right)=g(r)+g\left(n x_{0}\right)=g\left(n x_{0}\right)=n g\left(x_{0}\right)$. If $r<0$, then $n g\left(x_{0}\right)=g\left(n x_{0}\right)=g\left((-r)+\left(n x_{0}+r\right)\right)=g(-r)+g\left(n x_{0}+r\right)=g\left(n x_{0}+r\right)$. In either
case, $\left.g\left(n x_{0}\right)+r\right)=n g\left(x_{0}\right)$; of course this is trivial if $r=0$. Since $g\left(n x_{0}+r\right)= n g\left(x_{0}\right)>M$ and $M$ was arbitrary, $g$ is not bounded above in $I$, a contradiction.

Obviously, the same proof works if $f$ is bounded below in some interval.

Corollary. Let $U$ be a real function on $(0, \infty)$ and suppose that $U(x+y)=U(x) U(y)$ for $x, y>0$. Suppose further that there is some interval on which $U$ is bounded above. Then either $U(x)=0$ for $x>0$, or else there is an A such that $U(x)=e^{A x}$ for $x>0$.

Proof. Since $U(x)=U^{2}(x / 2), U$ is nonnegative. If $U(x)=0$, then $U\left(x / 2^{n}\right)=0$ and so $U$ vanishes at points arbitrarily near 0 . If $U$ vanishes at a point, it must by the functional equation vanish everywhere to the right of that point. Hence $U$ is identically 0 or else everywhere positive.

In the latter case, the theorem applies to $f(x)=\log U(x)$, this function being bounded above in some interval, and so $f(x)=A x$ for $A=\log U(1)$.

A21. A number-theoretic fact.

Theorem. Suppose that $M$ is a set of positive integers ciosed under addition and that $M$ has greatest common divisor 1 . Then $M$ contains all integers exceeding some $n_{0}$.

Proof. Let $M_{1}$ consist of all the integers $m,-m$, and $m-m^{\prime}$ with $m$ and $m^{\prime}$ in $M$. Then $M_{1}$ is closed under addition and subtraction (it is a subgroup of the group of integers). Let $d$ be the smallest positive element of $M_{1}$. If $n \in M_{1}$, write $n=q d+r$, where $0 \leq r<d$. Since $r=n-q d$ lies in $M_{1}, r$ must actually be 0 . Thus $M_{1}$ consists of the multiples of $d$. Since $d$ divides all the integers in $M_{1}$ and hence all the integers in $M$, and since $M$ has greatest common divisor $1, d=1$. Thus $M_{1}$ contains all the integers.

Write $1=m-m^{\prime}$ with $m$ and $m^{\prime}$ in $M$ (if 1 itself is in $M$, the proof is easy), and take $n_{0}=\left(m+m^{i}\right)^{2}$. Given $n>n_{0}$, write $n=q\left(m+m^{\prime}\right)+r$, where $0 \leq r<m+m^{\prime}$. From $n>n_{0} \geq(r+1)\left(m+m^{\prime}\right)$ follows $q=(n-r) /\left(m+m^{\prime}\right)>r$. But $n= q\left(m+m^{\prime}\right)+r\left(m-m^{\prime}\right)=(q+r) m+(q-r) m^{\prime}$, and since $q+r \geq q-r>0$, $n$ lies in $M$.

A22. One- and two-sided derivatives.

Theorem. Suppose that $f$ and $g$ are continuous on $[0, \infty)$ and $g$ is the right-hand derivative of $f$ on $(0, \infty): f^{+}(t)=g(t)$ for $t>0$. Then $f^{+}(0)=g(0)$ as well, and $g$ is the two-sided derivative of $f$ on $(0, \infty)$.

Proof. It suffices to show that $F(t)=f(t)-f(0)-\int_{0}^{t} g(s) d s$ vanishes for $t \geq 0$. By assumption, $F$ is continuous on $[0, \infty)$ and $F^{+}(t)=0$ for $t>0$. Suppose that $F\left(t_{0}\right)>F\left(t_{1}\right)$, where $0<t_{0}<t_{1}$. Then $G(t)=F(t)-\left(t-t_{0}\right)\left(F\left(t_{1}\right)-F\left(t_{0}\right)\right) /\left(t_{1}-t_{0}\right)$ is continuous on $[0, \infty), G\left(t_{0}\right)=G\left(t_{1}\right)$, and $G^{+}(t)>0$ on $(0, \infty)$. But then the maximum of $G$ over $\left[t_{0}, t_{1}\right]$ must occur at some interior point; since $G^{+} \leq 0$ at a local maximum, this is impossible. Similarly $F\left(t_{0}\right)<F\left(t_{1}\right)$ is impossible. Thus $F$ is constant over $(0, \infty)$ and by continuity is constant over $[0, \infty)$. Since $F(0)=0, F$ vanishes on $[0, \infty)$.

A23. A differential equation. The equation $f^{\prime}(t)=A f(t)+g(t)(t \geq 0 ; g$ continuous $)$ has the particular solution $f_{0}(t)=e^{A t} \int_{0}^{t} g(s) e^{-A s} d s$; for an arbitrary solution $f$, $\left(f(t)-f_{0}(t)\right) e^{-A t}$ has derivative 0 and hence equals $f(0)$ identically. All solutions thus have the form $f(t)=e^{A t}\left[f(0)+\int_{0}^{t} g(s) e^{-A s} d s\right]$.

A24. A trigonometric identity. If $z \neq 1$ and $z \neq 0$, then

$$
\sum_{k=-l}^{l} z^{k}=z^{-l} \sum_{k=0}^{2 l} z^{k}=z^{-l} \frac{1-z^{2 l+1}}{1-z}=\frac{z^{-l}-z^{l+1}}{1-z},
$$

and hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{l=0}^{m-1} \sum_{k=-l}^{l} z^{k} & =\sum_{l=0}^{m-1} \frac{z^{-l}-z^{l+1}}{1-z}=\frac{1}{1-z}\left[\frac{1-z^{-m}}{1-z^{-1}}-z \frac{1-z^{m}}{1-z}\right] \\
& =\frac{1-z^{-m}+1-z^{m}}{(1-z)\left(1-z^{-1}\right)}=\frac{\left(z^{m / 2}-z^{-m / 2}\right)^{2}}{\left(z^{1 / 2}-z^{-1 / 2}\right)^{2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Take $z=e^{i x}$. If $x$ is not an integral multiple of $2 \pi$, then

$$
\sum_{l=0}^{m-1} \sum_{k=-l}^{l} e^{i k x}=\frac{\left(\sin \frac{1}{2} m x\right)^{2}}{\left(\sin \frac{1}{2} x\right)^{2}} .
$$

If $x=2 \pi n$, the left-hand side here is $m^{2}$, which is the limit of the right-hand side as $x \rightarrow 2 \pi n$.

## Infinite Series

A25. Nonnegative series. Suppose $x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots$ are nonnegative. If $E$ is a finite set of integers, then $E \subset\{1,2, \ldots, n\}$ for some $n$, so that by nonnegativity $\sum_{k \in E} x_{k} \leq \sum_{k=1}^{n} x_{k}$. The set of partial sums $\sum_{k=1}^{n} x_{k}$ thus has the same supremum as the larger set of sums $\sum_{k \in E} x_{k}$ ( $E$ finite). Therefore, the nonnegative series $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{k}$ converges if and only if the sums $\sum_{k \in E} x_{k}$ for finite $E$ are bounded, in which case the sum is the supremum: $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{k}=\sup _{E} \sum_{k \in E} x_{k}$.

A26. Dirichlet's theorem. Since the supremum in A25 is invariant under permutations, so is $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{k}$ : If the $x_{k}$ are nonnegative and $y_{k}=x_{f(k)}$ for some one-to one map $f$ of the positive integers onto themselves, then $\sum_{k} x_{k}$ and $\sum_{k} y_{k}$ diverge or converge together and in the latter case have the same sum.

A27. Double series. Suppose that $x_{i j}, i, j=1,2, \ldots$, are nonnegative. The $i$ th row gives a series $\sum_{j} x_{i j}$, and if each of these converges, one can form the series $\sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j}$. Let the terms $x_{i j}$ be arranged in some order as a single infinite series $\sum_{i j} x_{i j}$; by Dirichlet's theorem, the sum is the same whatever order is used.

Suppose each $\sum_{j} x_{i j}$ converges and $\sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j}$ converges. If $E$ is a finite set of the pairs ( $i, j$ ), there is an $n$ for which $\sum_{(i, j) \in E} x_{i j} \leq \sum_{i \leq n} \sum_{j \leq n} x_{i j} \leq \sum_{i \leq n} \sum_{j} x_{i j} \leq \sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j}$; hence $\Sigma_{i j} x_{i j}$ converges and has sum at most $\Sigma_{i} \Sigma_{j} x_{i j}$. On the other hand, if $\Sigma_{i j} x_{i j}$ converges, then $\sum_{i \leq m} \sum_{j \leq n} x_{i j} \leq \sum_{i j} x_{i j}$; letting $n \rightarrow \infty$ and then $m \rightarrow \infty$ shows that each $\sum_{j} x_{i j}$ converges and that $\sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j} \leq \sum_{i j} x_{i j}$. Therefore, in the nonnegative case, $\Sigma_{i j} x_{i j}$ converges if and only if the $\Sigma_{j} x_{i j}$ all converge and $\Sigma_{i} \Sigma_{j} x_{i j}$ converges, in which case $\sum_{i j} x_{i j}=\sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j}$.

By symmetry, $\sum_{i j} x_{i j}=\sum_{i} \sum_{i} x_{i j}$. Thus the order of summation can be reversed in a nonnegative double series: $\sum_{i} \sum_{j} x_{i j}=\sum_{j} \sum_{i} x_{i j}$.

A28. The Weierstrass M-test.

Theorem. Suppose that $\lim _{n} x_{n k}=x_{k}$ for each $k$ and that $\left|x_{n k}\right| \leq M_{k}$, where $\sum_{k} M_{k}<\infty$. Then $\sum_{k} x_{k}$ and all the $\sum_{k} x_{n k}$ converge, and $\lim _{n} \sum_{k} x_{n k}=\sum_{k} x_{k}$.

Proof. The series of course converge absolutely, since $\sum_{k} M_{k}<\infty$. Now $\left|\sum_{k} x_{n k}-\sum_{k} x_{k}\right| \leq \sum_{k \leq k_{0}}\left|x_{n k}-x_{k}\right|+2 \sum_{k>k_{0}} M_{k}$. Given $\epsilon$, choose $k_{0}$ so that $\sum_{k>k_{0}} M_{k}<\epsilon / 3$, and then choose $n_{0}$ so that $n>n_{0}$ implies $\left|x_{n k}-x_{k}\right|<\epsilon / 3 k_{0}$ for $k \leq k_{0}$. Then $n>n_{0}$ implies $\left|\sum_{k} x_{n k}-\sum_{k} x_{k}\right|<\epsilon$.

A29. Power series. The principal fact needed is this: If $f(x)=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} a_{k} x^{k}$ converges in the range $|x|<r$, then it is differentiable there and

$$
\begin{equation*}
f^{\prime}(x)=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} k a_{k} x^{k-1} \tag{8}
\end{equation*}
$$

For a simple proof, choose $r_{0}$ and $r_{1}$ so that $|x|<r_{0}<r_{1}<r$. If $|h|<r_{0}-|x|$, so that $|x \pm h|<r_{0}$, then the mean-value theorem gives (here $0 \leq \theta_{h} \leq 1$ )

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|\frac{(x+h)^{k}-x^{h}}{h}-k x^{k-1}\right|=\left|k\left(x+\theta_{h} h\right)^{k-1}-k x^{k-1}\right| \leq 2 k r_{0}^{k-1} . \tag{9}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $2 k r_{0}^{k-1} / r_{1}^{k}$ goes to 0 , it is bounded by some $M$, and if $M_{k}=\left|a_{k}\right| \cdot M r_{1}^{k}$, then $\sum_{k} M_{k}<\infty$ and $\left|a_{k}\right|$ times the left member of (9) is at most $M_{k}$ for $|h|<r_{0}-|x|$. By the $M$-test [A28] (applied with $h \rightarrow 0$ instead of $n \rightarrow \infty$ ),

$$
\lim _{h \rightarrow 0} \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} a_{k} \frac{(x+h)^{k}-x^{k}}{h}=\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} k a_{k} x^{k-1} .
$$

Hence (8).
Repeated application of (8) gives

$$
f^{(j)}(x)=\sum_{k=j}^{\infty} k(k-1) \cdots(k-j+1) a_{k} x^{k-j} .
$$

For $x=0$, this is $a_{j}=f^{(j)}(0) / j!$, the formula for the coefficients in a Taylor series. This shows in particular that the values of $f(x)$ for $|x|<r$ determine the coefficients $a_{k}$.

A30. Cesàro averages. If $x_{n} \rightarrow x$, then $n^{-1} \sum_{k=1}^{n} x_{k} \rightarrow x$. To prove this, let $M$ bound $\left|x_{k}\right|$, and given $\epsilon$, choose $k_{0}$ so that $\left|x-x_{k}\right|<\epsilon / 2$ for $k \geq k_{0}$. If $n>k_{0}$ and $n>4 k_{0} M / \epsilon$, then

$$
\left|x-\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{n} x_{k}\right| \leq \frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=1}^{k_{0}-1} 2 M+\frac{1}{n} \sum_{k=k_{0}}^{n} \frac{\epsilon}{2}<\epsilon .
$$

A31. Dyadic expansions. Define a mapping $T$ of the unit interval $\Omega=(0,1]$ into itself by

$$
T \omega= \begin{cases}2 \omega & \text { if } 0<\omega \leq \frac{1}{2} \\ 2 \omega-1 & \text { if } \frac{1}{2}<\omega \leq 1\end{cases}
$$

Define a function $d_{1}$ on $\Omega$ by

$$
d_{1}(\omega)= \begin{cases}0 & \text { if } 0<\omega \leq \frac{1}{2}, \\ 1 & \text { if } \frac{1}{2}<\omega \leq 1,\end{cases}
$$

and let $d_{i}(\omega)=d_{1}\left(T^{i-1} \omega\right)$. Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sum_{i=1}^{n} \frac{d_{i}(\omega)}{2^{i}}<\omega \leq \sum_{i=1}^{n} \frac{d_{i}(\omega)}{2^{i}}+\frac{1}{2^{n}} \tag{10}
\end{equation*}
$$

for all $\omega \in \omega$ and $n \geq 1$. To verify this for $n=1$, check the cases $\omega \leq \frac{1}{2}$ and $\omega>\frac{1}{2}$ separately. Suppose that (10) holds for a particular $n$ and for all $\omega$. Replace $\omega$ by $T \omega$ in (10) and use the fact that $d_{i}(T \omega)=d_{i+1}(\omega)$; separate consideration of the cases $\omega \leq \frac{1}{2}$ and $\omega>\frac{1}{2}$ now shows that (10) holds with $n+1$ in place of $n$

Thus (10) holds for all $n$ and $\omega$, and it follows that $\omega=\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} d_{i}(\omega) / 2^{i}$. This gives the dyadic representation of $\omega$. If $d_{i}(\omega)=0$ for all $i>n$, then $\omega=\sum_{i=1}^{n} d_{i}(\omega) / 2^{i}$, which contradicts the left-hand inequality in (10). Thus the expansion does not terminate in 0 's.

## Convex Functions

A32. A function $\varphi$ on an open interval $I$ (bounded or unbounded) is convex if

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(t x+(1-t) y) \leq t \varphi(x)+(1-t) \varphi(y) \tag{11}
\end{equation*}
$$

for $x, y \in I$ and $0 \leq t \leq 1$. From this it follows by induction that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi\left(\sum_{i=1}^{n} p_{i} x_{i}\right) \leq \sum_{i=1}^{n} p_{i} \varphi\left(x_{i}\right) \tag{12}
\end{equation*}
$$

if the $x_{i}$ lie in $I$ and the $p_{i}$ are nonnegative and add to 1 .
If $\varphi$ has a continuous, nondecreasing derivative $\varphi^{\prime}$ on $I$, then $\varphi$ is convex. Indeed, if $a<b<c$, the average of $\varphi^{\prime}$ over ( $a, b$ ) is at most the average over ( $b, c$ ):

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\varphi(b)-\varphi(a)}{b-a} & =\frac{1}{b-a} \int_{a}^{b} \varphi^{\prime}(s) d s \leq \varphi^{\prime}(b) \leq \frac{1}{c-b} \int_{b}^{c} \varphi^{\prime}(s) d s \\
& =\frac{\varphi(c)-\varphi(b)}{c-b}
\end{aligned}
$$

The inequality between the extreme terms here reduces to

$$
\begin{equation*}
(c-a) \varphi(b) \leq(c-b) \varphi(a)+(b-a) \varphi(c), \tag{13}
\end{equation*}
$$

which is (11) with $x=a, y=c, t=(c-b) /(c-a)$.
A33. Geometrically, (11) means that the point $B$ in Figure (i) lies on or below the chord $A C$. But then slope $A B \leq$ slope $A C$; algebraically, this is $(\varphi(b)-\varphi(a)) /(b-a) \leq(\varphi(c)-\varphi(a)) /(c-a)$, which is the same as (13). As $B$ moves to $A$ from the right,
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-075.jpg?height=471&width=1551&top_left_y=237&top_left_x=136)
slope $A B$ is thus nonincreasing and hence has a limit. In other words, $\varphi$ has a right-hand derivative $\varphi^{+}$. Figure (ii) shows that slope $D E \leq$ slope $E F \leq$ slope $F G$. Let $E$ move to $D$ from the right and let $G$ move to $F$ from the right: The right-hand derivative at $D$ is at most that at $F$, and $\varphi^{+}$is nondecreasing. Since the slope of $X Y$ in Figure (iii) is at least as great as the right-hand derivative at $X$, the curve to the right of $X$ lies on or above the line through $X$ with slope $\varphi^{+}(x)$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(y) \geq \varphi(x)+(y-x) \varphi^{+}(x), \quad y \geq x . \tag{14}
\end{equation*}
$$

Figure (iii) also makes it clear that $\varphi$ is right-continuous.
Similarly, $\varphi$ has a nondecreasing left-hand derivative $\varphi^{-}$and is left-continuous. Since slope $A B \leq$ slope $B C$ in Figure (i), $\varphi^{-}(b) \leq \varphi^{\urcorner}(b)$. Since clearly $\varphi^{+}(b)<\infty$ and $-\infty<\varphi^{-}(b), \varphi^{+}$and $\varphi^{-}$are finite. Finally, (14) and its right-sided analogue show that the curve lies on or above each line through $Z$ in Figure (iv) having slope between $\varphi^{-}(z)$ and $\varphi^{+}(z)$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\varphi(x) \geq \varphi(z)+m(x-z), \quad \varphi^{-}(z) \leq m \leq \varphi^{+}(z) . \tag{15}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is a support line.

## Some Multivariable Calculus

A34. Suppose that $U$ is an open set in $R^{k}$ and $T: U \rightarrow R^{k}$ is continuously differentiable; let $D_{x}=\left[t_{i j}(x)\right]$ and $J(x)=\operatorname{det} D_{x}$ be the Jacobian matrix and determinant, as in Theorem 17.2. Let $Q^{-}$be a closed rectangle in $U$.

Theorem. If $\left|t_{i j}\left(x^{\prime}\right)-t_{i j}(x)\right| \leq \alpha$ for $x, x^{\prime} \in Q^{-}$and all $i, j$, then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|T x^{\prime}-T x-D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)\right| \leq k^{2} \alpha\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|, \quad x, x^{\prime} \in Q . \tag{16}
\end{equation*}
$$

Before proceeding to the proof, note that, since the $t_{i j}$ are continuous, $\alpha$ can be taken to go to 0 as $Q$ contracts to the point $x$. In other words, (16) implies

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lim _{x^{\prime} \rightarrow x} \frac{\left|T x^{\prime}-T x-D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)\right|}{\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|}=0 . \tag{17}
\end{equation*}
$$

This shows that $D_{x}$ acts as a multivariable derivative. Suppose on the other hand that (17) holds at $x$ for an initially unspecified matrix $D_{x}$. Take $x_{j}^{\prime}=x_{j}+h$ and $x_{l}^{\prime}=x_{l}$ for $l \neq j$, and let $h$ go to 0 . It follows that the entires of $D_{x}$ must be the partial derivatives $t_{i j}(x)$; If (17) holds, then $D_{x}$ must be the Jacobian matrix.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-076.jpg?height=500&width=473&top_left_y=244&top_left_x=173)
(v)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/c4bc0196-7bad-4ed2-84c8-094ffab65583-076.jpg?height=323&width=213&top_left_y=241&top_left_x=885)

(vi)

Proof of (16). For $j=0,1, \ldots, k$, let $z^{j}$ agree with $x^{\prime}$ in the first $j$ places and with $x$ in the last $k-j$ places. Then $z^{0}=x, z^{k}=x^{\prime}$, and $\left|z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right|=\left|\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)_{j}\right|= \left|x_{j}^{\prime}-x_{j}\right|$ (Figure (v)). By the mean-value theorem in one dimension, there is a point $w^{i j}$ on the segment from $z^{j-1}$ to $z^{j}$ such that $t_{i}\left(z^{j}\right)-t_{i}\left(z^{j-1}\right)=t_{i j}\left(w^{i j}\right)\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)_{j}$. Since

$$
\left(D_{x}\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)\right)_{i}=\sum_{l} t_{i l}(x)\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)_{l}=t_{i j}(x)\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)_{j},
$$

it follows that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mid T x^{\prime} & -T x-D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right) \mid \\
& \leq \sum_{i j}\left|t_{i}\left(z^{j}\right)-t_{i}\left(z^{j-1}\right)-\left(D_{x}\left(z^{j}-z^{\prime-1}\right)\right)_{i}\right| \\
& =\sum_{i j}\left|t_{i j}\left(w^{i j}\right)-t_{i j}(x)\right| \cdot\left|\left(z^{j}-z^{j-1}\right)_{j}\right| \\
& \leq \sum_{i j} \alpha\left|x_{j}^{\prime}-x_{j}\right| \leq k^{2} \alpha\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|
\end{aligned}
$$ $\square$

A35. The multivariable inverse-function theorem. Let $x_{0}$ be a point of the open set $U$.
Theorem. If $J\left(x_{0}\right) \neq 0$, then there are open sets $X$ and $Y$, containing $x_{0}$ and $y_{0}=T x_{0}$, respectively, such that $T$ is a one-to-one map from $X$ onto $Y=T X$; further, $T^{-1}: Y \rightarrow X$ is continuously differentiable, and the Jacobian matrix of $T^{-1}$ at $y$ is $D_{T^{-1} y}^{-1}$.

This is a local theorem. It is not assumed, as in Theorem 17.2, that $T$ is one-to-one on $U$ and $J(x)$ never vanishes; but under those additional conditions, $T U$ is open and the inverse point mapping is continuously differentiable. To understand the role of the condition $J\left(x_{0}\right) \neq 0$, consider the case where $k=1, x_{0}=0$, and $T x$ is $x^{2}$ or $x^{3}$.

Proof. Let $Q$ be a rectangle such that $x_{0} \in Q^{\circ} \subset Q^{-} \subset U$ and $J(x) \neq 0$ for $x \in Q^{-}$. As $(x, u)$ ranges over the compact set $Q^{-} \times[u:|u|=1],\left|D_{x} u\right|$ is bounded below by some positive $\beta$ :

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|D_{x} u\right| \geq \beta|u| \quad \text { if } x \in Q^{-}, u \in R^{k} . \tag{18}
\end{equation*}
$$

Making $Q$ smaller will ensure that $\left|t_{i j}(x)-t_{i j}\left(x^{\prime}\right)\right| \leq \beta / 2 k^{2}$ for all $x$ and $x^{\prime}$ in $Q^{-}$ and all $i, j$. Then (16) and (18) give, for $x, x^{\prime} \in Q^{-}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|T x^{\prime}-T x\right| & \geq\left|D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)\right|-\left|T x^{\prime}-T x-D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)\right| \\
& \geq\left|D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)\right|-\frac{1}{2} \beta\left|x^{\prime}-x\right| \geq \frac{1}{2} \beta\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus

$$
\begin{equation*}
\left|x^{\prime}-x\right| \leq \frac{2}{\beta}\left|T x^{\prime}-T x\right| \quad \text { for } x, x^{\prime} \in Q^{-} . \tag{19}
\end{equation*}
$$

This shows that $T$ is one-to-one on $Q^{-}$.
Since $x_{0}$ does not lie in the compact set $\partial Q, \inf _{x \in \partial Q}\left|T x-T x_{0}\right|=d>0$. Let $Y$ be the open ball with center $y_{0}=T x_{0}$ and radius $d / 2$ (Figure (vi)). Fix a $y$ in $Y$. The problem is to show that $y=T x$ for some $x$ in $Q^{\circ}$, which means finding an $x$ such that $\varphi(x)=|y-T x|^{2}=\sum_{i}\left(y_{i}-t_{i}(x)\right)^{2}$ vanishes. By compactness, the minimum of $\varphi$ on $Q^{-}$is achieved there. If $x \in \partial Q$ (and $y \in Y$ ), then $2\left|y-y_{0}\right|<d \leq\left|T x-y_{0}\right| \leq \mid T x- y\left|+\left|y-y_{0}\right|\right.$, so that $| y-T x_{0}\left|<|y-T x|\right.$. Therefore, $\varphi\left(x_{0}\right)<\varphi(x)$ for $x \in \partial Q$, and so the minimum occurs in $Q^{\circ}$ rather than on $\partial Q$. At the minimizing point, $\partial \varphi / \partial x_{j}= -\sum_{i} 2\left(y_{i}-t_{i}(x)\right) t_{i j}(x)=0$, and since $D_{x}$ is nonsingular, it follows that $y=T x$ : Each $y$ in $Y$ is the image under $T$ of some point $x$ in $Q^{\circ}$. By (19), this $x$ is unique (although it is possible that $y=T z$ for some $z$ outside $Q$ ).

Let $X=Q^{\circ} \cap T^{-1} Y$. Then $X$ is open and $T$ is a one-to-one map of $X$ onto $Y$. Now let $T^{-!}$denote the inverse point transformation on $Y$. By (19), $T^{-1}$ is continuous.

To prove differentiability, consider in $Y$ a fixed point $y$ and a variable point $y^{\prime}$ such that $y^{\prime} \rightarrow y$ and $y^{\prime} \neq y$. Let $x=T^{-1} y$ and $x^{\prime}=T^{-1} y^{\prime}$; then $x^{\prime}$ is a function of $y^{\prime}$, $x^{\prime} \rightarrow x$, and $x^{\prime} \neq x$. Define $v$ by $T x^{\prime}-T x=D_{x}\left(x^{\prime}-x\right)+v$; then $v$ is a function of $x^{\prime}$ and hence of $y^{\prime}$, and $|v| /\left|x^{\prime}-x\right| \rightarrow 0$ by (17). Apply $D_{x}^{-1}: D_{x}^{-1}\left(T x^{\prime}-T x\right)=x^{\prime}-x+ D_{x}^{-1} v$, or $T^{-1} y^{\prime}-T^{-1} y=D_{x}^{-1}\left(y^{\prime}-y\right)-D_{x}^{-1} v$. By (18) and (19),

$$
\frac{\left|T^{-1} y^{\prime}-T^{-1} y-D_{x}^{-1}\left(y^{\prime}-y\right)\right|}{\left|y^{\prime}-y\right|}=\frac{\left|D_{x}^{-1} v\right|}{\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|} \cdot \frac{\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|}{\left|y^{\prime}-y\right|} \leq \frac{|v| / \beta}{\left|x^{\prime}-x\right|} \cdot \frac{2}{\beta} .
$$

The right side goes to 0 as $y^{\prime} \rightarrow y$.
By the remark following (17), the components of $D_{x}^{-1}$ must be the partial derivatives of the inverse mapping. $T^{-1}$ has Jacobian matrix $D_{T^{-1} y}^{-1}$ at $y$. The components of an inverse matrix vary continuous with the components of the original matrix (think for example of the inverse as specified by the cofactors), and so $T^{-1}$ is even continuously differentiable on $Y$.

## Continued Fractions

A36. In designing a planetarium, Christian Huygens confronted this problem: Given the ratio $x$ of the periods of two planets, approximate it by the ratio of the periods of two linked gears. If one gear has $p$ teeth and the other has $q$, then the ratio of their periods is $p / q$, so that the problem is to approximate the real number $x$ by the rational $p / q$. Of course $x$, being empirical, is already rational, but the numerator and denominator may be so large that gears with those numbers of teeth are not practical: in the approximation $p / q$, both $p$ and $q$ must be of moderate size.

Since the gears play symmetrical roles, there is no more reason to approximate $x$ by $r=p / q$ than there is to approximate $1 / x$ by $1 / r=q / p$. Suppose, to be definite, that $x$ and $r$ lie to the left of 1 . Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
|x-r|=x r\left|\frac{1}{x}-\frac{1}{r}\right| \leq\left|\frac{1}{x}-\frac{1}{r}\right|, \tag{20}
\end{equation*}
$$

and the inequality is strict unless $x=r$ : If $x<1$, it is better to approximate $1 / x$ and then invert the approximation, since that will control both errors.

For a numerical illustration, approximate $x=.127$ by rounding it up to $r=.13$; the calculations (to three places) are

$$
\begin{align*}
r-x & =.13-.127=.003 \\
\frac{1}{x}-\frac{1}{r} & =7.874-7.692=.182 \tag{21}
\end{align*}
$$

The second error is large. So instead, approximate $1 / x=7.874$ by rounding it down to $1 / r^{\prime}=7.87$. Since $1 / 7.87=.1271$ (to four places), the calculations are

$$
\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{x}-\frac{1}{r^{\prime}} & =7.874-7.87=.004  \tag{22}\\
r^{\prime}-x & =.1271-.127=.0001
\end{align*}
$$

This time, both errors are small, and the error .0001 in the new approximation to $x$ is smaller than the corresponding .003 in (21). It is because $x$ lies to the left of 1 that inversion improves the accuracy; see (20).

If this inversion method decreases the error, why not do another inversion in the middle, in finding a rational approximation to $1 / x$ ? It makes no sense to invert $1 / x$ itself, since it lies to the right of 1 (and inversion merely leads back to $x$ anyway). But to approximate $1 / x=7.874$ is to approximate the fractional part .874 , and here a second inversion will help, for the same reason the first one does. This suggests Huygens's iterative procedure.

In modern notation, the scheme is this. For $x$ (rational or irrational) in ( 0,1 ), let $T x=\{1 / x\}$ and $a_{1}(x)=\lfloor 1 / x\rfloor$ be the fractional and integral parts of $1 / x$; and set $T 0=0$. This defines a mapping of $[0,1)$ onto itself:

$$
T x= \begin{cases}\left\{\frac{1}{x}\right\}=\frac{1}{x}-\left\lfloor\frac{1}{x}\right\rfloor=\frac{1}{x}-a_{1}(x) & \text { if } 0<x<1,  \tag{23}\\ 0 & \text { if } x=0 .\end{cases}
$$

Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=\frac{1}{a_{1}(x)+T x} \quad \text { if } 0<x<1 . \tag{24}
\end{equation*}
$$

What (20) says is that replacing $T x$ on the right in (24) by a good rational approximation to it gives an even better rational approximation to $x$ itself.

To carry this further requires a convenient notation. For positive variables $z_{i}$ define the continued fractions

$$
\begin{aligned}
& 1 \sqrt{z_{1}}=\frac{1}{z_{1}}, \quad 1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+1 \sqrt{z_{2}}=\frac{1}{z_{1}+\frac{1}{z_{2}}} \\
& 1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+1 \sqrt{z_{2}}+1 \sqrt{z_{3}}=\frac{1}{z_{1}+\frac{1}{z_{2}+\frac{1}{z_{3}}}}
\end{aligned}
$$

and so on. It is "typographically" clear that

$$
\begin{equation*}
1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{z_{n}}=1 /\left[z_{1}+\left(1 \sqrt{z_{2}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{z_{n}}\right)\right] \tag{25}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{equation*}
\sqrt[1]{z_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{z_{n}}=1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{z_{n-1}+1 / z_{n}} . \tag{26}
\end{equation*}
$$

For a formal theory, use (25) as a recursive definition and then prove (26) by induction (or vice versa). An infinite continued fraction is defined by

$$
1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+1 \sqrt{z_{2}}+\cdots=\lim _{n} 1 \sqrt{z_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{z_{n}},
$$

provided the limit exists. A continued fraction is simple if the $z_{i}$ are positive integers.
If $T^{n-1} x>0$, let $a_{n}(x)=a_{1}\left(T^{n-1} x\right)$; the $a_{n}(x)$ are the partial quotients of $x$. If $x$ and $T x$ are both positive, then (24) applies to each of them:

$$
x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)+T x}=1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{2}(x)+T^{2} x} .
$$

If none of the iterates $x, T x, \ldots, T^{n-1} x$ vanishes, then it follows by induction (use (26)) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n-1}(x)}+1 \sqrt{a_{n}(x)+T^{n} x} . \tag{27}
\end{equation*}
$$

This is an extension of (24), and the idea following (24) extends as well: a good rational approximation to $T^{n} x$ in (27) gives a still better rational approximation to $x$. Even if $T^{n} x$ is approximated very crudely by 0 , there results a sharp approximation

$$
\begin{equation*}
x \approx 1 \sqrt{a_{1}(x)}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n}(x)} \tag{28}
\end{equation*}
$$

to $x$ itself. The right side here is the $n$th convergent to $x$, and it goes very rapidly to $x$; see Section 24.

By the definition (23), $x$ and $T x$ are both rational or both irrational. For an irrational $x$, therefore, $T^{\prime \prime} x$ remains forever among the irrationals, and (27) holds for all $n$. If $x$ is rational, on the other hand, $T^{n} x$ remains forever among the rationals, and in fact, as the following argument show's, $T^{n} x$ eventually hits 0 and stays there.

Suppose that $x$ is a rational in $(0,1): x=d_{1} / d_{0}$, where $0<d_{1}<d_{0}$. If $T x>0$, then $T x=\left\{d_{0} / d_{1}\right\}=d_{2} / d_{1}$, where $0<d_{2}<d_{1}$ because $0<T x<1$. (If $d_{1} / d_{0}$ is irreducible, so is $d_{2} / d_{1}$.) If $T^{2} x>0$, the argument can be repeated:

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=\frac{d_{1}}{d_{0}}, \quad T x=\frac{d_{2}}{d_{1}}, \quad T^{2} x=\frac{d_{3}}{d_{2}}, \quad d_{0}>d_{1}>d_{2}>d_{3}>0 . \tag{29}
\end{equation*}
$$

And so on. Since the $d_{n}$ decrease as long as the $T^{n} x$ remain positive, $T^{n} x$ must vanish for some $n$, and then $T^{m} x=0$ for $m \geq n$. If $n_{x}$ is the smallest integer for which $T^{n} x=0\left(n_{r} \geq 1\right.$ if $\left.x>0\right)$, then by (27),

$$
\begin{equation*}
x = 1 \sqrt { a _ { 1 } ( x ) } + \cdots + 1 \longdiv { a _ { n _ { x } } ( x ) } . \tag{30}
\end{equation*}
$$

Thus each positive rational has a representation as a finite simple continued fraction. If $0<x<1$ and $T x=0$, then $1>x=1 / a_{1}(x)$, so that $a_{1}(x) \geq 2$. Applied to $T^{n_{x}-1} x$, this shows that the $a_{n_{x}}(x)$ in (30) must be at least 2 .

Section 24 requires a uniqueness result. Suppose that

$$
\begin{equation*}
