x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n-1}}+1 \sqrt{a_{n}+t}, \tag{31}
\end{equation*}
$$

where the $a_{i}$ are positive integers and

$$
\begin{equation*}
0<x<1, \quad 0 \leq t<1, \quad a_{n}+t>1 . \tag{32}
\end{equation*}
$$

The last condition rules out $a_{n}=1$ and $t=0$ (which in the case $n=1$ is also ruled out by $x<1$ ). It follows from (31) and (32) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
a_{1}(x)=a_{1}, \ldots, a_{n}(x)=a_{n}, \quad T^{n} x=t . \tag{33}
\end{equation*}
$$

The case $n=1$ being easy, suppose the implication holds for $n-1$, where $n \geq 2$. Since $0<1 /\left(a_{n}+t\right)<1$, the induction hypothesis (use (26)) gives $a_{k}(x)=a_{k}$ for $k<n$ and $T^{n-1} x=1 /\left(a_{n}+t\right)$. Now apply the case $n=1$ to $T^{n-1} x$. (If $a_{n}=1$ and $t=0$, then $a_{k}(x)=a_{k}$ for $k \leq n-2, a_{n-1}(x)=a_{n-1}+1$, and $T^{n-1} x=0$.)

Consider now the infinite case. Assume that

$$
\begin{equation*}
x=1 \sqrt{a_{1}}+1 \sqrt{a_{2}}+\cdots, \tag{34}
\end{equation*}
$$

converges, where the $a_{n}$ are positive integers. Then

$$
\begin{equation*}
a_{n}(x)=a_{n}, \quad T^{n} x=1 \sqrt{a_{n+1}}+1 \sqrt{a_{n+2}}+\cdots, \quad n \geq 1 . \tag{35}
\end{equation*}
$$

To prove this, let $n \rightarrow \infty$ in (25): the continued fraction $t=1 \sqrt{a_{2}}+1 \sqrt{a_{3}}+\cdots$ converges and $x=1 /\left(a_{1}+t\right)$. It follows by induction (use (26)) that

$$
\begin{equation*}
1 \sqrt{a_{1}}>1 \sqrt{a_{1}}+\cdots+1 \sqrt{a_{n}} \geq 1 \sqrt{a_{1}}+1 \sqrt{a_{2}}, \quad n \geq 2 . \tag{36}
\end{equation*}
$$

Hence $0<x<1$, and the same must be true of $t$. Therefore, $a_{1}$ and $t$ are the integer and fractional parts of $1 / x$, which proves (35) for $n=1$. Apply the same argument to
$T x$, and continue. The $x$ defined by (34) is irrational: otherwise, $T^{n} x=0$ for some $n$, which contradicts (35) and (36).

Thus the value of an infinite simple continued fraction uniquely determines the partial quotients. The same is almost true of finite simple continued fractions. Since (31) and (32) imply (33), it follows that if $x$ is given by (30), then any continued fraction of $n_{x}$ terms that represents $x$ must indeed match (30) term for term. But, for example, $1 \sqrt{3}+1 \sqrt{5}=1 \sqrt{3}+1 \sqrt{4}+1 \sqrt{1}$. This is always possible: replace $a_{n_{x}}(x)$ in (30) (where $a_{n_{\lambda}}(x) \geq 2$ ) by $a_{n_{2}}(x)-1+1 \sqrt{1}$. Apart from this ambiguity, the representation is unique-and the representation (30) that results from repeated application of $T$ to a rational $x$ never ends with a partial quotient of $1^{\dagger}$
${ }^{\dagger}$ See Rockett \& Szusz for more on continued fractions.

## Notes on the Problems

These notes consist of hints, solutions, and references to the literature. As a rule a solution is complete in proportion to the frequency with which it is needed for the solution of subsequent problems

## Section 1

1.1. (a) Each point of the discrete space lies in one of the four sets $A_{1} \cap A_{2}$, $A_{1}^{c} \cap A_{2}, A_{1} \cap A_{2}^{c}, A_{1}^{c} \cap A_{2}^{c}$ and hence would have probability at most $2^{-2}$; continue.
(b) If, for each $i, B_{i}$ is $A_{i}$ or $A_{i}^{c}$, then $B_{1} \cap \cdots \cap B_{n}$ has probability at most $\operatorname{II}_{i=1}^{n}\left(1-\alpha_{i}\right) \leq \exp \left[-\sum_{i=1}^{n} \alpha_{i}\right]$
1.3. (b) Suppose $A$ is trifling and let $A^{-}$be its closure. Given $\epsilon$ choose intervals ( $\left.a_{k}, b_{k}\right], k=1, \ldots, n$, such that $A \subset \bigcup_{k=1}^{n}\left(a_{k}, b_{k}\right]$ and $\sum_{k=1}^{n}\left(b_{k}-a_{k}\right)<\epsilon / 2$. If $x_{k}=a_{k}-\epsilon / 2 n$, then $A^{-} \subset \bigcup_{k=1}^{n}\left(x_{k}, b_{k}\right\rceil$ and $\sum_{k=1}^{n}\left(b_{k}-x_{k}\right)<\epsilon$.

For the other parts of the problem, consider the set of rationals in $(0,1)$.
1.4. (a) Cover $A_{r}(i)$ by $(r-1)^{n}$ intervals of length $r^{-n}$.
(c) Go to the base $r^{k}$. Identify the digits in the base $r$ with the keys of the typewriter. The monkey is certain eventually to reproduce the eleventh edition of the Britannica and even, unhappily, the fifteenth.
1.5. (a) The set $A_{3}(1)$ is itself uncountable, since a point in it is specified by a sequence of 0's and 2's (excluding the countably many that end in 0's).
(b) For sequences $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}$ of 0 's, 1 's, and 2's, let $M_{u_{1} \quad u_{n}}$ consist of the points in ( 0,1 ] whose nonterminating base- 3 expansions start out with those digits. Then $A_{3}(1)=(0,1]-\cup M_{u_{1}, u_{n}}$, where the union extends over $n \geq 1$ and the sequences $u_{1}, \ldots, u_{n}$ containing at least one 1 . The set described in part (b) is $[0,1]-\cup M_{u_{1} . \quad u_{n}}^{\mathrm{o}}$, where the union is as before, and this is the closure of $A_{3}(1)$.

From this representation of $C$, it is not hard to deduce that it can be defined as the set of points in $[0,1]$ that can be written in base 3 without any 1's if terminating expansions are also allowed. For example, $C$ contains $\frac{2}{3}=.1222 \cdots =.2000 \ldots$ because it is possible to avoid 1 in the expansion.
(c) Given $\epsilon$ and an $\omega$ in $C$, choose $\omega^{\prime}$ in $A_{3}(1)$ within $\epsilon / 2$ of $\omega$; now define $\omega^{\prime \prime}$ by changing from 2 to 0 some digit of $\omega^{\prime}$ far enough out that $\omega^{\prime \prime}$ differs from $\omega^{\prime}$ by at most $\epsilon / 2$.
1.7. The interchange of limit and integral is justified because the series $\sum_{k} r_{k}(\omega) 2^{-k}$ converges uniformly in $\omega$ (integration to the limit is studied systematically in Section 16). There is a direct derivation of (1.40): let $n \rightarrow \infty$ in $\sin t= 2^{n} \sin 2^{-n} t \cdot \Pi_{k=1}^{n} \cos 2^{-k} t$, which follows by induction from the half-angle formula.
1.10. (a) Given $m$ and a subinterval $(a, b]$ of $(0,1]$, choose a dyadic interval $I$ in ( $a, b$ ], and then choose in $I$ a dyadic interval $J$ of order $n>m$ such that $\left|n^{-1} s_{n}(\omega)\right|>\frac{1}{2}$ for $\omega \in J$. This is possible because to specify $J$ is to specify the first $n$ dyadic digits of the points in $J$, choose the first digits in such a way that $J \subset I$ and take the following ones to be 1 , with $n$ so large that $n^{-1} S_{n}(\omega)$ is near 1 for $\omega \in J$.
(b) A countable union of sets of the first category is also of the first category; $(0,1]=N \cup N^{c}$ would be of the first category if $N^{c}$ were. For Baire's theorem, see Royden, p 139.
1.11. (a) If $x=p_{0} / q_{0} \neq p / q$, then

$$
\left|x-\frac{p}{q}\right|=\frac{\left|p_{0} q-q_{0} p\right|}{q_{0} q} \geq \frac{1}{q_{0} q} .
$$

(c) The rational $\sum_{k=1}^{n} 1 / 2^{\alpha(k)}$ has denominator $2^{\alpha(n)}$ and approximates $x$ to within $2 / 2^{\alpha(n+1)}$.

## Section 2

2.3. (b) Let $\Omega$ consist of four points, and let $\mathscr{F}$ consist of the empty set, $\Omega$ itself, and all six of the two-point sets.
2.4. (b) For example, take $\Omega$ to consist of the integers, and let $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ be the $\sigma$-field generated by the singletons $(k)$ with $k \leq n$. As a matter of fact, any example in which $\mathscr{F}_{n}$ is a proper subclass of $\mathscr{F}_{n+1}$ for all $n$ will do, because it can be shown that in this case $\bigcup_{n} \mathscr{F}_{n}$ necessarily fails to be a $\sigma$-field; see A . Broughton and B. W. Huff: A comment on unions of sigma-fields, Amer. Math. Monthly, 84 (1977), 553-554.
2.5. (b) The class in question is certainly contained in $f(\mathscr{A})$ and is easily seen to be closer under the formation of finite intersections. But $\left(\bigcup_{i=1}^{m} \bigcap_{j=1}^{n_{1}} A_{i j}\right)^{c}= \bigcap_{i=1}^{m} \bigcup_{j=1}^{n_{i}} A_{i j}^{c}$, and $\bigcup_{j=1}^{n_{i}} A_{i j}^{c}=\bigcup_{j=1}^{n_{i}}\left[A_{i j}^{c} \cap \bigcap_{k=1}^{j-1} A_{i k}\right]$ has the required form.
2.8. If $\mathscr{H}$ is the smallest class over $\mathscr{A}$ closed under the formation of countable unions and intersections, clearly $\mathscr{K} \subset \sigma(\mathscr{A})$. To prove the reverse inclusion, first show that the class of $A$ such that $A^{c} \in \mathscr{K}$ is closed under the formation of countable unions and intersections and contains $\mathscr{A}$ and hence contains $\mathscr{K}$.
2.9. Note that $\bigcup_{n} B_{n} \in \sigma\left(\cup_{n} \mathscr{A}_{B_{n}}\right)$.
2.10. (a) Show that the class of $A$ for which $I_{A}(\omega)=I_{A}\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$ is a $\sigma$-field. See Example 4.8.
2.11. (b) Suppose that $\mathscr{F}$ is the $\sigma$-field of the countable and the cocountable sets in $\Omega$. Suppose that $\mathscr{F}$ is countably generated and $\Omega$ is uncountable. Show that $\mathscr{F}$
is generated by a countable class of singletons; if $\Omega_{0}$ is the union of these, then $\mathscr{F}$ must consist of the sets $B$ and $B \cup \Omega_{0}^{c}$ with $B \subset \Omega_{0}$, and these do not include the singletons in $\Omega_{0}^{c}$, which is uncountable because $\Omega$ is.
(c) Let $\mathscr{F}_{2}$ consist of the Borel sets in $\Omega=(0,1]$, and let $\mathscr{F}_{1}$ consist of the countable and the cocountable sets there.
2.12. Suppose that $A_{1}, A_{2}, \ldots$ is an infinite sequence of distinct sets in a $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$, and let $\mathscr{E}$ consist of the nonempty sets of the form $\bigcap_{n=1}^{\infty} B_{n}$, where $B_{n}=A_{n}$ or $B_{n}=A_{n}^{c}, n=1,2, \ldots$. Each $A_{n}$ is the union of the $\mathscr{E}$-sets it contains, and since the $A_{n}$ are distinct, $\mathscr{G}$ must be infinite. But there are uncountably many distinct countable unions of $\mathscr{E}$-sets, and they all lie in $\mathscr{F}$.
2.18. For this and the subsequent problems on applications of probability theory to arithmetic, the only number theory required is the fundamental theorem of arithmetic and its immediate consequences. The other problems on stochastic arithmetic are $4.15,4.16,5.19,5.20,6.16,18.17,25.15,30.9,30.10,30.11$, and 30.12. See also Theorem 30.3.
(b) Let $A$ consist of the even integers, let $C_{k}=\left[m: v_{k}<m \leq v_{k+1} \frac{1}{1}\right.$, and let $B$ consist of the even integers in $C_{1} \cup C_{3} \cup \cdots$ together with the odd integers in $C_{2} \cup C_{4} \cup \cdots$; take $v_{k}$ to increase very rapidly with $k$ and consider $A \cap B$.
(c) If $c$ is the least common multiple of $a$ and $b$, then $M_{a} \cap M_{b}=M_{c}$. From $M_{a} \in \mathscr{D}$ conclude in succession that $M_{a} \cap M_{b} \in \mathscr{D}, \quad M_{a_{1}} \cap \cdots \cap M_{a_{j}} \cap M_{b_{1}}^{c} \cap \cdots \cap M_{b_{k}}^{c} \in \mathscr{Q}, f\left(\mathscr{M}^{\prime}\right) \subset \mathscr{Q}$. By the same sequence of steps, show how $D$ on $\mathscr{M}$ determines $D$ on $f(\mathscr{M})$.
(d) If $B_{l}=M_{a}-\cup_{p \leq l} M_{a p}$, then $a \in B_{l}$ and (the inclusion-exclusion formula requires only finite additivity)

$$
\begin{aligned}
D\left(B_{i}\right) & =\frac{1}{a}-\sum_{p \leq l} \frac{1}{a p}+\sum_{p<q \leq l} \frac{1}{a p q}-\cdots \\
& =\frac{1}{a} \prod_{p \leq l}\left(1-\frac{1}{p}\right) \leq \frac{1}{a} \exp \left(-\sum_{p \leq i} \frac{1}{p}\right) \rightarrow 0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

Choose $l_{a}$ so that, if $C_{a}=B_{l_{a}}$, then $D\left(C_{a}\right)<2^{-a-1}$. If $D$ were a probability measure on $f(\mathscr{M}), D(\Omega) \leq \frac{1}{2}$ would follow. See Problem 4.16 for a different approach.
2.19. (a) Apply the intermediate-value theorem to the function $f(x)=\lambda(A \cap(0, x)$. Note that this even proves part (c) for $\lambda$ (under the assumption that $\lambda$ exists).
(b) If $0<P(B)<P(A)$, then either $0<P(B) \leq \frac{1}{2} P(A)$ or $0<P(B-A) \leq \frac{1}{2} P(A)$. Continue.
(c) If $P\left(\cup_{k} H_{k}\right)<x$, choose $C$ so that $C \subset A-\cup_{k} H_{k}$ and $0<P(C)<x- P\left(\bigcup_{k} H_{k}\right)$. If $n^{-1}<P(C)$, then $P\left(\bigcup_{k<n} H_{k}\right)+h_{n}<P\left(\bigcup_{k<n} H_{k}\right)+P\left(H_{n}\right)+ P(C) \leq P\left(\bigcup_{k<n} H_{k}\right)+h_{n}$.
2.21. (c) If $\mathscr{A}_{n-1}$ were a $\sigma$-field, $\mathscr{B} \subset \mathscr{A}_{n-1}$ would follow.
2.22. Use the fact that, if $\alpha_{1}, \alpha_{2}, \ldots$ is a sequence of ordinals satisfying $\alpha_{n}<\Omega$, then there exists an ordinal $\alpha$ such that $\alpha<\Omega$ and $\alpha_{n}<\alpha$ for all $n$.
2.23. Suppose that $B_{j} \in \cup_{\beta<\alpha} \mathscr{A}_{\beta}, j=1,2, \ldots$. Choose odd integers $n_{j}$ in such a way that $B_{j} \in \mathscr{I}_{\beta_{\alpha}\left(n_{j}\right)}$ and the $n_{j}$ are all distinct; choose $\mathscr{I}_{0}$-sets such that

$$
\Phi_{\beta_{\alpha}\left(n_{j}\right)}\left(C_{m_{m_{j}}}, C_{m_{m_{j} 2}} ; \ldots\right)=B_{j} ;
$$

for $n$ not of the form $n_{j}$, choose $\mathscr{I}_{0}$-sets for which $\Phi_{\beta_{\alpha}(n)}\left(C_{m_{11}}, C_{m_{n 2}}, \ldots\right)$ is $\varnothing$ or ( 0,1$]$ as $n$ is odd or even. Then $\bigcup_{j=1}^{\infty} B_{j}=\Phi_{\alpha}\left(C_{1}, C_{2}, \ldots\right)$. Similarly, $B^{c}= \Phi_{\alpha}\left(C_{1}, C_{2}, \ldots\right)$ for $\mathscr{I}_{0}$-sets $C_{n}$ if $B \in \bigcup_{\beta<\alpha} \mathscr{I}_{\beta}$. The rest of the proof is essentially the same as before.

## Section 3

3.1. (a) The finite additivity of $P$ is used in the proof that $\mathscr{F}_{0} \subset \mathscr{A}$ and again (via monotonicity; see (2.5)) in the proof of (3.7). The countable additivity of $P$ is used (via countable subadditivity; see Theorem 2.1) in the proof of (3.7).
(b) For a specific example consider $U_{n}\left(2^{-1}+n^{-1}, 1\right]$ in connection with Problem 2.15. But an example is provided by every $P$ that is finitely but not countably additive: If $P$ is finitely additive on a field $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ and $A_{r}$ are disjoint $\mathscr{F}_{0}$-sets whose union $A$ also lies in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$, then monotonicity (which requires finite additivity only) gives $\sum_{k \leq n} P\left(A_{k}\right)=P\left(\cup_{k \leq n} A_{k}\right) \leq P(A)$ and hence $\sum_{k} P\left(A_{k}\right) \leq P(A)$. Countable subadditivity will ensure that there is equality here.
(c) The proof of (3.7) involves the countable subadditivity of $P$ on $\mathscr{F}_{0}$, which is only assumed to be a field (that being the whole point of the theorem).
3.2. (a) Given $\epsilon$, choose $\mathscr{F}_{0}$-sets $A_{n}$ such that $A \subset \bigcup_{n} A_{n}$ and $\sum P\left(A_{n}\right)<P^{*}(A)+ \epsilon$; if $B=\bigcup_{n} A_{n}$, then $A \subset B, B \in \mathscr{F}$, and $P(B)<P^{*}(A)+\epsilon$; hence the right side of (3.9) is at most $P^{*}(A)$ On the other hand, $A \subset B$ and $B \in \mathscr{F}$ imply $P^{*}(A) \leq P^{*}(B)=P(B)$. Hence (3.9). If $A \subset B_{k}, B_{k} \in \mathscr{F}, P\left(B_{k}\right)<P^{*}(A)+k^{-1}$, and $B=\bigcap_{k} B_{k}$, then $A \subset B, B \in \mathscr{F}$, and $P^{*}(A)=P(B)$. For (3.10), argue by compleinentation.
(b) Suppose that $P_{*}(A)=P^{*}(A)$ and chose $\mathscr{F}$-sets $A_{1}$ and $A_{2}$ in such a way that $A_{1} \subset A \subset A_{2}$ and $P\left(A_{1}\right)=P\left(A_{2}\right)$. Given $E$, choose an $\mathscr{F}$-set $B$ in such a way that $E \subset B$ and $P^{*}(E)=P(B)$. Then $P^{*}(A \cap E)+P^{*}\left(A^{c} \cap E\right) \leq P\left(A_{2} \cap B\right)+P\left(A_{1}^{c} \cap B\right)$. Now use (2.7) to bound the last sum by $P(B)+P\left(A_{2}\right. \left.-A_{1}\right)=P^{*}\left(E^{\prime}\right)$.
3.3. First note the general fact that $P^{*}$ agrees with $P$ on $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ if and only if $P$ is countably, additive there, a condition not satisfied in parts (b) and (e). Using Problem 3.2 simplifies the analysis of $P^{*}$ and $\mathscr{M}\left(P^{*}\right)$ in the other parts.

Note in parts (b) and (e) that, if $P^{*}$ and $P_{*}$ are defined by (3.1) and (3.2), then, since $P^{*}(A)=0$ for all $A$, (3.4) holds for all $A$ and (3.3) holds for no $A$. Countable additivity thus plays an essential role in Problem 3.2.
3.6 (c) Split $E^{c}$ by $A$ : $P_{\circ}(E)=1-P^{\circ}\left(E^{c}\right)=1-P^{\circ}\left(A \cap E^{c}\right)-P^{\circ}\left(A^{c} \cap E^{c}\right)=1- P^{\circ}\left(A \cap E^{c}\right)-P\left(A^{c}\right)=P(A)-P^{\circ}(A-E)$.
3.7, (b) Apply (3.13): For $A \in \mathscr{F}_{0}, Q(A)=P^{\circ}(H \cap A)+P_{\circ}\left(H^{c} \cap A\right)=P^{\circ}(H \cap A) +P(A)-P^{\circ}\left(A-\left(H^{c} \cap A\right)\right)=P(A)$.
(c) If $A_{1}$ and $A_{2}$ are disjoint $\mathscr{F}_{0}$-sets, then by (3.12),

$$
P^{\circ}\left(H \cap\left(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\right)\right)=P^{\circ}\left(H \cap A_{1}\right)+P^{\circ}\left(H \cap A_{2}\right) .
$$

Apply (3.13) to the three terms in this equation, successively using $A_{1} \cup A_{2}, A_{1}$, and $A_{2}$ for $A$ :

$$
P_{\circ}\left(H^{c} \cap\left(A_{1} \cup A_{2}\right)\right)=P_{\circ}\left(H^{c} \cap A_{1}\right)+P_{\circ}\left(H^{c} \cap A_{2}\right) .
$$

But for these two equations to hold it is enough that $H \cap A_{1} \cap A_{2}=\varnothing$ in the first case and $H^{c} \cap A_{1} \cap A_{2}=\varnothing$ in the second (replacing $A_{1}$ by $A_{1} \cap A_{2}^{c}$ changes nothing).
3.8. By using Banach limits (Banach, p. 34) one can similarly prove that density $D$ on the class $\mathscr{D}$ (Problem 2.18) extends to a finitely additive probability on the class of all subsets of $\Omega=\{1,2, \ldots\}$.
3.14. The argument is based on cardinality. Since the Cantor set $C$ has Lebesgue measure $0,2^{C}$ is contained in the class $\mathscr{L}$ of Lebesgue sets in (0, 1]. But $C$ is uncountable: card $\mathscr{B}=\operatorname{card}(0,1]<\operatorname{card} 2^{C} \leq \operatorname{card} \mathscr{L}$.
3.18. (a) Since the $A \oplus r$ are disjoint Borel sets, $\sum_{r} \lambda(A \oplus r) \leq 1$, and so the common value $\lambda(A)$ of the $\lambda(A \oplus r)$ must be 0 . Similarly, if $A$ is a Borel sct contained in some $H \oplus r$, then $\lambda(A)=0$.
(b) If the $E \cap(H \oplus r)$ are all Borel sets, they all have Lebesgue measure 0 , and so $E$ is a Borel set of Lebesgue measure 0 .
3.19. (b) Given $A_{1}, B_{1}, \ldots, A_{n-1}, B_{n-1}$, note that their union $C_{n}$ is nowhere dense, so that $I_{n}$ contains an interval $J_{n}$ disjoint from $C_{n}$. Choose in $J_{n}$ disjoint, nowhere dense sets $A_{n}$ and $B_{n}$ of positive measure.
(c) Note that $A$ and $B_{n}$ are disjoint and that $A_{n} \cup B_{n} \subset G$.
3.20. (a) If $I_{n}$ are disjoint open intervals with union $G$, then $b^{-1} \lambda(A)>\Sigma_{n} \lambda\left(I_{n}\right) \geq \sum_{n} b^{-1} \lambda\left(A \cap I_{n}\right) \geq b^{-1} \lambda(A)$.

## Section 4

4.1. Let $r$ be the quantity on the right in (4.30), assumed finite. Suppose that $x<r$; then $x<\bigvee_{k=n}^{\infty} x_{k}$ for $n \geq 1$ and hence $x<x_{k}$ for some $k \geq n: x<x_{n}$ i.o. Suppose that $x<x_{n}$ i.o.; then $x<\bigvee_{k=n}^{\infty} x_{k}$ for $n \geq 1: x \leq r$. It follows that $r=\sup \left[x: x<x_{n}\right.$ i.o. $]$, which is easily seen to be the supremum of the limit points of the sequence. The argument for (4.31) is similar.
4.10. The class $\mathscr{F}$ is the $\sigma$-field generated by $\mathscr{G} \cup\{H\}$ (Problem 2.7(a)). If $\left(H \cap G_{1}\right) \cup\left(H^{c} \cap G_{2}\right)=\left(H \cap G_{1}^{\prime}\right) \cup\left(H^{c} \cap G_{2}^{\prime}\right)$, then $G_{1} \Delta G_{1}^{\prime} \subset H^{c}$ and $G_{2} \Delta G_{2}^{\prime} \subset H$; consistency now follows because $\lambda_{*}(H)=\lambda_{*}\left(H^{c}\right)=0$. If $A_{n}= \left(H \cap G_{1}^{(n)}\right) \cup\left(H^{c} \cap G_{2}^{(n)}\right)$ are disjoint, then $G_{1}^{(m)} \cap G_{1}^{(n)} \subset H^{c}$ and $G_{2}^{(m)} \cap G_{2}^{(n)} \subset H$ for $m \neq n$, and therefore (see Problem 2.17) $P\left(\cup_{n} A_{n}\right)=\frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(\cup_{n} \tilde{G}_{1}^{(n)}\right) +\frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(U_{n} G_{2}^{(n)}\right)=\sum_{n}\left(\frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(G_{1}^{(n)}\right)+\frac{1}{2} \lambda\left(G_{2}^{(n)}\right)\right)=\sum_{n} P\left(A_{n}\right)$. The intervals with rational endpoints generate $\mathscr{G}$.
4.14. Show as in Problem 1.1(b) that the maximum of $P\left(B_{1} \cap \cdots \cap B_{n}\right)$, where $B_{i}$ is $A_{i}$ or $A_{i}^{c}$, goes to 0 . Let $A_{\lambda}=\left[\omega: \sum_{n} I_{A_{n}}(\omega) 2^{-n} \leq x\right]$, show that $P\left(A \cap A_{x}\right)$ is continuous in $x$, and proceed as in Problem 2.19(a).
4.15. Calculate $D\left(F_{l}\right)$ by (2.36) and the inclusion-exclusion formula, and estimate $P_{n}\left(F_{l}-F\right)$ by subadditivity; now use $0 \leq P_{n}\left(F_{l}\right)-P_{n}(F)=P_{n}\left(F_{l}-F\right)$. For the calculation of the infinite product, see Hardy \& Wright, p. 246.

## Section 5

5.5. (a) If $m=0, \alpha \geq 0$, and $x>0$, then $P[X \geq \alpha] \leq P\left[(X+x)^{2} \geq(\alpha+x)^{2}\right] \leq E[(X \left.+x)^{2}\right] /(\alpha+x)^{2}=\left(\sigma^{2}+x^{2}\right) /(\alpha+x)^{2}$; minimize over $x$.
5.8. (b) It is enough to prove that $\varphi(t)=f\left(t\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)+(1-t)(x, y)\right)$ is convex in $t (0 \leq t \leq 1)$ for $(x, y)$ and $\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)$ in $C$. If $\alpha=x^{\prime}-x$ and $\beta=y^{\prime}-y$, then (if $f_{11}>0$ )

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varphi^{\prime \prime} & =f_{11} \alpha^{2}+2 f_{12} \alpha \beta+f_{22} \beta^{2} \\
& =\frac{1}{f_{11}}\left(f_{11} \alpha+f_{12} \beta\right)^{2}+\frac{1}{f_{11}}\left(f_{11} f_{22}-f_{12}^{2}\right) \beta^{2} \geq 0
\end{aligned}
$$

Examples like $f(x, y)=y^{2}-2 x y$ show that convexity in each variable separately does not imply convexitv.
5.9. Check (5.39) for $f(x, y)=-x^{1 / p} y^{1 / q}$.
5.10. Check (5.39) for $f(x, y)=-\left(x^{1 / p}+y^{1 / p}\right)^{p}$.
5.19 For (5.43) use (2.36) and the fundamental theorem of arithmetic since the $p_{i}$ are distinct, the $p_{i}^{k_{i}}$ individually divide $m$ if and only if their product does. For (5.44) use inclusion-exclusion. For (5.47), use (5.29) (see Problem 5.12)).
5.20. (a) By (5.47), $E_{n}\left[\alpha_{p}\right] \leq \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} p^{-k} \leq 2 / p$. And, of course, $n^{-1} \log n!=E_{n}[\log ]= \sum_{p} E_{n}\left[\alpha_{p}\right] \log p$.
(b) Use (5.48) and the fact that $E_{n}\left[\alpha_{p}-\delta_{p}\right] \leq \sum_{k=2}^{\infty} p^{-k}$.
(c) By (5.49),

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{n<p \leq 2 n} \log p & =\sum_{n<p \leq 2 n}\left(\left\lfloor\frac{2 n}{p}\right\rfloor-2\left\lfloor\frac{n}{p}\right\rfloor\right) \log p \\
& \leq 2 n\left(E_{2 n}\left[\log ^{*}\right]-E_{n}\left[\log ^{*}\right]\right)=O(n)
\end{aligned}
$$

Deduce (5.50) by splitting the range of summation by successive powers of 2 .
(d) If $K$ bounds the $O(1)$ terms in (5.51), then

$$
\sum_{p \leq x} \log p \geq \theta x \sum_{\theta x<p \leq x} p^{-1} \log p \geq \theta x\left(\log \theta^{-1}-2 K\right) .
$$

(e) For (5.53) use

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{p \leq x} \frac{\log p}{\log x} & \leq \pi(x) \leq \sum_{p \leq x^{1 / 2}} 1+\sum_{x^{1 / 2}<p \leq x} \frac{\log p}{\log x^{i / 2}} \\
& \leq x^{1 / 2}+\frac{2}{\log x} \sum_{p \leq x} \log p .
\end{aligned}
$$

By (5.53), $\pi(x) \geq x^{1 / 2}$ for large $x$, and hence $\log \pi(x) \asymp \log x$ and $\pi(x) \asymp x / \log \pi(x)$. Apply this with $x=p_{r}$ and note that $\pi\left(p_{r}\right)=r$.

## Section 6

6.3. Since for given values of $X_{n 1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{n, k-1}(\omega)$ there are for $X_{n}(\omega)$ the $k$ possible values $0,1, \ldots, k-1$, the number of values of $\left(X_{n 1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{n n}(\omega)\right)$ is $n!$. Therefore, the map $\omega \rightarrow\left(X_{n 1}(\omega), \ldots, X_{n n}(\omega)\right)$ is one-to-one, and the $X_{n k}(\omega)$ determine $\omega$. It follows that if $0 \leq x_{i}<i$ for $1 \leq i \leq k$, then the number of permutations $\omega$ satisfying $X_{n i}(\omega)=x_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq k$, is just $(k+1)(k+2) \cdots n$, so that $P\left[X_{n i}=x_{i}, 1 \leq i \leq k\right]=1 / k!$. It now follows by induction on $k$ that $X_{n 1}, \ldots, X_{n k}$ are independent and $P\left[X_{n k}=x\right]=k^{-1}(0 \leq x<k)$.

Now calculate

$$
\begin{aligned}
E\left[X_{n k}\right] & =\frac{k-1}{2} \\
E\left[S_{n}\right] & =\frac{0+1+\cdots+(n-1)}{2}=\frac{n(n-1)}{4} \sim \frac{n^{2}}{4} \\
\operatorname{Var}\left[X_{n k}\right] & =\frac{0^{2}+1^{2}+\cdots+(k-1)^{2}}{k}-\left(\frac{k-1}{2}\right)^{2}=\frac{k^{2}-1}{12} \\
\operatorname{Var}\left[S_{n}\right] & =\frac{1}{12} \sum_{k=1}^{n}\left(k^{2}-1\right)=\frac{2 n^{3}+3 n^{2}-5 n}{72} \sim \frac{n^{3}}{36}
\end{aligned}
$$

Apply Chebyshev's inequality.
6.7. (a) If $k^{2} \leq n<(k+1)^{2}$, let $a_{n}=k^{2}$; if $M$ bounds the $\left|x_{n}\right|$, then

$$
\left|\frac{1}{n} s_{n}-\frac{1}{a_{n}} s_{a_{n}}\right| \leq\left|\frac{1}{n}-\frac{1}{a_{n}}\right| \cdot n M+\frac{1}{a_{n}}\left(n-a_{n}\right) M=2 M \frac{n-a_{n}}{a_{n}} \rightarrow 0
$$

6.16. From (5.53) and (5.54) it follows that $a_{n}=\sum_{p} n^{-1}\lfloor n / p\rfloor \rightarrow \infty$. The ieft side of (6.8) is

$$
\frac{1}{n}\left|\frac{n}{p q}\right|-\frac{1}{n}\left|\frac{n}{p}\right| \frac{1}{n}\left|\frac{n}{q}\right| \leq \frac{1}{p q}-\left(\frac{1}{p}-\frac{1}{n}\right)\left(\frac{1}{q}-\frac{1}{n}\right) \leq \frac{1}{n p}+\frac{1}{n q}
$$

## Section 7

7.3. If one grants that there are only countably many effective rules, the result is an immediate consequence of the mathematics of this and the preceding sections: $C$ is a countable intersection of $\mathscr{F}$-sets of measure 1 . The argument proves in particular the nontrivial fact that collectives exist.
7.7. If $n \leq \tau$, then $W_{n}=W_{n-1}-X_{n-1}=W_{1}-S_{n-1}$, and $\tau$ is the smallest $n$ for which $S_{n-1}=W_{1}$. Use (7.8) for the question of whether the game terminates. Now

$$
F_{\tau}=F_{0}+\sum_{k=1}^{\tau-1}\left(W_{1}-S_{k-1}\right) X_{k}=F_{0}+W_{1} S_{\tau-1}-\frac{1}{2}\left(S_{\tau-1}^{2}-(\tau-1)\right)
$$

7.8. Let $x_{1}, \ldots x_{i}$ be the initial pattern and put $\Sigma_{0}=x_{1}+\cdots+x_{i}$. Define $\Sigma_{n}= \Sigma_{n-1}-W_{n} X_{n}, L_{0}=k$, and $L_{n}=L_{n-1}-\left(3 X_{n}+1\right) / 2$. Then $\tau$ is the smallest $n$ such that $L_{n} \leq 0$, and $\tau$ is by the strong law finite with probability 1 if $E\left[3 X_{n}+1\right]=6\left(p-\frac{1}{3}\right)>0$. For $n \leq \tau, \Sigma_{n}$ is the sum of the pattern used to determine $W_{n+1}$. Since $F_{n}-F_{n-1}=\Sigma_{n-1}-\Sigma_{n}$, it follows that $F_{n}=F_{0}+\Sigma_{0}-\Sigma_{n}$ and $F_{\tau}=F_{0}+\Sigma_{0}$.
7.9. Observe that $E\left[F_{n}-F_{\tau}\right]=E\left[\sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k} I_{[\tau<k]}\right]=\sum_{k=1}^{n} E\left[X_{k}\right] P[\tau<k]$.

## Section 8

8.8. (b) With probability 1 the population either dies out or goes to infinity. If, for example, $p_{k 0}=1-p_{k, k+1}=1 / k^{2}$, then extinction and explosion each have positive probability.
8.9. To prove that $x_{i} \equiv 0$ is the only possibility in the persistent case, use Problem 8.5, or else argue directly: If $x_{i}=\sum_{j \neq i_{0}} p_{i j} x_{j}, i \neq i_{0}$, and $K$ bounds the $\left|x_{i}\right|$, then $x_{i}=\sum p_{i j_{1}} \ldots p_{j_{n-1} j_{n}} x_{j_{n}}$, where the sum is over $j_{1}, \ldots, j_{n-1}$ distinct from $i_{0}$, and hence $\left|x_{i}\right| \leq K P_{i}\left[X_{k} \neq i_{0}, k \leq n\right] \rightarrow 0$.
8.13. Let $P$ be the set of $i$ for which $\pi_{i}>0$, let $N$ be the set of $i$ for which $\pi_{i} \leq 0$, and suppose that $P$ and $N$ are both nonempty. For $i_{0} \in P$ and $j_{0} \in N$ choose $n$ so that $p_{i_{0} j_{0}}^{(n)}>0$. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
0 & <\sum_{j \in N} \sum_{i \in P} \pi_{i} p_{i j}^{(n)}=\sum_{j \in N} \pi_{j}-\sum_{j \in N} \sum_{i \in N} \pi_{i} p_{i j}^{(n)} \\
& =\sum_{i \in N} \pi_{i} \sum_{j \in P} p_{i j}^{(n)} \leq 0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

Transfer from $N$ to $P$ any $i$ for which $\pi_{i}=0$ and use a similar argument.
8.16. Denote the sets (8.32) and (8.52) by $P$ and by $F$, respectively. Since $F \subset P$, $\operatorname{gcd} P \leq \operatorname{gcd} F$. The reverse inequality follows from the fact that each integer in $P$ is a sum of integers in $F$.
8.17. Consider the chain with states $0,1, \ldots$ and $\alpha$ and iransition probabiiities $p_{0 j}=f_{j+1}$ for $j \geq 0, p_{0 \alpha}=1-f, p_{i_{1} i-1}=1$ for $i \geq 1$, and $p_{\alpha \alpha}=1$ ( $\alpha$ is an absorbing state). The transition matrix is

$$
\left[\begin{array}{ccccc}
f_{1} & f_{2} & f_{3} & \cdots & 1-f \\
1 & 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 \\
0 & 1 & 0 & \cdots & 0 \\
\cdots & \cdots & \cdots & \cdots & \cdots \\
0 & 0 & 0 & \cdots & 1
\end{array}\right]
$$

Show that $f_{00}^{(n)}=f_{n}$ and $p_{00}^{(n)}=u_{n}$, and apply Theorem 8.1. Then assume $f=1$, discard the state $\alpha$ and any states $j$ such that $f_{k}=0$ for $k>j$, and apply Theorem 8.8.

In Feller, Volume 1, the renewal theorem is proved by purely analytic means and is then used as the starting point for the theory of Markov chains. Here the procedure is the reverse.
8.19. The transition probabilities are $p_{0 r}=1$ and $p_{i, r-i+1}=p, p_{i, r-i}=q, 1 \leq i \leq r$; the stationary probabilities are $u_{1}=\cdots=u_{r}=q^{-1} u_{0}=(r+q)^{-1}$. The chance of getting wet is $u_{0} p$, of which the maximum is $2 r+1-2 \sqrt{r(r+1)}$. For $r=5$ this is .046 , the pessimal value of $p$ being .523 . Of course, $u_{0} p \leq 1 / 4 r$. In more reasonable climates fewer umbrellas suffice; if $p=-25$ and $r=3$, then $u_{0} p=$ .050 ; if $p=.1$ and $r=2$, then $u_{0} p=.031$. At the other end of the scale, if $p=.8$ and $r=3$, then $u_{0} p=.050$; and if $p=.9$ and $r=2$, then $u_{0} p=.043$.
8.22. For the last part, consider the chain with state space $C_{m}$ and transition probabilities $p_{i j}$ for $i, j \in C_{m}$ (show that they do add to 1 ).
8.23. Let $C^{\prime}=S-(T \cup C)$, and take $U=T \cup C^{\prime}$ in (8.51). The probability of absorption in $C$ is the probability of ever entering it, and for initial states $i$ in $T \cup C$ these probabilities are the minimal solution of

$$
\begin{array}{rlrl}
y_{i} & =\sum_{j \in T} p_{i j} y_{j}+\sum_{j \in C} p_{i j}+\sum_{j \in C^{\prime}} p_{i j} v_{j}, & i & \in T \cup C^{\prime}, \\
0 \leq y_{i} \leq 1, & i \in T \cup C^{\prime} .
\end{array}
$$

Since the states in $C^{\prime}$ ( $C^{\prime}=\varnothing$ is possible) are persistent and $C$ is closed, it is impossible to move from $C^{\prime}$ to $C$. Therefore, in the minimal solution of the system above, $y_{i}=0$ for $i \in C^{\prime}$. This gives the system (8.55). It also gives, for the minimal solution, $\sum_{j \in T} p_{i j} y_{j}+\sum_{j \in C} p_{i l}=0, i \in C^{\prime}$. This makes probabilistic sense: for an $i$ in $C^{\prime}$, not only is it impossible to move to a $j$ in $C$, it is impossible to move to a $j$ in $T$ for which there is positive probability of absorption in $C$.
8.24. Fix on a state $i$, and let $S_{\nu}$ consist of those $j$ for which $p_{i j}^{(n)}>0$ for some $n$ congruent to $\nu$ modulo $t$. Choose $k$ so that $p_{j i}^{(k)}>0$; if $p_{i j}^{(m)}$ and $p_{i j}^{(n)}$ are positive, then $t$ divides $m+k$ and $n+k$, so that $m$ and $n$ are congruent modulo $t$. The $S_{y}$, are thus well defined.
8.25. Show that Theorem 8,6 applies to the chain with transition probabilities $p_{i j}^{(t)}$.
8.27. (a) From $P C=C \Lambda$ follows $P c_{i}=\lambda_{i} c_{i}$, from $R P=\Lambda R$ follows $r_{i} P=\lambda_{i} r_{i}$, and from $R C=I$ follows $r_{i} c_{j}=\delta_{i j}$. Clearly $\Lambda^{n}$ is diagonal and $P^{n}=C \Lambda^{n} R$. Hence $p_{i j}^{(n)}=\sum_{u t} C_{i u} \lambda_{u}^{n} \delta_{u t} R_{i j}=\sum_{u} \lambda_{u}^{n}\left(c_{u} r_{u}\right)_{i j}=\sum_{u} \lambda_{u}^{n}\left(A_{u}\right)_{i j}$.
(b) By Problem 8.26, there are scalars $\rho$ and $\gamma$ such that $r_{1}=\rho r_{0}=\rho\left(\pi_{1}, \ldots, \pi_{s}\right)$ and $c_{1}=\gamma c_{0}$, where $c_{0}$ is the column vector of 1's. From $r_{1} c_{1}=1$ follows $\rho \gamma=1$, and hence $A_{1}=c_{1} r_{1}=c_{0} r_{0}$ has rows ( $\pi_{1}, \ldots, \pi_{s}$ ). Of course, (8.56) gives the exact rate of convergence. It is useful for numerical work; see Çinlar, pp. 364 ff.
(c) Suppose all four $p_{i j}$ are positive. Then $\pi_{1}=p_{21} /\left(p_{21}+p_{12}\right), \pi_{2}=p_{12} /\left(p_{21}\right. +p_{12}$ ), the second eigenvalue is $\lambda=1-p_{12}-p_{21}$, and

$$
P^{n}=\left[\begin{array}{ll}
\pi_{1} & \pi_{2} \\
\pi_{1} & \pi_{2}
\end{array}\right]+\lambda^{n}\left[\begin{array}{rr}
\pi_{2} & -\pi_{2} \\
-\pi_{1} & \pi_{1}
\end{array}\right] .
$$

Note that for given $n$ and $\epsilon, \lambda^{n}>1-\epsilon$ is possible, which means that the $p_{i j}^{(n)}$ are not yet near the $\pi_{j}$. In the case of positive $p_{i j}, P$ is always diagonalizable by

$$
C=\left[\begin{array}{rr}
1 & \pi_{2} \\
1 & -\pi_{1}
\end{array}\right], \quad R=\left[\begin{array}{cc}
\pi_{1} & \pi_{2} \\
1 & -1
\end{array}\right]
$$

(d) For example, take $t=\frac{1}{3}, 0<\epsilon<t$, and

$$
P=\left[\begin{array}{ccc}
t & t & t \\
t & t & t \\
t-\epsilon & t+\epsilon & t
\end{array}\right] .
$$

In this case, 0 is an eigenvalue with algebraic multiplicity 2 and geometric multiplicity 1.
8.30. Show that $\alpha_{n}=\pi_{i}$ and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\beta_{n}-\alpha_{n} & =\frac{2}{n(n-1)} \sum_{k=1}^{n-1} \pi_{i}(n-k)\left(p_{i i}^{(k)}-\pi_{i}\right) \\
& =O\left(\frac{1}{n^{2}} \sum_{k=1}^{n}(n-k) \rho^{k}\right)=O\left(\frac{1}{n}\right),
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\rho$ is as in Theorem 8.9.
8.36. The definitions give

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{i}\left[f\left(X_{\sigma_{n}}\right)\right] & =P_{i}\left[\sigma_{n}<n\right] f(0)+P_{i}\left[\sigma_{n}=n\right] f(i+n) \\
& =1-P_{i}\left[\sigma_{n}=n\right]+P_{i}\left[\sigma_{n}=n\right]\left(1-f_{i+n, 0}\right) \\
& =1-p_{i} \ldots p_{i+n-1}+p_{1} \ldots p_{i+n-1}\left(p_{i+n} p_{i+n+1} \ldots\right),
\end{aligned}
$$

and this goes to 1 . Since $P_{i}\left[\tau \leq n=\sigma_{n}\right] \geq P_{i}\left([\tau \leq n] \cap\left[X_{k}>0, k \geq 1\right]\right) \rightarrow 1- f_{i 0}>0$, there is an $n$ of the kind required in the last part of the problem. And now

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{i}\left[f\left(X_{\tau}\right)\right] & \leq P_{i}\left[\tau<n=\sigma_{n}\right] f(i+n)+1-P_{i}\left[\tau<n=\sigma_{n}\right] \\
& =1-P_{i}\left[\tau<n=\sigma_{n}\right] f_{i+n, 0} .
\end{aligned}
$$

8.37. If $i \geq 1, n_{1}<n_{2},\left(i, \ldots, i+n_{1}\right) \in I_{n_{1}}$, and $\left(i \ldots, i+n_{2}\right) \in I_{n_{2}}$, then $P_{i}\left[\tau=n_{1}\right.$, $\left.\tau=n_{2}\right] \geq \boldsymbol{P}_{i}\left[X_{k}=i+k, k \leq n_{2}\right]>0$, which is impossible.

## Section 10

10.7. Let $\mu_{1}$ be counting measure on the $\sigma$-field of all subsets of a countably infinite $\Omega$, let $\mu_{2}=2 \mu_{1}$, and let $\mathscr{P}$ consist of the cofinite sets. Granted the existence of Lebesgue measure $\lambda$ on $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, one can construct another example: let $\mu_{1}=\lambda$ and $\mu_{2}=2 \lambda$, and let $\mathscr{P}$ consist of the half-infinite intervals $(-\infty, x]$.

There are similar examples with a field $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ in place of $\mathscr{P}$. Let $\Omega$ consist of the rationals in ( 0,1 ], let $\mu_{1}$ be counting measure, let $\mu_{2}=2 \mu_{1}$, and let $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ consist of finite disjoint unions of "intervals" [ $r \in \Omega: a<r \leq b$ ].

## Section 11

11.4. (b) If $(f, g] \subset \bigcup_{k}\left(f_{k}, g_{k}\right]$, then $(f(\omega), g(\omega)] \subset \bigcup_{k}\left(f_{k}(\omega), f_{k}(\omega)\right]$ for all $\omega$, and Theorem 1.3 gives $g(\omega)-f(\omega) \leq \sum_{k}\left(g_{k}(\omega)-f_{k}(\omega)\right)$. If $h_{m}=\left(g-f-\sum_{k \leq n}\right. \left.\left(g_{k}-f_{k}\right)\right) \vee 0$, then $h_{n} \downarrow 0$ and $g-f \leq \Sigma_{k \leq n}\left(g_{k}-f_{k}\right)+h_{n}$. The positivity and continuity of $\Lambda$ now give $\nu_{0}(f, g] \leq \sum_{k} \nu_{0}\left(f_{k}, g_{k}\right]$. A similar, easier argument shows that $\sum_{k} \nu_{0}\left(f_{k}, g_{k}\right] \leq \nu_{0}(f, g)$ if ( $f_{k}, g_{k}$ ] are disjoint subsets of ( $f, g$ ].
11.5. (b) From (11.7) it follows that $[f>1] \in \mathscr{F}_{0}$ for $f$ in $\mathscr{L}$ Since $\mathscr{L}$ is linear, $[f>x]$ and $[f<-x]$ are in $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ for $f \in \mathscr{L}$ and $x>0$. Since the sets ( $x, \infty$ ) and $(-\infty,-x)$ for $x>0$ generate $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, each $f$ in $\mathscr{L}$ is measurable $\sigma\left(\mathscr{F}_{0}\right)$. Hence $\mathscr{F}=\sigma\left(\mathscr{F}_{0}\right)$.

It is easy to show that $\mathscr{F}_{0}$ is a semiring and is in fact closed under the formation of proper differences. It can happen that $\Omega \notin \mathscr{F}_{0}$-for example, in the case where $\Omega=\{1,2\}$ and $\mathscr{L}$ consists of the $f$ with $f(1)=0$. See Jurgen Kindler: A simple proof of the Daniell--Stone representation theorem. Amer. Math. Monthly, 90 (1983), 396-397.)

12.4. (a) If $\theta_{n}=\theta_{m}$, then $\theta_{n-m}=0$ and $n=m$ because $\theta$ is irrational. Take $K=\bigcap_{n} \cup_{u_{1}} \quad u_{n} \quad K_{u_{1}} \quad u_{n}$. The Cantor set is a special case.

## Section 13

13.3. If $f=\sum_{i} x_{i} I_{A_{i}}$ and $A_{i} \in T^{-1} \mathscr{F}^{\prime}$, take $A_{i}^{\prime}$ in $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$ so that $A_{i}=T^{-1} A_{i}^{\prime}$, and set $\varphi=\sum_{i} x_{i} I_{A_{i}^{\prime}}$. For the general $f$ measurable $T^{-1} \mathscr{F}^{\prime}$, there exist simple functions $f_{n}$, measurable $T^{-1} \mathscr{F}^{\prime}$, such that $f_{n}(\omega) \rightarrow f(\omega)$ for each $\omega$. Choose $\varphi_{n}$, measurable $\mathscr{F}^{\prime}$, so that $f_{n}=\varphi_{n} T$. Let $C^{\prime}$ be the set of $\omega^{\prime}$ for which $\varphi_{n}\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$ has a finite limit, and define $\varphi\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)=\lim _{n} \varphi_{n}\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)$ for $\omega^{\prime} \in C^{\prime}$ and $\varphi\left(\omega^{\prime}\right)=0$ for $\omega^{\prime} \notin C^{\prime}$. Theorem 20.1(ii) is a special case.
13.7. The class of Borel functions contains the continuous functions and is closed under pointwise passages to the limit and hence contains $\mathscr{X}$.

By imitating the proof of the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem, show that, if $f$ and $g$ lie in $\mathscr{X}$, then so do $f+g, f g, f-g, f \vee g$ (note that, for example, $[g: f+g \in \mathscr{X}]$ is closed under passages to the limit). If $f_{n}(x)$ is 1 or $1-n(x-\alpha)$ or 0 as $x \leq \alpha$ or $\alpha \leq x \leq \alpha+n^{-1}$ or $\alpha+n^{-1} \leq x$, then $f_{n}$ is continuous and $f_{n}(x) \rightarrow I_{(-\infty, \alpha]}(x)$. Show that $\left[A: I_{A} \in \mathscr{X}\right]$ is a $\lambda$-system. Conclude that $\mathscr{X}$ contains all indicators of Borel sets, all simple Borel functions, all Borel functions.
13.13. Let $B=\left\{b_{1}, \ldots, b_{k}\right\}$, where $k \leq n, E_{i}=C-b_{i}^{-1} A$, and $E=\bigcup_{i=1}^{k} E_{i}$. Then $E=C-\bigcup_{i=1}^{k} b_{i}^{-1} A$. Since $\mu$ is invariant under rotations, $\mu\left(E_{i}\right)=1-\mu(A)< n^{-1}$, and hence $\mu(E)<1$. Therefore $C-E=\bigcap_{i=1}^{k} b_{i}^{-1} A$ is nonempty. Use any $\theta$ in $C-E$.

## Section 14

14.3. (b) Since $u \leq F(x)$ is equivalent to $\varphi(u) \leq x$, it follows that $u \leq F(\varphi(u))$. And since $F(x)<u$ is equivalent to $x<\varphi(u)$, it follows further that $F(\varphi(u)-\epsilon)<u$ for positive $\epsilon$.
14.4. (a) If $0<u<v<1$, then $P[u \leq F(X)<v, X \in C]=P[\varphi(u) \leq X<\varphi(v), X \in C]$. If $\varphi(u) \in C$, this is at most $P[\varphi(u) \leq X<\varphi(v)]=F(\varphi(v)-)-F(\varphi(u)-) =F(\varphi(v)-)-F(\varphi(u)) \leq v-u$; if $\varphi(u) \notin C$, it is at most $P[\varphi(u)<X<\varphi(v)] =F(\varphi(v)-)-F(\varphi(u)) \leq v-u$. Thus $P[F(X) \in[u, v), X \in C] \leq \lambda[u, v)$ if $0< u<v<1$. This is true also for $u=0$ (let $u \downarrow 0$ and note that $P[F(X)=0]=0$ ) and for $v=1$ (let $v \uparrow 1$ ). The finite disjoint unions of intervals $[u, v)$ in $[0,1)$ form a field there, and by addition $P[F(X) \in A, X \in C] \leq \lambda(A)$ for $A$ in this field. By the monotone class theorem, the inequality holds for all Borel sets in $[0,1)$. Since $P[F(X)=1, X \in C]=0$, this holds also for $A=\{1\}$.
14.5. The sufficiency is easy. To prove necessity, choose continuity points $x_{i}$ of $F$ in such a way that $x_{0}<x_{1}<\cdots<x_{k}, F\left(x_{0}\right)<\epsilon, F\left(x_{k}\right)>1-\epsilon$, and $x_{i}-x_{i-1}< \epsilon$. If $n$ exceeds some $n_{0},\left|F\left(x_{i}\right)-F_{n}\left(x_{i}\right)\right|<\epsilon / 2$ for all $i$. Suppose that $x_{i-1} \leq x \leq x_{i}$. Then $F_{n}(x) \leq F_{n}\left(x_{i}\right) \leq F\left(x_{i}\right)+\epsilon / 2 \leq F(x+\epsilon)+\epsilon / 2$. Establish a similar inequality going the other direction, and give special arguments for the cases $x \leq x_{0}$ and $x \geq x_{k}$.

## Section 15

15.1. Suppose there is an $\mathscr{F}_{\text {-partition such that }} \Sigma_{i}\left[\sup _{A} f\right] \mu\left(A_{i}\right)<\infty$. Then $a_{i}=\sup _{A_{i}} f<\infty$ for $i$ in the set $I$ of indices for which $\mu\left(A_{i}\right)>0$. If $a= \max _{l} a_{i}$, then $\mu[f>a]=\Sigma_{, \mu}\left(A_{i} \cap[f>a]\right) \leq \Sigma_{, \mu}\left(A_{i} \cap\left[f>a_{i}\right]\right)=0$. And $A_{i} \cap[f>0]=\varnothing$ for $i$ outside the set $J$ of indices for which $\mu\left(A_{i}\right)<\infty$, so that $\mu[f>0]=\Sigma \mu\left(A_{i} \cap[f>0]\right) \leq \Sigma_{j} \mu\left(A_{i}\right)<\infty$.
15.4. Let ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}^{+}, \mu^{+}$) be the completion (Problems 3.10 and 10.5) of ( $\Omega, \mathscr{F}, \mu$ ). If $g$ is measurable $\mathscr{F},[f \neq g] \subset A, A \in \mathscr{F}, \mu(A)=0$, and $H \in \mathscr{R}^{1}$, then $[f \in H]= \left(A^{c} \cap[f \in H]\right) \cup(A \cap[f \in H])=\left(A^{c} \cap[g \in H]\right) \cup(A \cap[f \in H])$ lies in $\mathscr{F}^{+}$, and hence $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}^{+}$.
(a) Since $f$ is measurable $\mathscr{F}^{+}$, it will be enough to prove that for each (finite) $\mathscr{F}^{+}$-partition ( $B_{j}$ ) there is an $\mathscr{F}$-partition ( $A_{i}$ ) such that $\Sigma_{i}\left[\inf _{A_{i}} f\right] \mu\left(A_{i}\right) \geq \sum_{j}\left[\inf _{B_{i}} f\right] \mu^{+}\left(B_{j}\right)$, and to prove the dual relation for the upper sums. Choose (Problem 3.2) $\mathscr{F}_{\text {-sets }} A_{i}$ so that $A_{i} \subset B_{i}$ and $\mu\left(A_{i}\right)=\mu_{*}\left(B_{i}\right)=\mu^{+}\left(B_{i}\right)$. For the partition consisting of the $A_{i}$ together with $\left(\bigcup_{i} A_{i}\right)^{c}$, the lower sum is at least $\Sigma_{i}\left[\inf _{B_{i}} f\right] \mu\left(A_{i}\right)=\Sigma_{i}\left[\inf _{B_{i}} f\right] \mu^{+}\left(B_{i}\right)$.
(b) Choose successively finer $\mathscr{F}$ partitions $\left\{A_{n i}\right\}$ in such a way that the corresponding upper and lower sums differ by at most $1 / n^{3}$. Let $g_{n}$ and $f_{n}$ have values $\inf _{A_{i}} f$ and $\sup _{A_{i}} f$ on $A_{n i}$. Use Markov's inequality-since $\mu(\Omega)$ is finite, it may as well be 1 -to show that $\mu\left[f_{n}-g_{n} \geq 1 / n\right] \leq 1 / n^{2}$, and then use the first Borel-Cantelli lemma to show that $f_{n}-g_{n} \rightarrow 0$ almost everywhere. Take $g=\lim _{n} g_{n}$.

## Section 16

16.3. $0 \leq f_{n}-f_{1} \uparrow f-f_{1}$.
16.4. (a) By Fatou's lemma,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int f d \mu-\int a d \mu & =\int \lim _{n}\left(f_{n}-a_{n}\right) d \mu \\
& \leq \lim \inf _{n} \int\left(f_{n}-a_{n}\right) d \mu=\lim \inf _{n} \int f_{n} d \mu-\int a d \mu
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int b d \mu-\int f d \mu & =\int \lim _{n}\left(b_{n}-f_{n}\right) d \mu \\
& \leq \lim \inf _{n} \int\left(b_{n}-f_{n}\right) d \mu=\int b d \mu-\lim \sup _{n} \int f_{n} d \mu
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore

$$
\lim \sup _{n} \int f_{n} d \mu \leq \int f d \mu \leq \lim \inf _{n} \int f_{n} d \mu
$$

16.6. For $\omega \in A$ and small enough complex $h$,

$$
\left|f\left(\omega, z_{0}+h\right)-f\left(\omega, z_{0}\right)\right|=\left|\int_{z_{0}}^{z_{0}+h} f^{\prime}(\omega, z) d z\right| \leq|h| g\left(\omega, z_{0}\right)
$$

16.8. Use the fact that $\int_{A}|f| d \mu \leq \alpha \mu(A)+\int_{[|f| \geq \alpha]}|f| \mathrm{d} \mu$.
16.9. If $\mu(A)<\delta$ implies $\int_{A}\left|f_{n}\right| \mathrm{d} \mu<\epsilon$ for all $n$, and if $\alpha^{-1} \sup _{n}| | f_{n} \mid \mathrm{d} \mu<\delta$, then $\mu\left[\left|f_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right] \leq \alpha^{-1} f\left|f_{n}\right| \mathrm{d} \mu<\delta$ and hence $f_{\left[\left|f_{n}\right| \geq \alpha\right]}\left|f_{n}\right| \mathrm{d} \mu<\epsilon$ for all $n$. For the reverse implication adapt the argument in the preceding note.
16.10. (b) Suppose that $f_{n}$ are nonnegative and satisfy condition (ii) and $\mu$ is nonatomic. Choose $\delta$ so that $\mu(A) \leq \delta$ implies $\int_{A} f_{n} d \mu \leq 1$ for all $n$. If $\mu\left[f_{n}=\infty\right]>0$, there is an $A$ such that $A \subset\left[f_{n}=\infty\right]$ and $0<\mu(A)<\delta$; but then $\int_{A} f_{n} d \mu=\infty$. Since $\mu\left[f_{n}=\infty\right]=0$, there is an $\alpha$ such that $\mu\left[f_{n}>\alpha\right] \leq \delta \leq \mu\left[f_{n} \geq \alpha\right]$. Choose $B \subset\left[f_{n}=\alpha\right]$ in such a way that $A=\left[f_{n}>\alpha\right] \cup B$ satisfies $\mu(A)=\delta$. Then $\alpha \delta=\alpha \mu(A) \leq \int_{A} f_{n} d \mu \leq 1$ and $\int f_{n} d \mu \leq 1+\alpha \mu\left(A^{c}\right) \leq 1+ \delta^{-1} \mu(\Omega)$.
16.12. (b) Suppose that $f \in \mathscr{L}$ and $f \geq 0$. If $f_{n}=\left(1-n^{-1}\right) f \vee 0$, then $f_{n} \in \mathscr{L}$ and $f_{n} \uparrow f$, so that $\nu\left(f_{n}, f\right]=\Lambda\left(f-f_{n}\right) \downarrow 0$. Since $\nu\left(f_{1}, f\right]<\infty$, it follows that $\nu[(\omega, t)$ : $f(\omega)=t]=0$. The disjoint union

$$
B_{n}=\bigcup_{i=1}^{n 2^{n}}\left(\left\lfloor\frac{i}{2^{n}}<f \leq \frac{i+1}{2^{n}}\right\rfloor \times\left(0, \frac{i}{2^{n}}\right\rfloor\right)
$$

increases to $B$, where $B \subset(0, f]$ and $(0, f]-B \subset[(\omega, t): f(\omega)=t]$. Therefore

$$
\Lambda(f)=\nu(0, f]=\lim _{n} \nu\left(B_{n}\right)=\lim _{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n 2^{n}} \frac{i}{2^{n}} \mu\left[\frac{i}{2^{n}}<f \leq \frac{i+1}{2^{n}}\right]=\int f d \mu
$$

## Section 17

17.1. (a) Let $A_{\epsilon}$ be the set of $x$ such that for every $\delta$ there are points $y$ and $z$ satisfying $|y-x|<\delta,|z-x|<\delta$, and $|f(y)-f(z)| \geq \varepsilon$. Show that $A_{\epsilon}$ is closed and $D_{f}$ is the unior of the $A_{\epsilon}$.
(c) Given $\epsilon$ and $\eta$, choose a partition into intervals $I_{i}$ for which the corresponding upper and lower sums differ by at most $\epsilon \eta$. By considering those $\bar{I}_{i}$ whose interiors meet $A_{\epsilon}$, show that $\epsilon \eta \geq \epsilon \lambda\left(A_{\epsilon}\right)$.
(d) Let $M$ bound $|f|$ and, given $\epsilon$, find an open $G$ such that $D_{f} \subset G$ and $\lambda(G)<\epsilon / M$. Take $\mathcal{C}=[0,1]-G$ and show by compactness that there is a $\delta$ such that $|f(y)-f(x)|<\epsilon$ if $x$ (but perhaps not $y$ ) lies in $C$ and $|y-x|<\delta$. If $[0,1]$ is decomposed into intervals $I_{i}$ with $\lambda\left(I_{i}\right)<\delta$, and if $x_{i} \in I_{i}$, let $g$ be the function with value $f\left(x_{i}\right)$ on $I_{i}$. Let $\Sigma^{\prime}$ denote summation over those $i$ for which $I_{i}$ meets $C$, and let $\Sigma^{\prime \prime}$ denote summation over the other $i$. Show that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|\int_{0}^{1} f(x) d x-\sum f\left(x_{i}\right) \lambda\left(I_{i}\right)\right| & \leq \int_{0}^{1}|f(x)-g(x)| d x \\
& \leq \sum^{\prime} 2 \epsilon \lambda\left(I_{i}\right)+\sum^{\prime \prime} 2 M \lambda\left(I_{i}\right)<4 \epsilon
\end{aligned}
$$

17.10. (c) Do not overlook the possibility that points in $(0,1)-K$ converge to a point in $K$.
17.11. (b) Apply the bounded convergence theorem to $f_{n}(x)=(1-n \operatorname{dist}(x,[s, t]))^{+}$.
(c) The class of Borel sets $B$ in $[u, v]$ for which $f=I_{B}$ satisfies (17.8) is a $\lambda$-system.
(e) Choose simple $f_{n}$ such that $0 \leq f_{n} \uparrow f$ To (17.8) for $f=f_{n}$, apply the monotone convergence theorem on the right and the dominated convergence theorem on the left.
17.12. If $g(x)$ is the distance from $x$ to $[a, b]$, then $f_{n}=(1-n g) \vee 0 \downarrow I_{[a, b]}$ and $f_{n} \in \mathscr{L}$; since the continuous functions are measurable $\mathscr{R}^{1}$, it follows that $\mathscr{F}=\mathscr{P}^{1}$. If $f_{n}(x) \downarrow 0$ for each $x$, then the compact sets $\left[x: f_{n}(x) \geq \epsilon\right]$ decrease to $\varnothing$ and hence one of them is $\varnothing$; thus the convergence is uniform.
17.13. The linearity and positivity of $\Lambda$ are certainly elementary facts, and for the continuity property, note that if $0 \leq f \leq \epsilon$ and $f$ vanishes outside $[a, b]$, then elementary considerations slow that $0 \leq \Lambda(f) \leq \epsilon(b-a)$.

## Section 18

18.2. First, $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{X}$ is generated by the sets of the forms $(x) \times X$ and $X \times\{x\}$. If the diagonal $E$ lies in $\mathscr{X} \times \mathscr{X}$, then there must be a countable $S$ in $X$ such that $E$ lies in the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{F}$ generated by the sets of these two forms for $x$ in $S$. If $\mathscr{P}$ consists of $S^{c}$ and the singletons in $S$, then $\mathscr{F}$ is the class of unions of sets in the partition $\left[P_{1} \times P_{2}: P_{1}, P_{2} \in \mathscr{P}\right]$. But $E \in \mathscr{F}$ is impossible.
18.3. Consider $A \times B$, where $A$ consists of a single point and $B$ lies outside the completion of $\mathscr{R}^{1}$ with respect to $\lambda$.
18.17. Put $f_{p}=p^{-1} \log p$, and put $f_{n}=0$ if $n$ is not a prime In the notation of (18.17), $F(x)=\log x+\varphi(x)$, where $\varphi$ is bounded bacause of (5.51). If $G(x)= -1 / \log x$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{p \leq x} \frac{1}{p} & =\frac{F(x)}{\log x}+\int_{2}^{x} \frac{F(t) d t}{t \log ^{2} t} \\
& =1+\frac{\varphi(x)}{\log x}+\int_{2}^{x} \frac{d t}{t \log t}+\int_{2}^{\infty} \frac{\varphi(t) d t}{t \log ^{2} t}-\int_{x}^{\infty} \frac{\varphi(t) d t}{t \log ^{2} t} .
\end{aligned}
$$

## Section 19

19.3. See Banach, p. 34.
19.4. (a) Take $f=0$ and $f_{n}=I_{(0,1 / n)}$ -
(b) Take $f=0$, and let $\left\{f_{n}\right\}$ be an infinite orthonormal set. Use the fact that $\sum_{n}\left(f_{n}, g\right)^{2} \leq\|g\|^{2}$.
19.5. Take $f_{n}=n I_{(0,1 / n)}$, and suppose that $f_{n_{k}}$ converges weakly to some $f$ in $L^{1}$. Integrate against the $L^{\infty}$-functions $\operatorname{sgn} f \cdot I_{\left(\epsilon_{1}\right)}$ and conclude that $f=0$ almost everywhere; now integrate against the function identically 1 and get a contradiction.

## Section 20

20.4. Suppose $U_{1}, \ldots, U_{k}$ are independent and uniformly distributed over the unit interval, put $V_{i}=2 n U_{i}-n$, and let $\mu_{n}$ be $(2 n)^{k}$ times the distribution of
$\left(V_{1}, \ldots, V_{k}\right)$. Then $\mu_{n}$ is supported by $Q_{n}=(-n, n] \times \cdots \times(-n, n]$, and if $I=\left(a_{1}, b_{1}\right] \times \cdots \times\left(a_{k}, b_{k}\right] \subset Q_{n}$, then $\mu_{n}(I)=\prod_{i=1}^{k}\left(b_{i}-a_{i}\right)$. Further, if $A \subset Q_{n} \subset Q_{m}(n<m)$, then $\mu_{n}(A)=\mu_{m}(A)$. Define $\lambda_{k}(A)=\lim _{n} \mu_{n}\left(A \cap Q_{n}\right)$.
20.7. By the argument preceding (8.16),

$$
P_{i}\left[T_{1}=n_{1}, \ldots, T_{k}=n_{k}\right]=f_{i j}^{\left(n_{1}\right)} f_{j j}^{\left(n_{2}-n_{1}\right)} \ldots f_{j j}^{\left(n_{k}-n_{k}-1\right)} .
$$

For the general initial distribution, average over $i$.
20.8. (a) Use the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem to show that $P\left[\left(X_{\pi!}, \ldots, X_{\pi n}\right) \in H\right]$ is the same for all permutations $\pi$.
(b) Use part (a) and the fact that $Y_{n}=r$ if and only if $T_{r}^{(n)}=n$.
(c) If $k \leq n$, then $Y_{k}=r$ if and only if exactly $r-1$ among the integers $1, . ., k-1$ precede $k$ in the permutation $T^{(n)}$.
(d) Observe that $T^{(n)}=\left(t_{1}, \ldots, t_{n}\right)$ and $Y_{n+1}=r$ if and oniy if $T^{(n+1)}=$ ( $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{r-1}, n+1, t_{r}, \ldots, t_{n}$ ), and conclude that $\sigma\left(Y_{n+1}\right)$ is independent of $\sigma\left(T^{(n)}\right)$ and hence of $\sigma\left(Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{n}\right)$-see Problem 20.6.
20.12. If $X$ and $Y$ are independent, then

$$
P[|(X+Y)-(x+y)|<\epsilon] \geq P\left[|X-x|<\frac{1}{2} \epsilon\right] P\left[|Y-y|<\frac{1}{2} \epsilon\right]
$$

and

$$
P[X+Y=x+y] \geq P[X=x] P[Y=y]
$$

20.14. The partial-fraction expansion gives

$$
c_{u}(y-x) c_{l}(x)=\frac{u v}{\pi^{2}} \frac{1}{R}(A+B+C+D)
$$

where $R=\left(u^{2}-v^{2}\right)^{2}+2\left(u^{2}+v^{2}\right) y^{2}+y^{4}$ and

$$
\begin{array}{cl}
A=\frac{y^{2}+v^{2}-u^{2}}{u^{2}+(y-x)^{2}}, & B=\frac{2 y(y-x)}{u^{2}+(y-x)^{2}} \\
C=\frac{y^{2}-v^{2}+u^{2}}{v^{2}+x^{2}}, & D=\frac{2 y x}{v^{2}+x^{2}}
\end{array}
$$

After the fact this can of course be checked mechanically. Integrate over $[-t, t]$ and let $t \rightarrow \infty: \int_{-,}^{t} D d x=0, \int_{-,}^{t} B d x \rightarrow 0$, and $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(A+C) d x=\left(y^{2}+\right. \left.v^{2}-u^{2}\right) u^{-1} \pi+\left(y^{2}-v^{2}+u^{2}\right) v^{-1} \pi=u^{-1} v^{-1} \pi^{2} R c_{u+1}(y)$. There is a very simple proof by characteristic functions; see Problem 26.9.
20.16. See Example 20.1 for the case $n=1$, prove by inductive convolution and a change of variable that the density must have the form $K_{n} x^{(n / 2)-1} e^{-x / 2}$, and then from the fact that the density must integrate to 1 deduce the form of $K_{n}$.
20.17. Show by (20.38) and a change of variable that the left side of (20.48) is some constant times the right side; then show that the constant must be 1 .
20.20. (a) Given $\epsilon$ choose $M$ so that $P[|X|>M]<\epsilon$ and $P[|Y|>M]<\epsilon$, and then choose $\delta$ so that $|x|,|y| \leq M,\left|x-x^{\prime}\right|<\delta$, and $\left|y-y^{\prime}\right|<\delta$ imply that $\left|f\left(x^{\prime}, y^{\prime}\right)-f(x, y)\right|<\epsilon$. Note that $P\left[\left|f\left(X_{n}, Y_{n}\right)-f(X, Y)\right| \geq \epsilon\right] \leq 2 \epsilon+P\left[\mid X_{n}-\right. X \mid \geq \delta]+P\left[\left|Y_{n}-Y\right| \geq \delta\right]$.
20.23. Take, for example, independent $X_{n}$ assuming the values 0 and $n$ with probabilities $1-n^{-1}$ and $n^{-1}$. Estimate the probability that $X_{k}=k$ for some $k$ in the range $n / 2<k \leq n$.
20.24. (b) For each $m$ split $A$ into $2^{m}$ sets $A_{m k}$ of probability $P(A) / 2^{M}$. Arrange all the $A_{m k}$ in one infinite sequence, and lct $X_{n}$ be the indicator of the $n$th set in it.
20.27. To get the distribution of $\Phi$, show by integration that for $0 \leq \phi \leq 2 \pi$, the intersection with the unit ball of the $\left(x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}\right)$-set where $0 \leq x_{3} \leq\left(x_{1}^{2}+\right. \left.x_{2}^{2}\right)^{1 / 2} \tan \phi$ has volume $\frac{2}{3} \pi \sin \phi$.

## Section 21

21.5. Consider $\sum I_{A_{a l}}$. A random variable is finite with probability 1 if (but not only if) it is integrable.
21.6. Calculate $\int_{0}^{\infty} x d F(x)=\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{0}^{x} d y d F(x)=\int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{y}^{\infty} d F(x) d y$.
21.8. (a) Write $E[Y-X]=\int_{X<Y} \int_{X<r \leq Y} d t d P-\int_{Y<X} \int_{Y<r \leq x} d t d P$.
21.10. (a) The most important dependent uncorrelated random variables are the trigonometric functions-the random variables $\sin 2 \pi n \omega$ and $\cos 2 \pi n \omega$ on the unit interval with Lebesgue measure. See Problem 19.8.
21.13. Use Fubini's theorem; see (20.29) and (20.30).
21.14. Even if $X=-Y$ is not integrable, $X+Y=0$ is. Since $|Y| \leq|x|+|x+Y|$, $E[|Y|]=\infty$ implies that $E[|x+Y|]=\infty$ for each $x$; use Problem 21.13. See also the lemma in Section 28.
