## Section 22

22.2. For sufficiency, use $E\left[\Sigma\left|X_{n}^{(c)}\right|\right]=\Sigma E\left[\left|X_{n}^{(c)}\right|\right]$.
22.8. (a) Put $U=\sum_{k} I_{[k \leq \tau]} X_{k}^{+}$and $V=\sum_{k} I_{[k \leq \tau]} X_{k}^{-}$, so that $S_{\tau}=U-V$. Since $[\tau \geq k]=\Omega-[\tau \leq k-1]$ lies in $\sigma\left(X_{1}, \ldots, \bar{X}_{k-1}\right)$, it follows that $E\left[I_{[\tau \geq k]} X_{k}^{+}\right]= E\left[I_{[\tau \geq k]}\right] E\left[X_{k}^{+}\right]=P[\tau \geq k] E\left[X_{1}^{+}\right]$. Hence $E[U]=\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} E\left[X_{1}^{+}\right] P[\tau \geq k]= E\left[X_{1}^{+}\right] E[\tau]$. Treat $V$ the same way.
(b) To prove $E[\tau]<\infty$, show that $P[\tau>(a+b) n] \leq\left(1-p^{a+b}\right)^{n}$. By (7.7), $S_{\tau}$ is $b$ with probability $\left(1-\rho^{a}\right) /\left(1-\rho^{a+b}\right)$ and $-a$ with the opposite probability. Since $E\left[X_{\mathrm{t}}\right]=p-q$,

$$
E[\tau]=\frac{a}{q-p}-\frac{a+b}{q-p} \frac{1-\rho^{a}}{1-\rho^{a+b}}, \quad \rho=\frac{q}{p} \neq 1
$$

22.11. For each $\theta, \sum_{n} e^{i X_{n}}\left(e^{i \theta} z\right)^{n}$ has the same probabilistic behavior as the original series, because the $X_{n}+n \theta$ reduced modulo $2 \pi$ are independent and uniformly distributed. Therefore, the rotation idea in the proof of Theorem 22.9 carries over. See Kahane for further results.
22.14. (b) Let $A=f^{-1} B$ and suppose $p$ is a period of $f$. Let $m=\lfloor x / p\rfloor$ and $n=\lfloor 1 / p\rfloor$. By periodicity, $P(A \cap[y, y+p])$ is the same for all $y$; therefore, $|P(A \cap[0, x])-m P(A \cap[0, p])| \leq p,|P(A)-n P(A \cap[0, p))| \leq p$, and $|P(A \cap[0, x])-P(A) x| \leq 2 p+|x-m / n| \leq 3 p$. Since $p$ can be taken arbitrarily small, $P(A \cap[0, x])=P(A) x$.
22.15. (a) By the inequalities $L(s) \leq M(s)$ and (22.24),

$$
B_{E}(2 s)=1 \wedge 3 L(2 s) \leq 3 M(2 s) \leq 3 B_{O}(s)
$$

For the other inequality, note first that $T(s)$ is nonincreasing and that $R(2 s) \leq 2 L(s)$ and $T(s) \leq L(s) \leq M(s)$. If $B_{E}(s) \leq 1 / 3$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
B_{o}(6 s) & \leq \frac{T(6 s)}{1-R(6 s)} \leq \frac{T(3 s)}{1-2 L(3 s)} \leq \frac{M(3 s)}{1-2 M(3 s)} \\
& \leq \frac{B_{E}(s)}{1-2 B_{E}(s)} \leq 3 B_{E}(s)
\end{aligned}
$$

On the other hand, if $B_{E}(s)>1 / 3$, then $B_{o}(6 s) \leq 1<3 B_{E}(s)$. In either case, $B_{O}(6 s) \leq 3 B_{E}(s)$.

## Section 23

23.3. Note that $A_{t}$ cannot exceed $t$. If $0 \leq u \leq t$ and $v \geq 0$, then $P\left[A_{t} \geq u, B_{t}>v\right]= P\left[N_{t+t}-N_{t-u}=0\right]=e^{-\alpha u} e^{-\alpha t}$.
23.4. (a) Use (20.37) and the distributions of $A_{t}$ and $B_{t}$.
(b) A long interarrival interval has a better chance of covering $t$ than a short one does.
23.6. The probability that $N_{S_{u+k}}^{\prime}-N_{S_{u}}^{\prime}=j$ is

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-\beta x} \frac{(\beta x)^{j}}{j!} \frac{\alpha^{k}}{\Gamma(k)} x^{k-1} e^{-\alpha x} d x=\frac{\alpha^{k} \beta^{j}}{(\alpha+\beta)^{k+j}} \frac{(j+k-1)!}{j!(k-1)!}
$$

23.8. Let $M_{t}$ be the given process and put $\varphi(t)=E\left[M_{t}\right]$. Since there are no fixed discontinuities, $\varphi(t)$ is continuous. Let $\psi(u)=\inf [t: u \leq \varphi(t)]$, and show that $N_{u}=M_{\psi(u)}$ is an ordinary Poisson process and $M_{t}=N_{\varphi(t)}$.
23.9. Let $t \rightarrow \infty$ in

$$
\frac{S_{N_{t}}}{N_{t}} \leq \frac{t}{N_{t}} \leq \frac{S_{N_{t}+1}}{N_{t}+1} \frac{N_{t}+1}{N_{t}}
$$

23.11. Restrict $t$ in Problem 23.10 to integers. The waiting times are the $Z_{n}$ of Problem 20.7, and account must be taken of the fact that the distribution of $Z_{1}$ may differ from that of the other $Z_{n}$.

## Section 25

25.1. (e) Let $G$ be an open set that contains the rationals and satisfies $\lambda(G)<\frac{1}{2}$. For $k=0,1, \ldots, n-1$, construct a triangle whose base contains $k / n$ and is contained in $G$ : make these bases so narrow that they do not overlap, and adjust the heights of the triangles so that each has area $1 / n$. For the $n$th density, piece together these triangular functions, and for the limit density, use the function identically 1 over the unit interval.
25.2. By Problem 14.8 it suffices to prove that $F_{n}(, \omega) \Rightarrow F$ with probability 1 , and for this it is enough that $F_{n}(x, \omega) \rightarrow F(x)$ with probability 1 for each rational $x$.
25.3. (b) It can be shown, for example, that (25.14) holds for $x_{n}=n!$. See Persi Diaconis: The distribution of leading digits and uniform distribution mod1, Ann. Prob., 5 (1977), 72-81.
(c) The first significant digits of numbers drawn at random from empirical compilations such as almanacs and engineering handbooks seem approximately to follow the limiting distribution in (25.15) rather than the uniform distribution over $1,2, \ldots, 9$. This is sometimes called Benford's law. One explanation is that the distribution of the observation $X$ and hence of $\log _{10} X$ will be spread over a large interval; if $\log _{10} X$ has a reasonably smooth density, it then seems plausible that $\left\{\log _{10} X\right\}$ should be approximately uniformly distributed. See Feller, Volume 2, p. 62.
25.9. Use Scheffé's theorem.
25.10. Put $f_{n}(x)=P\left[X_{n}=\gamma_{n}+k \delta_{n}\right] \delta_{n}^{-1}$ for $\gamma_{n}+k \delta_{n}<x \leq \gamma_{n}+(k+1) \delta_{n}$. Construct random variables $Y_{n}$ with densities $f_{n}$, and first prove $Y_{n} \Rightarrow X$. Show that $Z_{n}=\gamma_{n}+\left[\left(Y_{n}-\gamma_{n}\right) / \delta_{n}\right] \delta_{n}$ has the distribution of $X_{n}$ and that $Y_{n}-Z_{n} \Rightarrow 0$.
25.11. For a proof of (25.16) see Feller, Volume 1, Chapter 7.
25.13. (b) Follow the proof of Theorem 25.8, but approximate $I_{(x, y]}$ instead of $I_{(-\infty, x]}$
25.20. Let $X_{n}$ assume the values $n$ and 0 with probabilities $p_{n}=1 /(n \log n)$ and $1-p_{n}$.

## Section 26

26.1. (b) Let $\mu$ be the distribution of $X$. If $|\varphi(t)|=1$ and $t \neq 0$, then $\varphi(t)=e^{i t a}$ for some $a$, and $0=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\left(1-e^{i t(x-a)}\right) \mu(d x)=\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(1-\cos t(x-a)) \mu(d x)$. Since the integral vanishes, $\mu$ must confine its mass to the points where the nonnegative integrand vanishes, namely to the points $x$ for which $t(x-a)=2 \pi n$ for some integer $n$.
(c) The mass of $\mu$ concentrates at points of the form $a+2 \pi n / t$ and also at points of the form $a^{\prime}+2 \pi n / t^{\prime}$. If $\mu$ is positive at two distinct points, it follows that $t / t^{\prime}$ is rational.
26.3. (a) Let $f_{0}(x)=\pi^{-1} x^{-2}(1-\cos x)$ be the density corresponding to $\varphi_{0}(t)$. If $p_{k}=\left(s_{k}-s_{k+1}\right) t_{k}$, then $\Sigma_{k=1}^{\infty} p_{k}=1$; since $\Sigma_{k=1}^{\infty} p_{k} \varphi_{0}\left(t / t_{k}\right)=\varphi(t)$ (check the points $\left.t=t_{j}\right), \varphi(t)$ is the characteristic function of the continuous density $\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} p_{k} t_{k} f_{0}\left(t_{k}, x\right)$.
(b) If $\lim _{t \rightarrow \infty} \varphi(t)=0$, approximate $\varphi$ by functions of the kind in part (a), pass to the limit, and use the first corollary to the continuity theorem. If $\varphi$ does not vanish at infinity, mix in a unit mass at 0 .
26.12. On the right in (26.30) replace $\varphi(t)$ by the integral defining it and apply Fubini's theorem; the integral average comes to

$$
\mu\{a\}+\int_{x \neq a} \frac{\sin T(x-a)}{T(x-a)} \mu(d x)
$$

Now use the bounded convergence theorem.
26.15. (a) Use (26.40) to prove that $\left|\varphi_{n}(t+h)-\varphi_{n}(t)\right| \leq 2 \mu_{n}(-a . a)^{c}+a|h|$.
(b) Use part (a).
26.17. (a) Use the second corollary to the continuity theorem.
26.19. For the Weierstrass approximation theorem, see Rubin ${ }_{1}$, Theorem 7.32.
26.22. (a) If $a_{n}$ goes to 0 along a subsequence, then $|\psi(t)| \equiv 1$; use part (c) of Problem 26.1.
(c) Suppose two subsequences of $\left\{a_{n}\right\}$ converge to $a_{0}$ and $a$, where $0<a_{0}<a$; put $\theta=a_{0} / a$ and show that $|\varphi(t)|=\left|\varphi\left(\theta^{k} t\right)\right|$.
(d) Observe that

$$
b_{n}=-i\left[e^{i t b_{n}}-1\right]\left[\int_{0}^{t} e^{i s b_{n}} d s\right]^{-1}
$$

26.25. First do the nonnegative case; then note that if $f$ and $g$ have the same coefficients, so do $f^{+}+g^{-}$and $g^{+}+f^{-}$

## Section 27

27.8. By the same reasoning as in Example 27.3, $\left(R_{n}-\log n\right) / \sqrt{\log n} \Rightarrow N$.
27.9. The Lindeberg theorem applies: $\left(S_{n}-n^{2} / 4\right) / \sqrt{n^{3} / 36} \Rightarrow N$.
27.11. Let $Y_{n}$ be $X_{n}$ or 0 according as $\left|X_{n}\right| \leq n^{1 / 2} \log n$ or not. Show that $X_{n}=Y_{n}$ for large $n$, with probability 1 , and that Lyapounov's theorem ( $\delta=1$ ) applies to the $Y_{n}$.
27.12. For example, let the distribution of $X_{n}$ be the mixture, with weights $1-n^{-2}$ and $n^{-2}$, of the standard normal and Cauchy distributions.
27.16. Write $\int_{x}^{\infty} e^{-u^{2} / 2} d u=x^{-1} e^{-x^{2} / 2}-\int_{x}^{\infty} u^{-2} e^{-u^{2} / 2} d u$.
27.17. For another approach to large-deviation theory, see Mark Pinsky: An elementary derivation of Khintchine's estimate for large deviations, Proc. Amer. Math. Soc., 22 (1969), 288-290.
27.19. (a) Everything comes from (4.7). If $A=\left[\left(l_{1}, \ldots, l_{k}\right) \in H\right]$ and $B \in \sigma\left(l_{k+n}, l_{k+n+1}, \ldots\right)$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
& |P(A \cap B)-P(A) P(B)| \\
& \quad \leq \sum\left|P\left(\left[l_{u}=i_{u}, u \leq k\right] \cap B\right)-P\left[l_{u}=i_{u}, u \leq k\right] P(B)\right|,
\end{aligned}
$$

where the sum extends over the $k$-tuples ( $i_{1}, \ldots, i_{k}$ ) of nonnegative integers in $H$. The summand vanishes if $u+i_{u}<k+n$ for $u \leq k$, the remaining terms add to at $\operatorname{most} 2 \sum_{u=1}^{k} P\left[l_{11} \geq k+n-u\right] \leq 4 / 2^{n}$
(b) To show that $\sigma^{2}=6$ (see (27.20)), show that $l_{1}$ has mean 1 and variance 2 and that

$$
\int_{\left[I_{1}=i\right]} l_{1} l_{1+n} d P= \begin{cases}P\left[l_{1}=i\right] i E\left[l_{1+n}\right] & \text { if } i<n, \\ P\left[l_{1}=i\right] i(i-n) & \text { if } i \geq n .\end{cases}
$$

## Section 28

28.2. (b) Pass to a subsequence along which $\mu_{n}\left(R^{1}\right) \rightarrow \infty$, choose $\epsilon_{n}$ so that it decreases to 0 and $\epsilon_{n} \mu_{n}\left(R^{1}\right) \rightarrow \infty$, and choose $x_{n}$ so that it increases to $\infty$ and $\mu_{n}\left(-x_{n}, x_{n}\right)>\frac{1}{2} \mu_{n}\left(R^{1}\right)$; consider the $f$ that satisfies $f\left( \pm x_{n}\right)=\epsilon_{n}$ for all $n$ and is defined by linear interpolation in between these points.
28.4. (a) If all functions (28.12) are characteristic functions, they are all certainly infinitely divisible. Since (28.12) is continuous at 0 , it need only be exhibited as a limit of characteristic functions. If $\mu_{n}$ has density $I_{[-n, n]}\left(1+x^{2}\right)$ with respect to $\nu$, then

$$
\exp \left[i \gamma t+i t \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{x}{1+x^{2}} \mu_{n}(d x)+\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\left(e^{i t x}-1-i t x\right) \frac{1}{x^{2}} \mu_{n}(d x)\right]
$$

is a characteristic function and converges to (28.12). It can also be shown that every infinitely divisible distribution (no moments required) has characteristic function of the form (28.12); see Gnedenko \& Kolmogorov, p. 76.
(b) Use (see Problem 18.19) $-|t|=\pi^{-1} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(\cos t x-1) x^{-2} d x$.
28.14. If $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ are independent and have distribution function $F$, then ( $X_{1}+ \left.\cdots+X_{n}\right) / \sqrt{n}$ also has distribution function $F$. Apply the central limit theorem.
28.15. The characteristic function of $Z_{n}$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\exp \frac{c}{n} \sum_{k} \frac{1}{(|k| / n)^{1+\alpha}}\left(e^{i t k / n}-1\right) & \rightarrow \exp c \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{e^{i t x}-1}{|x|^{1+\alpha}} d x \\
& =\exp \left[-c|t|^{\alpha} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{1-\cos x}{|x|^{1+\alpha}} d x\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

## Section 29

29.1. (a) If $f$ is lower semicontinuous, $[x: f(x)>t]$ is open. If $f$ is positive, which is no restriction, then $\int f d \mu=j_{0}^{\infty} \mu[f>t] d t \leq \int_{0}^{\infty} \liminf _{n} \mu_{n}[f>t] d t \leq \liminf \int_{n}^{\infty} \mu_{n}[f>t] d t=\operatorname{limin} \mathrm{f}_{n} \int f d \mu_{n}$.
(b) If $G$ is open, then $I_{G}$ is lower semicontinuous.
29.7. Let $\Sigma$ be the covariance matrix. Let $M$ be an orthogonal matrix such that the entries of $M \Sigma M^{\prime}$ are 0 except for the first $r$ diagonal entries, which are 1 . If $Y=M X$, then $Y$ has covariance matrix $M \Sigma M^{\prime}$, and so $Y=\left(Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{r}, 0, \ldots, 0\right)$, where $Y_{1}, \ldots, Y_{r}$ are independent and have the standard normal distribution. But $|X|^{2}=\sum_{i=1}^{k} Y_{i}^{2}$.
29.8. By Theorem 29.5, $X_{n}$ has asymptotically the centered normal distribution with covariances $\sigma_{i j}$. Put $x=\left(p_{1}^{1 / 2}, \ldots, p_{k}^{1 / 2}\right)$ and show that $\Sigma x^{\prime}=0$, so that 0 is an eigenvalue of $\Sigma$. Show that $\Sigma y^{\prime}=y^{\prime}$ if $y$ is perpendicular to $x$, so that $\Sigma$ has 1 as an eigenvalue of multiplicity $k-1$. Use Problem 29.7 together with Theorem $29.2\left(h(x)=|x|^{2}\right)$.
29.9. (a) Note that $n^{-1} \sum_{i=1}^{n} Y_{n i}^{2} \Rightarrow 1$ and that $\left(X_{n 1}, \ldots, X_{n t}\right)$ has the same distribution as $\left(Y_{n}, \ldots, Y_{n}\right) /\left(n^{-1} \sum_{i=1}^{n} Y_{n i}^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}$.

## Section 30

30.1. Rescale so that $s_{n}^{2}=1$, and put $L_{n}(\epsilon)=\sum_{k} \int_{\left|X_{n k}\right| \geq \epsilon} X_{n k}^{2} d P$. Choose increasing $n_{u}$ so that $L_{n}\left(u^{-1}\right) \leq u^{-3}$ for $n \geq n_{u}$, and put $M_{n}=u^{-1}$ for $n_{u} \leq n<n_{u+1}$. Then $M_{n} \rightarrow 0$ and- $L_{n}\left(M_{n}\right) \leq M_{n}^{3}$. Put $Y_{n k}=X_{n k} I_{\left[\left|X_{n k}\right| \leq M_{n}\right]^{.}}$. Show that $\sum_{k} E\left[Y_{n k}\right] \rightarrow 0$ and $\sum_{k} E\left[Y_{n k}^{2}\right] \rightarrow 1$, and apply to $\sum_{k} Y_{n k}$ the central limit theorem under (30.5). Show that $\sum_{k} P\left[X_{n k} \neq Y_{n k}\right] \rightarrow 0$.
30.4. Suppose that the moment generating function $M_{n}$ of $\mu_{n}$ converges to the moment generating function $M$ of $\mu$ in some interval about $s$. Let $\nu_{n}$ have density $e^{s x} / M_{n}(s)$ with respect to $\mu_{n}$, and let $\nu$ have density $e^{s x} / M(s)$ with respect to $\mu$. Then the moment generating function of $\nu_{n}$ converges to that of $\nu$ in some interval about 0 , and hence $\nu_{n} \Rightarrow \nu$. Show that $\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} f(x) \mu_{n}(d x) \rightarrow \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} f(x) \mu(d x)$ if $f$ is continuous and has bounded support; see Problem 25.13(b).
30.5. (a) By Hölder's inequality $\left|\sum_{j=1}^{k} t_{j} x_{j}\right|^{r} \leq k^{r-1} \sum_{j=1}^{k}\left|t_{j} x_{j}\right|^{r}$, and so $\sum_{r} \theta^{r}\left|\sum_{j} t_{j} x_{j}\right|^{r} \mu(d x) / r!$ has positive radius of convergence. Now

$$
\int_{R^{k}}\left(\sum_{j=1}^{k} t_{j} x_{j}\right)^{r} \mu(d x)=\sum t_{1}^{r_{1}} \cdots t_{k}^{r_{k}} \alpha\left(r_{1}, \ldots, r_{k}\right),
$$

where the summation extends over $k$-tuples that add to $r$. Project $\mu$ to the line by the mapping $\sum_{j} t_{j} x_{j}$, apply Theorem 30.1, and use the fact that $\mu$ is determined by its values on half-spaces.
30.6. Use the Cramér-Wold idea.
30.8. Suppose that $k=2$ in (30.30). Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
& M\left[\left(\cos \lambda_{1} x\right)^{r_{1}}\left(\cos \lambda_{2} x\right)^{r_{2}}\right] \\
& \quad=M\left[\left(\frac{e^{i \lambda_{1} x}+e^{-i \lambda_{1} x}}{2}\right)^{r_{1}}\left(\frac{e^{i \lambda_{2} x}+e^{-i \lambda_{2} x}}{2}\right)^{r_{2}}\right] \\
& \quad=2^{-r_{1}-r_{2}} \sum_{j_{1}=0}^{r_{1}} \sum_{j_{2}=0}^{r_{2}}\binom{r_{1}}{j_{1}}\binom{r_{2}}{j_{2}} M\left[\exp i\left(\lambda_{1}\left(2 j_{1}-r_{1}\right)+\lambda_{2}\left(2 j_{2}-r_{2}\right)\right) x\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

By (26.33) and the independence of $\lambda_{1}$ and $\lambda_{2}$, the last mean here is 1 if $2 j_{1}-r_{1}=2 j_{2}-r_{2}=0$ and is 0 otherwise. A similar calculation for $k=1$ gives (30 28), and a similar calculation for general $k$ gives (30.30). The actual form of the distribution in (30.29) is unimportant. For (30.31) use the multidimensiona! method of moments (Problem 30.6) and the mapping theorem. For (30.32) use the central limit theorem; by (30.28). $X_{1}$ has mean 0 and variance $\frac{1}{2}$.
30.10. If $n^{1 / 2}<m \leq n$ and the inequality in (30.33) holds, then $\log \log n^{1 / 2}< \log \log n-\epsilon(\log \log n)^{1 / 2}$, which implies $\log \log n<\epsilon^{-2} \log ^{2} 2$. For large $n$ the probability in (30.33) is thus at most $1 / \sqrt{n}$.

## Section 31

31.1. Consider the argument in Example 31.1. Suppose that $F$ has a nonzero derivative at $x$, and let $I_{n}$ be the set of numbers whose base- $r$ expansions agree in the first $n$ places with that of $x$. The analogue of (31.16) is $P[X \in \left.I_{n+1}\right] / P\left[X \in I_{n}\right] \rightarrow r^{-1}$, and the ratio here is one of $p_{0}, \ldots, p_{r}$. If $p_{i} \neq r^{-1}$ for some $i$, use the second Borel-Cantelli lemma to show that the ratio is $p_{i}$ infinitely often except on a set of Lebesgue measure 0 . (This last part of the argument is unnecessary if $r=2$.)

The argument in Example 31.3 needs no essential change. The analogue of (31.17) is

$$
F(x)=p_{0}+\cdots+p_{i-1}+p_{i} F(r x-i), \quad \frac{i}{r} \leq x \leq \frac{i+1}{r}, \quad 0 \leq i<r-1 .
$$

31.3. (b) Take $f_{1}=I_{g^{-1} H_{0}}$ and $f_{2}=F ;\left(f_{1} f_{2}\right)^{-1}\{1\}=H_{0}$ is not a Lebesgue set.
31.9. Suppose that $A$ is bounded, define $\mu$ by $\mu(B)=\lambda(B \cap A)$, and let $F$ be the corresponding distribution function. It suffices to show that $F^{\prime}(x)=1$ for $x$ in $A$, apart from a set of Lebesgue measure 0 . Let $C_{\epsilon}$ be the set of $x$ in $A$ for which $F^{\prime}(x) \leq 1-\epsilon$. From Theorem 31.4(i) deduce that $\lambda\left(C_{\epsilon}\right)=\mu\left(C_{\epsilon}\right) \leq(1- \epsilon) \lambda\left(C_{\epsilon}\right)$ and hence $\lambda\left(C_{\epsilon}\right)=0$. Thus $F^{\prime}(x)>1-\epsilon$ almost everywhere on $A$. Obviously, $F^{\prime}(x) \leq 1$.
31.11. Let $A$ be the set of $x$ in the unit interval for which $F^{\prime}(x)=0$, take $\alpha=0$, and define $A_{n}$ as in the first part of the proof of Theorem 31.4. Choose $n$ so that $\lambda\left(A_{n}\right) \geq 1-\epsilon$. Split $\{1,2, \ldots, n\}$ into the set $M$ of $k$ for which $((k-1) / n, k / n]$ meets $A_{n}$ and the opposite set $N$. Prove successively that $\Sigma_{k \in M}[F(k / n)- F((k-1) / n)] \leq \epsilon, \sum_{k \in N}[F(k / n)-F((k-1) / n)] \geq 1-\epsilon, \sum_{k \in M} 1 / n \geq \lambda\left(A_{n}\right) \geq 1-\epsilon, \sum_{k=1}^{n}|f(k / n)-f((k-1) / n)| \geq 2-2 \epsilon$.
31.15. $\Pi_{n=1}^{\infty}\left(\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2} e^{2 i t / 3^{n}}\right)$.
31.18. For $x$ fixed, let $u_{n}$ and $v_{n}$ be the pair of successive dyadic rationals of order $n$ ( $v_{n}-u_{n}=2^{-n}$ ) for which $u_{n}<x \leq v_{n}$. Show that

$$
\frac{f\left(v_{n}\right)-f\left(u_{n}\right)}{v_{n}-u_{n}}=\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} \frac{a_{k}\left(v_{n}\right)-a_{k}\left(u_{n}\right)}{v_{n}-u_{n}}=\sum_{k=0}^{n-1} a_{k}^{-}(x),
$$

where $a_{k}^{-}$is the left-hand derivative. Since $a_{k}^{-}(x)= \pm 1$ for all $x$ and $k$, the difference ratio cannot have a finite limit
31.22. Let $A$ be the $x$-set where (31.35) fails if $f$ is replaced by $f \varphi$; then $A$ has Lebesgue measure 0 . Let $G$ be the union of all open sets of $\mu$-measure 0 ; represent $G$ as a countable disjoint union of open intervals, and let $B$ be $G$ together with any endpoints of zero $\mu$-measure of these intervals. Let $D$ be the set of discontinuity points of $F$. If $F(x) \notin A, x \notin B$, and $x \notin D$, then $F(x-h) <F(x)<F(x+h), F(x \pm h) \rightarrow F(x)$, and

$$
\frac{1}{F(x+h)-F(x-h)} \int_{F(x-h)}^{F(x+h)} f(\varphi(t)) d t \rightarrow f(\varphi(F(x))) .
$$

Now $x-\epsilon<\varphi(F(x)) \leq x$ follows from $F(x-\epsilon)<F(x)$, and hence $\varphi(F(x))= x$. If $\lambda$ is Lebesgue measure restricted to ( 0,1 ), then $\mu=\lambda \varphi^{-1}$, and (31.36) follows by change of variable. But (36.36) is easy if $x \in D$, and hence it holds outside $B \cup\left(D^{c} \cap F^{-1} A\right)$. But $\mu(B)=0$ by construction and $\mu\left(D^{c} \cap F^{-1} A\right)=0$ by Problem 14.4.

## Section 32

32.7. Define $\mu_{n}$ and $\nu_{n}$ as in (32.7), and write $\nu_{n}=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{(n)}+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{(n)}$, where $\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{(n)}$ is absolutely continuous with respect to $\mu_{n}$ and $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{(n)}$ is singular with respect to $\mu_{n}$. Take $\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}=\sum_{n} \nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{(n)}$ and $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}=\sum_{n} \nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{(n)}$.

Suppose that $\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}(E)+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}(\mathrm{E})=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{\prime}(E)+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\prime}(E)$ for all $E$ in $\mathscr{F}$. Choose an $S$ in $\mathscr{F}$ that supports $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}$ and $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\prime}$ and satisfies $\mu(S)=0$. Then $\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}(E)= \nu_{\mathrm{ac}}\left(E \cap S^{c}\right)=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}\left(E \cap S^{c}\right)+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}\left(E \cap S^{c}\right)=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{\prime}\left(E \cap S^{c}\right)+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\prime}\left(E \cap S^{c}\right)=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{\prime}(E \cap \left.S^{c}\right)=\nu_{\mathrm{ac}}^{\prime}(E)$. A similar argument shows that $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}(E)=\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\prime}(E)$.
32.8. (a) Show that $\mathscr{B}$ is closed under the formation of countable unions, choose $\mathscr{B}$-sets $B_{n}$ such that $\mu\left(B_{n}\right) \rightarrow \sup _{\mathscr{B}} \mu(B)(<\infty)$, and take $B_{0}=\bigcup_{n} B_{n}$.
(b) The same argument.
(c) Suppose $\mu\left(D_{0}\right)>0$. The maximality of $B_{0}$ implies that $B_{0} \cup D_{0}$ contains an $E$ such that $\mu(E)>0$ and $\nu(E)<\infty$. Since $B_{0} \cap E \subset B_{0} \in \mathscr{B}, \mu\left(B_{0} \cap E\right)=0 \left(\nu(E)<\infty\right.$ rules out $\left.\nu\left(B_{0} \cap E\right)=\infty\right)$. Therefore, $\mu\left(D_{0} \cap E\right)>0$ and $\nu\left(D_{0} \cap E\right)<\infty$, which contradicts the maximality of $C_{0}$.
(d) Take the density to be $\infty$ on $\Omega_{0}^{c}$.
32.9. Define $f$ and $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}$ as in (32.8), and let $f^{\circ}$ and $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\circ}$ be the corresponding function and measure for $\mathscr{F}^{\circ}: \nu(E)=\int_{E} f^{\circ} d \mu+\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\circ}(E)$ for $E \in \mathscr{F}^{\circ}$, and there is an $\mathscr{F}^{\circ}$-set $S^{\circ}$ such that $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\circ}\left(\Omega-S^{\circ}\right)=0$ and $\mu\left(S^{\circ}\right)=0$. If $E \in \mathscr{F}^{\circ}$, it follows that $\int_{E} f^{\circ} d \mu=\int_{E-S^{\circ}} f^{\circ} d \mu=\int_{E-S^{\circ}} f^{\circ} d \mu^{\circ}=\nu^{\circ}\left(E-S^{\circ}\right)=\nu\left(E-S^{\circ}\right) \geq \int_{E-S^{\circ}} f d \mu=\int_{E} f d \mu$.

It is instructive to consider the extreme case $\mathscr{F}^{\circ}=(0, \Omega)$, in which $\nu^{\circ}$ is absolutely continuous with respect to $\mu^{\circ}$ (provided $\mu(\Omega)>0$ ) and hence $\nu_{\mathrm{s}}^{\circ}$ vanishes.

## Section 33

33.2. (a) To prove independence, check the covariance. Now use Example 33.7.
(b) Use the fact that $R$ and $\Theta$ are independent (Example 20.2).
(c) As the single event $[X=Y]=[X-Y=0]=[\Theta=\pi / 4] \cup[\Theta=5 \pi / 4]$ has probability 0 , the conditional probabilities have no meaning, and strictly speaking there is nothing to resolve But whether it is natural to regard the degrees of freedom as one or as two depends on whether the $45^{\circ}$ line through the origin is regarded as an element of the decomposition of the plane into $45^{\circ}$ lines or whether it is regarded as the union of two elements of the decomposition of the plane into rays from the origin.

Borel's paradox can be explained the same way: The equator is an element of the decomposition of the sphere into lines of constant latitude; the Greenwich meridian is an element of the decomposition of the sphere into great circles with common poles. The decomposition matters, which is to say the $\sigma$-field matters.
33.3. (a) If the guard says, " 1 is to be executed," then the conditional probability that 3 is also to be executed is $1 /(1+p)$. The "paradox" comes from assuming that $p$ must be 1 , in which case the conditional probability is indeed $\frac{1}{2}$. But if $p \neq \frac{1}{2}$, then the guard does give prisoner 3 some information.
(b) Here "one" and "other" are undefined, and the problem ignores the possibility that you have been introduced to a girl. Let the sample space be

$$
\begin{array}{llll}
b b o \frac{\alpha}{4}, & \text { bgo } \frac{\beta}{4}, & \text { gbo } \frac{\gamma}{4}, & \text { ggo } \frac{\delta}{4}, \\
b b y \frac{1-\alpha}{4}, & \text { bgy } \frac{1-\beta}{4}, & \text { gby } \frac{1-\gamma}{4}, & \text { ggy } \frac{1-\delta}{4} .
\end{array}
$$

For example, $b g o$ is the event (probability $\beta / 4$ ) that the older child is a boy, the younger is a girl, and the child you have been introduced to is the older; and $g g y$ is the event (probability $(1-\delta) / 4)$ that both children are girls and the one you have been introduced to is the younger. Note that the four sexdistributions do have probability $\frac{1}{4}$. If the child you have been introduced to is a boy, then the conditional probability that the other child is also a boy is $p=1 /(2+\beta-\gamma)$. If $\beta=1$ and $\gamma=0$ (the parents present a son if they have one), then $p=\frac{1}{3}$. If $\beta=\gamma$ (the parents are indifferent), then $p=\frac{1}{2}$. Any $p$ between $\frac{1}{9}$ and 1 is possible.

This problem shows again that one must keep in mind the entire experiment the sub- $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{G}$ represents, not just one of the possible outcomes of the experiment.
33.6. There is no problem, unless the notation gives rise to the illusion that $p(A \mid x)$ is $P(A \cap[X=x]) / P[X=x]$.
33.15. If $N$ is a standard normal variable, then

$$
\frac{1}{\sqrt{n}} p_{y}\left(y+\frac{x}{\sqrt{n}}\right)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} e^{-x^{2} / 2} f\left(y+\frac{x}{\sqrt{n}}\right) / E\left[f\left(y+\frac{N}{\sqrt{n}}\right)\right] .
$$

## Section 34

34.3. If $(X, Y)$ takes the values $(0,0),(1,-1)$, and $(1,1)$ with probability $\frac{1}{3}$ each, then $X$ and $Y$ are dependent but $E[Y \| X]=E[Y]=0$.

If $(X, Y)$ takes the values $(-1,1),(0,-2)$, and $(1,1)$ with probability $\frac{1}{3}$ each, then $E[X]=E[Y]=E[X Y]=0$ and so $E[X Y]=E[X] E[Y]$, but $E[Y \| X]= Y \neq 0=E[Y]$. Of course, this is another example of dependent but uncorrelated random variables.
34.4. First show that $\int f d P_{0}=\int_{B} f d P / P(B)$ and that $P[B \| \mathscr{G}]>0$ on a set of $P_{0^{-}}$ measure 1. Let $G$ be the general set in $\mathscr{G}$.
(a) Since

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{G} P_{0}[A \| \mathscr{A}] P[B \| \mathscr{A}] d P & =\int_{G} P_{0}[A \| \mathscr{A}] I_{B} d P=\int_{B} I_{G} P_{0}[A \| \mathscr{G}] d P \\
& =P(B) \int_{\Omega} I_{G} P_{0}[A \| \mathscr{A}] d P_{0}=P(B) P_{0}(A \cap G) \\
& =\int_{G} P[A \cap B \| \mathscr{G}] d P
\end{aligned}
$$

it follows that

$$
P_{0}[A \| \mathscr{G}] P[B \| \mathscr{G}]=P[A \cap B \| \mathscr{G}]
$$

holds on a set of $P$-measure 1.
(b) If $P_{i}(A)=P\left(A \mid B_{i}\right)$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{G \cap B_{i}} P_{i}[A \| \mathscr{G}] d P & =P\left(B_{i}\right) \int_{\Omega} I_{G} P_{i}[A \| \mathscr{A}] d P_{i}=P\left(B_{i}\right) P_{i}(A \cap G) \\
& =\int_{G \cap B_{i}} P[A \| \mathscr{G} \vee \mathscr{H}] d P .
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, $\int_{C} I_{B} P_{i}[A \| \mathscr{G}] d P=\int_{C} I_{B} P[A \| \mathscr{G} \vee \mathscr{H}] d P$ if $C=G \cap B_{i}$, and of course this holds for $C=G \cap B_{j}$ if $j \neq i$. But $C$ 's of this form constitute a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{G} \vee \mathscr{H}$, and hence $I_{B_{i}} P_{i}[A \| \mathscr{G}]=I_{B_{i}} P[A \| \mathscr{G} \vee \mathscr{H}]$ on a set of $P$-measure 1 . Now use the result in part (a).
34.9. All such results can be proved by imitating the proofs for the unconditional case or else by using Theorem 34.5 (for part (c), as generalized in Problem 34.7). For part (a), it must be shown that it is possible to take the integral measurable $\mathscr{E}$.
34.10. (a) If $Y=X-E\left[X \| \mathscr{G}_{1}\right]$, then $X-E\left[X \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]=Y-E\left[Y \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]$, and $E[(Y- \left.\left.E\left[Y \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]\right)^{2} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]=E\left[Y^{2} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]-E^{2}\left[Y \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right] \leq E\left[Y^{2} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]$. Take expected values.
34.11. First prove that

$$
P\left[A_{1} \cap A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]=E\left[I_{A_{1}} P\left[A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{12}\right] \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right] .
$$

From this and (i) deduce (ii). From

$$
E\left[I_{A_{1}} P\left[A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right] \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]=P\left[A_{1} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right] P\left[A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right]
$$

(ii), and the preceding equation deduce

$$
\int_{A_{1} \cap A_{2}} P\left[A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{2}\right] d P=\int_{A_{1} \cap A_{2}} P\left[A_{3} \| \mathscr{G}_{12}\right] d P
$$

The sets $A_{1} \cap A_{2}$ form a $\pi$-system generating $\mathscr{A}_{12}$.
34.16. (a) Obviously (34.18) implies (34.17). If (34.17) holds, then clearly (34.18) holds for $X$ simple. For the general $X$, choose simple $X_{k}$ such that $\lim _{k} X_{k}=X$ and $\left|X_{k}\right| \leq|X|$. Note that

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \left|\int_{A_{n}} X d P-\alpha \int X d P\right| \\
& \quad \leq\left|\int_{A_{n}} X_{k} d P-\alpha \int X_{k} d P\right|+(1+|\alpha|) E\left[\left|X-X_{k}\right|\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

let $n \rightarrow \infty$ and then let $k \rightarrow \infty$.
(b) If $\Omega \in \mathscr{P}$, then the class of $E$ satisfying (34.17) is a $\lambda$-system, and so by the $\pi-\lambda$ theorem and part (a), (34.18) holds if $X$ is measurable $\sigma(\mathscr{P})$. Since $A_{n} \in \sigma(\mathscr{P})$, it follows that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{A_{n}} X d P & =\int_{A_{n}} E[X \| \sigma(\mathscr{P})] d P \rightarrow \alpha \int E[X \mid i \sigma(\mathscr{P})] d P \\
& =\alpha \int X d P
\end{aligned}
$$

(c) Replace $X$ by $X d P_{0} / d P$ in (34.18).

(b) Chebyshev's inequality.

## Section 35

35.4. (b) Let $S_{n}$ be the number of $k$ such that $1 \leq k \leq n$ and $Y_{k}=\frac{3}{2}$. Then $X_{n}=3^{S_{n}} / 2^{n}$. Take logarithms and use the strong law of large numbers.
35.9. Let $K$ bound $\left|X_{1}\right|$ and the $\left|X_{n}-X_{n-1}\right|$. Bound $\left|X_{\tau}\right|$ by $K \tau$. Write $f_{\tau \leq k} X_{\tau} d P= \sum_{i=1}^{k} f_{\tau=i} X_{i} d P=\sum_{i=1}^{k}\left(f_{\tau \geq i} X_{t} d P-f_{\tau \geq i+1} X_{i} d P\right)$. Transform the last integral by the martingale property and reduce the expression to $E\left[X_{1}\right]-\int_{\tau>k} X_{k+1} d P$. Now

$$
\left|\int_{\tau>k} X_{k+1} d P\right| \leq K(k+1) P[\tau>k] \leq K(k+1) k^{-1} \int_{\tau>k} \tau d P \rightarrow 0
$$

35.13. (a) By the result in Problem 32.9, $X_{1}, X_{2}, \ldots$ is a supermartingale. Since $E\left[\left|X_{n}\right|\right]=E\left[X_{n}\right] \leq \nu(\Omega)$, Theorem 35.5 applies.
(b) If $A \in \mathscr{F}_{n}$, then $\int_{A}\left(Y_{n}+Z_{n}\right) d P+\sigma_{n}^{\prime}(A)=\int_{A} X_{\infty} d P+\sigma_{\infty}(A)=\nu(A)= f_{A} X_{n} d P+\sigma_{n}(A)$. Since the Lebesgue decomposition is unique (Problem 32.7), $Y_{n}+Z_{n}=X_{n}$ with probability 1 . Since $X_{n}$ and $Y_{n}$ converge, so does $Z_{n}$. If $A \subseteq \mathscr{F}_{k}$ and $n \geq k$, then $\int_{A} Z_{n} d P \leq \sigma_{\infty}(A)$, and by Fatou's lemma, the limit $Z$ satisfies $\int_{A} Z d P \leq \sigma_{\infty}(A)$. This holds for $A$ in $\bigcup_{k} \mathscr{F}_{k}$ and hence (monotone class theorem) for $A$ in $\mathscr{F}_{\infty}$. Choose $A$ so that $P(A)=1$ and $\sigma_{\infty}(A)=0$ : $E[Z]=\int_{A} Z d P \leq \sigma_{\infty}(A)=0$.

It can happen that $\sigma_{n}(\Omega)=0$ and $\sigma_{\infty}(\Omega)=\nu(\Omega)>0$, in which case $\sigma_{n}$ does not converge to $c_{\infty}$ and the $X_{n}$ cannot be integrated to the limit.
35.17. For a very general result, see J. L. Doob: Application of the theory of martingales, Le Calcui des Probabilités et ses Applications (Colloques Internationaux du Centre de la Recherche Scientifique, Paris, 1949).

## Section 36

36.5. (b) Show by part (a) and Problem 34.18 that $f_{n}$ is the conditional expected value of $f$ with respect to the $\sigma$-field $\mathscr{T}_{n+1}$ generated by the coordinates $x_{n+1}, x_{n+2}, \ldots$. By Theorem 35.9, (36.30) will follow if each set in $\bigcap_{n} \mathscr{T}_{n}$ has $\pi$-measure either 0 or 1 , and here the zero-one law applies.
(c) Show that $g_{n}$ is the conditional expected value of $f$ with respect to the $\sigma$-field generated by the coordinates $x_{1}, \ldots, x_{n}$, and apply Theorem 35.6.
36.7. Let $\mathscr{L}$ be the countable set of simple functions $\sum_{i} \alpha_{i} I_{A_{i}}$ for $\alpha_{i}$ rational and $\left\{A_{i}\right\}$ a finite decomposition of the unit interval into subintervals with rational endpoints. Suppose that the $X_{t}$ exist, and choose (Theorem 17.1) $Y_{t}$ in $\mathscr{L}$ so that $E\left[\left|X_{t}-Y_{t}\right|\right]<\frac{1}{4}$. From $E\left[\left|X_{s}-X_{t}\right|\right]=\frac{1}{2}$, conclude that $E\left[\left|Y_{s}-Y_{t}\right|\right]>0$ for $s \neq t$. But there are only countably many of the $Y_{r}$. It does no good to replace Lebesgue measure by some other measure on the unit interval.

## Section 37

37.1. If $t_{1}, \ldots, t_{k}$ are in increasing order and $t_{0}=0$, then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sum_{i, j} K\left(t_{i}, t_{j}\right) x_{i} x_{j} & =\sum_{i, j} x_{i} x_{j} \sum_{l=1}^{\min \{i, j\}}\left(t_{i}-t_{l-1}\right) \\
& =\sum_{l}\left(t_{l}-t_{l-1}\right)\left(\sum_{i \geq l} x_{i}\right)^{2} \geq 0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

37.4 (a) Use Problem 36.6(b).
(b) Let $\left[W_{t}: t \geq 0\right]$ be a Brownian motion on $\left(\Omega, \mathscr{F} . P_{0}\right)$, where $W(\cdot, \omega) \in C$ for every $\omega$. Define $\xi$ : $\Omega \rightarrow R^{T}$ by $Z_{1}(\xi(\omega))=W_{1}(\omega)$. Show that $\xi$ is measurable $\mathscr{F} / \mathscr{R}^{T}$ and $P=P_{0} \xi^{-1}$. If $C \subset A \in \mathscr{R}^{T}$, then $P(A)=P_{0}\left(\xi^{-1} A\right)= P_{0}(\Omega)=1$.
37.5. Consider $W(1)=\sum_{k=1}^{n}(W(k / n)-W((k-1) / n))$ for notational convenience. Since

$$
n \int_{|W(1 / n)| \geq \epsilon} W^{2}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right) d P=\int_{|W(1)| \geq \epsilon \sqrt{n}} W^{2}(1) d P \rightarrow 0
$$

the Lindeberg theorem applies.
37.14. By symmetry,

$$
\rho(s, t)=2 P\left[W_{s}>0, \inf _{s \leq u \leq r}\left(W_{u}-W_{s}\right) \leq-W_{s}\right] ;
$$

$W_{s}$ and the infimum here are independent because of the Markov property, and so by (20.30) (and symmetry again)

$$
\begin{aligned}
\rho(s, t) & =2 \int_{0}^{\infty} P\left[\tau_{x} \leq t-s\right] \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi s}} e^{-x^{2} / 2 s} d x \\
& =2 \int_{0}^{\infty} \int_{0}^{1-s} \frac{x}{\sqrt{2 \pi}} \frac{1}{u^{3 / 2}} e^{-x^{2} / 2 u} \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi s}} e^{-x^{2} / 2 s} d u d x
\end{aligned}
$$

Reverse the integral, use $\int_{0}^{\infty} x e^{-x^{2} r / 2} d x=1 / r$, and put $v=(s /(s+u))^{1 / 2}$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
\rho(s, t) & =\frac{1}{\pi} \int_{0}^{1-s} \frac{1}{u+s} \frac{s^{1 / 2}}{u^{1 / 2}} d u \\
& =\frac{2}{\pi} \int_{\sqrt{s / t}}^{1} \frac{d v}{\sqrt{1-v^{2}}}
\end{aligned}
$$

## PROBABILITY AND MEASURE

## Third Edition

Now in its new third edition, Probability and Measure offers advanced students, scientists, and engineers an integrated introduction to measure theory and probability. Retaining the unique approach of the previous editions, this text interweaves material on probability and measure, so that probability problems generate an interest in measure theory and measure theory is then developed and applied to probability. Probability and Measure provides thorough coverage of probability, measure, integration, random variables and expected values, convergence of distributions, derivatives and conditional probability, and stochastic processes. The Third Edition features an improved treatment of Brownian motion and the replacement of queuing theory with ergodic theory.

Like the previous editions, this new edition will be well received by students of mathematics, statistics, economics, and a wide variety of disciplines that require a solid understanding of probability theory.

PATRICK BILLINGSLEY is Professor of Statistics and Mathematics at the University of Chicago. He is the coauthor (with Watson et al.) of Statistics for Management and Economics; (with D. L. Huntsberger) of Elements of Statistical Inference; and the author of Convergence of Probability Measures (Wiley-Interscience), among other works. Dr. Billingsley has also edited the Annals of Probability for the Institute of Mathematical Statistics. He received his PhD in mathematics from Princeton University.
